THE RED PAGE.
When ’.Onier smote ’is bloomin’ lyre,
He’d 'eard men sing by ’land an’ sea ;
An’ what he thought ’e might require,
’H went an’ took—the same as me !
The market-girls an’ fishermen,
The shepherds an’ the sailors, too,
They ’eard old songs turn up again,
But hep’ it quiet—same as you !
They knew ’e stole ; 'e knew they knowed,
They did n’t tell, nor make a fuss,
But winked at ’Oilier down the road,
And 'e winked back—the same as us l
Rudyard Ruling.
THERE you have, in Kipling’s own
words, Kipling’s own idea of his
writer’s privilege. He has realised
that no man works for himself or by
himself. We all build our palaces or hovels
with the materials which other men have
collected ; and the most we can hope to do
is to bring a little, a very little, fresh
material which our own hands have felled
from the unexplored forest or dug from the
virgin mine. Originality comes chiefly in
the use of the tools and materials, in the
shaping and plan of the edifice.
But there are three ways of dealing with
our debt to predecessors. We can amuiow-
ledge the debt; we can ignore it; or we can
repudiate it outright. The first way is
honest;- the last way is knavish ; the middle
way is the way of compromise with con-
science so as to get all possible credit at
the least possible cost of principle.
It is the middle way that Kipling has
chosen. His tine talent, his splendid indus-
try, would not by themselves have raised
the pile of excellent work which stands to
his profit and reputation. Kipling has
realised that to be exploiter pays better
than to be inventor ; arid he has
become a capitalist of other men’s
brains, developing facts and ideas which
would be for the most part useless without
his intelligent control. But the labourers
get no share of the profits of Kipling’s in-
dustry : perhaps they do not deserve a
share, seeing that they contribute so little to
make the product marketable ; and the
whole credit of the enterprise goes to
Kipling. For answer to objectors, there is
printed with Kipling’s work the frank
•declaration, the cynical confession, at the
head of this column.
* * * *
Kipling’s way is open to thousands of men
who have so far mastered the technic of
tneir art that they can take a vital idea, pass
it through their own minds, and so
transform it with their own language
that, it appears positively new, only
the indispensable kernel being bor-
rowed. If a man of Daley’s skill with
rhymes chose to go to the Elizabethan song-
books and poetry-books, he would find hun-
dreds of bright fancies, quaint conceptions, ;
which needed only re-writing with the art at
his disposal in order to be modernised and
re-vitalised. There are scores of paragraphs
in The Buhet-in every week, contributed by
obscure seers, which only require Dorring-
;ton’s labour to become brilliant short stories.
But it is a good thing for literature that
there are few writers so entirely unscrupu-
lous as Kipling. The unwritten literary
law, based upon majority-ideas of honesty,
says that you shall not deliberately take
another man’s work as the essential founda-
tion of your own without giving that other
credit for his transferred value, in his indi-
vidual degree.
Doubtless Kipling has exceptional talent
for this business of literary assimilation ;
and his own roots also blossom in his work.
He can fairly claim that, but for his seeing
eye, his ready hand, many of the flowers
which he has gathered would have wasted
their sweetness, or never would have
bloomed at all. His quick recognition of
“points” of phrase, or rhythm, or colour,
or plot, is perhaps his most excellent faculty.
When Kipling wrote “ Bill ’Awkins ”
“ ’As anybody seen Bill ’Awkins ? ”
“Now, ’ow in the devil would I know?”
“’E’s taken my girl out walkin’,
An’ I’ve got to tell ’im so—
Gawd—bless —’im !
I’ve got to tell ’im so ”
New Orleans people said at once it was
modelled on a familiar negro ditty :
“ Hab any o’ yo’ seen my Lulu ? ”
“ How in de debbil would you know her ?”
“ T’d know her by her apurn-strings
’N’ her shoe-strings a-flangin’ on de flo’,
Gol —darn—her !
’N’ her shoe-strings a-hangin’ on de flo’.”
But the New Orleans ditty had only a local
audience, Kipling’s an international. And
of the thousands who admired the verna-
cular force of “Bill ’Awkins ” only a com-
parative two or three knew that its vigour
was born in some humble negro brain on the
banks of Mississippi.
» * * *
Kipling prend son bien oil il le trouve; and
as he has read more than most men, and
travelled much more than most, no single
brain can track him to all his sources. But
when he wrote “Recessional,” the cry was
instant that he had borrowed the germ-idea
from Cardinal Newman’s “England.”
Comparison will show that he borrowed a
good deal more than the germ. Here is
Newman’s verse :
England.
Tyre of the West, and glorying in the name
More than in Faith’s pure fame !
0 trust not crafty fort nor rock renown’d
Earn’d upon hostile ground ;
Wielding Trade’s master-keys, at thy proud will
To lock or loose its waters, England ! trust not
still.
Dread thine own power ! Since haughty Babel’s
prime
High towers have been man’s crime.
Since her hoar age, when the huge moat lay bare,
Strongholds have been men’s snare.
Thy nest is in the crags; ah, refuge frail!
Mad counsel in its hour, or traitors, will prevail.
He who scann’d Sodom for its righteous men
Still spares thee for tliy ten ;
But, should vain tongues the Bride of Heaven
defy,
He will not pass thee by ;
For, as earth’s Kings welcome their spotless guest,
So gives he them by turn, to suffer or be blest.
Let any skilled verse-writer throw New-
man’s ideas into a metre of Quarles’
“ Divine Emblems ” —a book still read and
treasured in many English homes, and con-
taining verses like these—
Lord God of Gods, before whose throne
Stand storms and fire, O what shall we
Return to Heaven that is our own,
When all the world belongs to thee ?
We have no offering to impart
But praises and a wounded heart.
Great God, whose kingdom hath no end,
Into whose secrets none can dive,
Whose mercy none can apprehend,
Whose justice none can feel, and live, —
What my dull heart cannot aspire
To know, Lord, teach me to admire——
add a few phrases of his own,* a few
familiar tags ; and if he does not get the fol-
lowing, with the topic of the British naval
review for a text, he will get something very
like it
Recessional.
God of our fathers, known of old,
Lord of our far-flung battle-line—
Beneath whose awful hand we hold
Dominion over palm and pine—
Lord God of Hosts, be with us yet,
Lest we forget—lest we forget !
The tumult and the shouting dies,
The captains and the kings depart;
Still stands thine ancient sacrifice.
An humble and a contrite heart.
Lord God of Hosts, be with us yet,
Lest we forget—lest we forget l
Far-called our navies melt away—
On dune and headland sinks the fire j,
Lo, all our pomp of yesterday
Is one with Nineveh and Tyre !
Judge of the Nations, spare us yet,
Lest we forget—lest we forget! .. .
And so on. Kipling took his emotion and
attitude from Newman, his metre from
Quarles, and the rest from his memory and
the daily newspapers. Is it any wonder
that he flung “Recessional ” into the waste-
basket ?—to be fished out by Mrs. Kipling,
blissfully ignorant how shoddy “ poetry ” is
made. That is indeed a befitting connec-
tion which links Kipling, as Imperialistic
poet, with the shoddy union of races called
“ Imperialism, ” —the poetical bagman of the
shoddy-exporters of Manchester! Give
Kipling all credit for being the only mhn to
see the “ Recessional” chance, and the only
man to take it, the only man to fake it : he
is an artisan who knows his business. But,
on the weakness of “Recessional,” to call
Kipling a supreme artist, and to hail his
product as belonging to the highest class of
original art —that is indeed a confusion of
categories into which only the ignorant and
uncritical can fall.
* * * *
Nowadays everything which Kipling pub-
lishes is to be read with suspicion to the
sound of “ ’Omer’s bloomin’ lyre.” A couple
of years ago a New York society asked Kip-
ling for a piece of verse to put on a social
programme. There was sent to them this
previously-published piece “with a cordial
note from the poet’s wife ” —who seems
innocently to act the part of a “ fence ” for-
getting rid of doubtful property :
Love and Death once ceased .their strife
At the Tavern of Man’s Life,
Called for wine and threw, alas !
Each his quiver on the grass.
When the bout was o’er they found
Mingled arrows strewed the ground :
Hastily they gathered then
Each the loves and lives of men.
Ah ! the fateful dawn deceived —
Mingled arrows each one sheaved !
Death’s dread armoury was stored
With the shafts he most abhorred ;
Love’s light quiver groaned beneath
Venom-headed darts of death,
Save ye, maidens ! This is why
Old men love while young men die.
Unluckily, while the N.Y. society was still
being congratulated on its autograph copy
and its good fortune, some nuisance of a
bookworm turned up these lines in the work
of Richard Flecknoe, a third-rate English
poet and dramatist in the reign of Charles 11.
Love and Death o’ th ; way once meeting,
Having passed a friendly greeting,
Sleep their weary eye-lids closing,
Lay them down, themselves reposing ;
When this fortune did befall ’em,
Which after did so much appal ’em ;
Love, whom clivers cares molested,
Could not sleep, but while Death rested,
All in haste away he posts him :
But his haste full dearly costs him ;
For it chanced, that going to sleeping,
Both had given their darts in keeping
Unto Night ; who (Error’s mother)
Blindly knowing not th’ one from th’ other,
Gave Love Death’s, and ne’er perceived it,
Whilst as blindly Love received it;
Since which time, their darts confounding,
Love now kills instead of wounding ;
Deatii, our hearts with sweetness filling,
Gently wounds, instead of killing.
Some journals appealed to Kipling for ail ex-
planation why he had passed off these verses
without a hint that they were Elecknoe’s ;
but the oracle, having.spoken once (see head
of this page) wis mute. Oracles are usually
sparing of their words;; and Kipling pre--
sumably contented himself with winking
cheerfully, same as ’Oiner.
+
Kipling's “omniscience” astounds com-
monplace readers no less than his versa-
tility : he is so wonderfully “crammed.”
As the Pall Mail Gazette sang :
The secrets of the sea are his; the mysteries of
Tnd,
He knows minutely every way in which mankind
has sinned ;
He has by heart the lightships ’twixt the Good-
wins and the Cape,
The language of the elephant, the ethics of the
ape;
He knows the slang of Silver Street, the horrors
of Lahore,
And how the man-seal breasts the waves that
buffet Labrador, ...
He knows each fine gradation ’twixt the General
and the sub.,
The terms employed by Atkins when they fling
him from a pub. ;
He knows an Ekka pony’s points, the leper’s
drear albode,
The seamy side of Simla, the flaring Mile End
road;
He knows the Devil’s tone to souls too pitiful to
damn,
He knows the taste of every regimental mess in
“ cham ” ;
He knows enough to annotate the Bible verse by
verse,
And how to draw the shekels from the British
public’s verse
Or seems to know. For if is a remarkable
thing that Kipling’s local reference hardly
ever passes muster with the local resident ;
his technical jargon hardly ever rings
truly to the technical expert. Kipling’s
description of every place most impresses
the people who do not live there ; his use of
trade terms appears wonderful to the men
in every other trade. He sketches Australia
in a few liues, and Ernest Favenc comments
on his inaccuracy ; he pictures an artist at
work, and D. H. Souter objects to his
phrasing, and re-writes his stanzas. The
inexplicable jumble of “The Young Queen,”
with its misapplied “ Kaikouras,” is a recent
case in point. How could it be otherwise?
how expect the swift Kipling method to be
also sure ? For, as Bedford wrote of
Kipling—
I put on my specs in Port Phillip, and I spat on
Sydney Quay,
For I’m the bloke that hits it in once—no serving
my time for me !
And I cleaned my nails over Brisbane, and I
sneezed for an hour at Perth.
And then I came home on the English mail and I
wrote of the.. Big Wide-Earthi
Kipling knows a great deal, especially about
India and about Painting—as shown in the
Indian stories andin “The Light that Failed”;
but his knowledge even of Painting and of
India is superficial, and in other things he
does not go even surface-deep. The allega-
tion that the Indian work was revised by
Kipling’s father, J. Lockwood Kipling, and
that “The Light that Failed” was over-
seen by Poynter, R.A., who married Kip-
ling’s aunt, may or may not be correct ; but
undoubtedly Kipling’s other work, for the
most part, is in glaring need of such skilled
revision by a man who knows the subject
through and through.
* * * *
For Australian cavils are paralleled every-
where. When Kipling published his story
of “An Unqualified Pilot” on the
Hughli River, the Calcutta Englishman
smote him hip and thigh to show that
even India alone was too vast for Kip-
ling to know, and pilloried some score of con-
spicuous errors in fact and inference. When
he published “ Captains Courageous” every-
body was amazed at the book’s verisimilitude
to the life of the cod-fishers—every body but
the cod-fishers. One of their mouthpieces
pointed out that “ to anyone who is used to
sniff salt water the production smells of the
lamp; and he has ruined the talk.” The
composition of his crew was criticised ; and,
as for the kel lick in the hands of a boy out
on the Banks—“l don’t know what one
could do with it out there,” said a cod-
fisher ; “I suppose Kipling saw it on shore
at Gloucester.”
Similarly, this is how the American rail-
road story—“ ‘OO7 ”—strikes an American
railroad-man—
Mr. Kipling is a keen observer, and writes
pretty good American for an outsider ; but if he
had spent a night in a roundhouse with his ears
open he would never have used “loco” for loco-
motive, or have omitted the familiar “engine”
altogether ; he would not have said “ bogie ” when
he meant “truck” ; he would not have allowed a
parlor-car to be hitched to a suburban commuter’s
train “ ahead of the caboose ” ; he would not have
made his engines speak of themselves as “ Ameri-
cans” (in the sense of pattern), or painted his
hero pea-green with a red “buffer-bar.”
Further, no American writer would use as a
simile for brilliancy “a fireman’s helmet, in a
street parade,” as few of his countrymen have
ever seen a fireman in a metallic lead-covering
such as is worn in London.
I suppose it’s all rig-lit to strengthen a situation
by omitting the guard rail from an eighty-foot
bridge—it gives, a pleasant,, breezy, western,
get-there-or-bust, nigger-on-the-safety-valve move-
ment ; and maybe it’s good fiction to bring about
the catastrophe with a hundred-pound piglet who
“rolled right under the pilot” and thereby
caused the “ bogies” to lift; but on plain, every-
day railroads there is a guard-rail at every open
culvert, and even the illustrations to Mr, Kipling’s
story admit Cow-catchers.
I’ve learned a good deal about India from
Kudyard ; but when I read h s Yankee stories I
wonder if, perhaps, I haven’t learned some things
that aren’t so.
* * * *
That is what most readers fail to wonder.
The engineer who finds fault with the
machinery in “ McAndrews’ Hymn ” sees as
little wrong with “ Stalky and Co.” as Eng-
lish public-school masters may see with
“ McAndrews’ Hymn.” We can all under-
stand a caricature of our own profession ;
but the caricature of another man’s profes-
sion, when vigorously made, is apt to be
taken for truth. Thus “A Farmer’s Son,”
writing to Literature (31/12/’9B), did not
notice the school characters of “ Stalky and
Co.” : he complained that Kipling had mis-
used the term “ milk-fever that manure
is not manipulated with a two-pronged,
long-handled “ pitchfork,” but with a three-
or four-pronged, short-handled, dung-fork ;
and that farm-labourers, used to cattle,
would not run from them in a panic as repre-
sented by Kipling.
Again, the Indian officer who finds faults
with minute details of Kipling’s soldier
stories may be supposed to take the nautical
Kipling for gospel—not knowing that in
the “ Ballad of Paul Jones ” Kipling intro-
duces a ship of a rating till then unknown
to the navies of the world—a “seventy-
three.” Or that
in the “ Clampflerdown ” ballad Kipling makes
a battleship “open fire at seven miles” on a light
cruiser, armed with the most awful little Hotch-
kiss gun, with which the cruiser walloped the
battleship ; and then the battleship’s crew, as she
was going down, stopped aboard the cruiser,
which was grinding against the battleship’s side,
and the cruiser’s company all lay down and died
of astonishment; and then, without being in the
least affected by the. big battleship’s suction, the
cruiser sheered off, defying all the laws of physics
as she had defied those of the universe, to say
nothing of the naval regulations.
Por that is criticism by a naval officer who
possibly believes- that Midvaney, Learoyd,
aud Ortheris are correct to the last atom of
pipeclay.. • - _
' ' --- —♦ —— , ;
Remains to say that, in spite of all,
Kipling is for the most part an original
observer, an original writer ; that some of
his verses will linger long, and that some of
his stories are imperishable ; and that, be-
cause his work is so good,' and some of it is
in its way so great, it is all the more pity
that he should become official pander to
“ the baser,military and commercial spirit ” ;
all the more pity that his writings should be
marked by so many lapses of taste and exe-
cution, of truth and honesty. This note of
some of Kipling's shortcomings is written to
assist in “ putting him where he belongs,”
and to serve as counterblast, to the rabid
adulation of the mob. That he should be
praised as one of the most forcible writers
of the last, century, in prose and in verse, is
no less his desert ; though his verse is rarely
poetry, and often his prose is merely
vigorous journalese.

Bards of the Backblocks
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A certain intensely-Jingo M.L. paper
comes nearer to the truth than it imagines by
continually referring to the York trip as “ the
Royal Visitation.”
Concerning Port Augusta, to which John
Forrest hopes to bring a railway from Kal-
goorlie :
There is no more melancholy-looking spectacle in
Australia than Port Augusta, at the head of Spencer’s
Gulf— the deep and narrow waterway which runs up
hundreds of miles into S.A. On the map its position
seems unrivalled; it looks as though it must command
more of the trade of Central Australia than any other
port. And, as a port, too, it was found remarkably well-
favored ; the biggest ships could come alongside. So 20
years ago it had a memorable boom ; it was placarded
everywhere as the coming “ Liverpool of the South.”
Wool-growing was at its best ; wheat-growing in the
north was giving phenomenal results ; there was talk of
vast mineral fields in the little-explored beyond. The
Govt, built large wharves ; the banks built pretentious
offices ; merchants lined the streets with warehouses ;
flour-mills and breweries followed ; squatting syndicates
established depots. Then drought knocked out
the squatting and farming, and in later years
has practically killed both. Port Pirie, lower down
the Gulf, collared the Broken Hi 1 trade, and
Govt, spent there enough money to make of it a
decent shipping place, wherefore what little trade was
still left for Pt. Augusta slipped away to the newer place;
and the Centralian mines refused to pay divs. So Pt.
Augusta’s trade dropped from hundreds of thousands to
thousands, whereas every provision had been made for it
to go to millions. To-day the town is almost deserted ;
one can rent a squatter’s dismantled mansion or a ware-
house, whichever one pleases, for a crown a week. The
score or so of tired people who still maunder in and out of
-the ruins survive on the hope that Someday the overland
line to Westralia will pass that way ; though how that is
going to fill their pockets they are much too far-gone to
either think or tell.
Threshing.
“What pressure?” “r u.jl sixty.” “Blow-up! ”
and the ricketty thresher’s a-hum ;
The bandcutters’ knives are a-gleaming, the feede
leans over the drum.
Buzz-z — Boom ! and a thump, and she’s going
“Shut-off, there ! she’s cracking the grain !
“ Quick ! here with the blessed screw-wrenches !
—and now she’s adjusted again.
“ All right! ” And again she is going; the cock; s
a-hunt for a flaw,
And out at the old elevator he’s doubtfully feelii f
the straw.
“No drink ! Oh, the drought of Gehenna is bad,
but ’tis certainly less . . .
Where the devil and snakes is the water ? —wha!
consciences cookies possess ! ”
But it’s fork-in the sheaves, and it’s fill her ; the
owner on profit is keen,
And the cocky is fain to be rid of the men with
the threshing-machine.
The feeder is growling at random, and cursing the
thistles and docks;
“Belt’s broken !’’—the flywheel is spinning
“Shut-off ! or she’ll loosen the chocks.”
But nvetted-up and well resined the belt’s in
position again,
And it’s ram the stuff in till the riddles are
choked with the rush of the grain.
The strawstack’s a wonderful stru3ture, and
threatens each minute to fall,
Yet the cocky’s commanding tie builder; “Go
on ! it must carry it all.”
Then the stacker commences to “ grow it,” the
cocky instructing him how—
“ Look out !” And they’ve got a foundation fo
carry it easily now.
And owner and cocky in concert can vent on the
stacker their spleen;
Though doubtful’s the wisdom of cursing a man
with a threshing-machine.
A whistle. “Lunch, oh!” —and we’re rushing
like demons set suddenly free—
Grab pannikins; dip in the bucket, and greedily ,
swallow the tea.
And tucker and tea disappear at a rate that nigh
threatens to choke;
For some of the coveted “ spell-oh ! ” is wanted
by most for a smoke.
And some are a-talking of pleasure, and more are
a-talking of graft, I
And one sits away from the crowd till the others
aver that he’s daft;
While down upon all is a-blazing the sun from the j
pitiless blue,
And rises and fences shim-shimmer away to the
limit of view.
“Time’s up !” And once more we are grafting,
but fun is where curses have been,
For a full stomach sweetens the tempers of men
with a threshing-machine.
And so the day goes, and for supper more tea and
salt junk we devour ;
And then with the timekeeper tally our totals at
sixpence an hour.
Then off to the stable or strawstack, or under the
stars for the night ;
Our camp doesn’t trouble the cocky as long as
we’re back by the light.
Or perhaps—it depends on “ permission it’s
dance in the kitchen awhile—
Where, spite of the graft of the daytime, we foot
it in rollicking style.
And seekers of pleasure saw never a brighter or
happier throng
Than trips to the old concertina, or listens to
somebody’s song.
Let them sneer—in their envy—the cynics of cold,
hypocritical mien,
As we waltz with the cocky’s plump daughters,
we men of the threshing-machine !

JONAH UP TO DATE.
SKIPPER BARTON (to Jonah Fegan): “Very sorry, Jonah, old man, but all this row has been kicked up because we took you on board; so overboard you go—only
we were a-thinking whether it would be more agreeable to your feelings to be chucked over, or to quietly step over yourself. Good-bye; dare say you’ll
find it more comfortable inside the whale than here!”

whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,

will be regarded as strictly confidential.

address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we

are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, APRIL 27, 1901.
“The Bulletin’s” Reverence for
England.
Editor Bulletin Your constant carping at
everything English disgusts me, and many others like me.
What is the cause of your undying hatred of the English
people and English institutions? Is it not possible for
anything English to please your bilious intelligence, or
to be good enough to meet your lofty approval ?—Extract
from indignant Jingo letter received at this office.
• • • •
The above letter, which dropped in the
other day, and occasional letters of the same
tenor which have dropped in on occasional
previous days, set this paper thinking hard.
Especially they set it thinking hard on the
grievous lack of intelligence that character-
ises its fellow-creatures. The Bulletin., as
far as it can find out, after close and careful
observation, is about the only consistent
admirer that Britain possesses in the Seven
States of Australasia. It is not an indis-
criminating admirer, certainly. It has never
alleged that everything Britain is or
does is good, and everything it isn't or
doesn’t is bad. That kind of admiration
is of no use to anybody. But this paper’s
voice has grown hoarse with constantly urging
the Australian people for 10 years back to
follow the admirable example of Britain on
many points, and its failure to induce them
to do so is probably owing to the fact that it
has been, and still is, a lone and solitary
admirer. A solitary admirer, however
powerful, isn’t sufficient in such a case. The
Bulletin has had to struggle against the
causeless, unreasoning, brutish and incurable
hatred of British policy, British institutions
and British example manifested on almost
every point by such men as Paukes, McMil-
lan, Bruce Smith, Reid, Sedoon, Philp,
Nelson, Forrest, Braddon. Fysh, Gillies,
and practically all the other old-rut poli-
ticians of Australasia. They have manife? 1
it, not by words, which matter 1 ; ‘
nothing, but by deeds, which matt
deal; and this paper, as Britai olitary
but discriminating admirer, : eary of

* * * •
The British characteristic which this paper
most reverences is a certain inveterate ten-
dency towards honesty in matters of public
finance. Such men as Reid, Gillies,
Philp, Lyne, Forrest, and Braddols would
be impossible at the British Treasury, and
the most impossible of them all would be
Reid. In England, instead of being leader
of the Opposition, with the possible rever-
sion of the Premiership in view, his awful
politico-financial record (if a man could have
such a record in the British House of Com-
mons) would almost certainly have led by
this time to his expulsion from the House.
It would certainly have led to a state of
things in which the meanest constituency in
the three kingdoms would hesitate to return
him as its member. The Briton doesn’t
understand Australia’s shiftless, dishonest
policy of making the accounts balance by
paying a great part of the ordinary
daily expenditure out of foreign loans,
and assuring the public that road-re-
pairs and forest-thinning and sand-shifting
and pauper-relief are “permanent and
reproductive works.” In all such matters
the British Government pays its way by
taxation, and if it can’t pay its way out of
existing taxation it levies more taxation.
And there is no reason why Australasia should
not do likewise, unless it be that to men
like Reid and Lyne and Philp and Nelson
and all the ruck of loud-mouthed Jingo poli-
ticians a good example becomes bad when it
is British. All these men and their kind
protest their admiration for Britain and its
institutions loudly enough in words, but
their absolute refusal to adopt even one of
the good principles of Britain’s policy adver-
tises, in deeds that speak more vociferously
than words, their hatred of, or indifference
to, all British examples and institutions.
The Bulletin admires the cleanliness and
honesty of Britain’s national finance, and
the public spirit which compels it to be
clean and honest ; and its admiration is of
that practical kind which makes it quite
ready to pay its share of the extra expense
required to place Australia's affairs on the
same footing. Australia’s financial principles
are a mixture of a stink-pot and a three-card
trick—but they are anti-British, and that is
apparently good enough for the average
Australian politician.
• • * •
Britain, like Australia, has a huge debt,
much of it created by processes whose folly
is too painful to be alluded to now. But
however foolish Britain might have been in
building up its debt, its courage in facing
the burden is magnificent. Almost every
year for the past three-quarters of a century,
Britain, by a dogged process of enormous
taxation, has paid something off its past ob-
ligations and done something to make the
burden lighter for its posterity. The process
of reduction has been interrupted over and
over again by new wars and new war-bills,
but still the tendency has always been
towards improvement. Britain has fought
fully fifty campaigns, large and small, in-
cluding the hideously expensive Crimean
and Transvaal experiences, since the late
Queen Victoria attained the throne, and its
public debt is smaller now than it was then.
It has met all this tremendous outlay out of
taxation, and still found means to reduce its
old liabilities. The Bulletin utterly
loathes the policy which led to many of these
wars, but it has nothing but respect for the
pluck of the nation which was resolved to
pay for its own sins, and not charge them to
posterity. The present South African war
has spread into three financial years, and
twice the British Government has made
great additions to the public taxation—the
second one was equal in itself to 10 per cent,
added to all the previous heavy burdens. If
Britain had a few more admirers like The
Bulletin, this country’s debt would be
much lighter and its future prospects a good
deal more hopeful.
* * • *
Britain is a Freetrade country in theory,
just as is New South Wales. But Britain is
also a Freetrade country in practice—at
least to this extent, that every Customs
duty levied on any article which can be
locally manufactured is counterbalanced by
an equivalent excise duty on the local pro-
duct. The Bulletin has no faith in Free-
trade, but at least it can admire a country
which has the courage of its opinions. No
British Chancellor of the Exchequer ever
preached Freetrade while protecting almost
every important local industry, as did
Parkes, M'Millan, Bruce Smith, and in a
less degree, George Reid, in New South
Wales. There is sufficient sense of
honesty or sense of consistency in the
British community to make such lamentable
political frauds impossible. No British
paper preaches Freetrade as do the Sydney
Herald, News, and Telegraph, while
enjoying and truculently demanding 50 per
cent. Protection under the postal regula-
tions. The British public is probably honest
enough to put its great aggregate boot
through any such journal. And no British
Government whines that, though It be-
lieves in Freetrade, it is (fltompblled to levy
Protectionist duties foil revenue purposes ;
if reveuue is needed, it puts up the
income tax to Is. 2d. in the £, and gets
the cash without outraging its alleged prin-
ts for years been
f its profound
eo ...■ . ... i. J,. .u . c and if it
had been backed by one adi of equal
weight it is just possible t, N.S.W.
finance would not be the laughing-stock it
has been.
* * * *
Also, there are other matters in which
this paper has been a lonesome voice in a
public wilderness, expressing its deep respect
for Britain and its people and its institu-
tions, and trying in vain to make headway
against the general contempt for the distant
land’s good example. Sydney, the city of
less than 500,000 people, which is governed
by 43 mayors and corporations, is an ab-
surdity which has no counterpart in the pro-
gressive land from which our progenitors
came—at their own or the public expense.
The awful municipal misgovernments of
Brisbane, Melbourne and Adelaide would
be equally impossible there ; large cities are
not run in that fashion nowadays
in the United Kingdom. The Bulle-
tin has wasted quarts of good ink in
pointing to such good examples as Glasgow
and Birmingham, and explaining to the
Legislature that it is hopeless to wait until
42 out of the 43 mayors and corporations
agree to abolish themselves for the sake of
unity, and urging that the Legislatures
should abolish the whole crowd and make
one sane municipal government on British
principles for all Sydney and its environs,
and do likewise in regard to the other
capitals. Likewise this paper has become
somewhat hoarse with explaining that in no
decent British city are the streets allowed to
go to rack and ruin, and slums go from bad
to worse, and huge deficits to pile up, merely
in order that the fatted landlord may not pay
a higher rate than Is. 4d. in the £. The
municipalities which are doing great work in
Britain realise that an old city can’t be made
new without money. And even when the
improvement schemes of their municipal
rulers have forced up the rates to 6s. Bd. in
the £ the people don’t cast them out with
the contumely which generally falls on the
Australian alderman who proposes a penny
increase in order to pay for shifting dead
animals off the highway.
* * * ♦
Also the roads-and-bridges member does
not flourish in the United Kingdom. The
local bodies build their own culverts* and
don’t spend £4O on a deputation to London
to ask for a £2O drain-pipe out of the con-
solidated revenue. British municipalities
much smaller than Sydney build far larger
works than the North Shore bridge, and pay
interest and sinking-fund out of the rates
without turning a hair, instead of weeping
for 35 years in the ear of the Government
for assistance. Britain has no such infamy
as the hanging laws of New South Wales ;
it outgrew them 30 or 40 years ago. And
no civilised and educated Englishman would
believe his ears if he were told that, during
the last 10 years, in Australia—a land
alleged to be peopled by people of his own
race—there had been about 200 cases
wherein dirty minded females brought capi-
tal charges against men, and were proved to
have done so by means of the grossest
perjury, and yet in not one case did the
law interfere to put down this rampant ini-
quity. The Bulletin has no reverence at all
for any single one of those British institu-
tions which Australian Jingoes worship ;
the Australian Jingo worships only those
features of the British character which won’t
cost him any money, and which may lead
him to a title. Its opinion of the monarchial
idea, and the House of Peers, and the big
landlord system, and the blatant land-steal
system, and the morals of the London stock
exchange, is very much akin to its opinion of
Sodom and Gomorrah. But, on the other
hand, it reverences a great many British ideas
which are honest and useful instead of large
and glaring and bounceable. And setting one
set of ideas against the other, The Bulletin
reckons that it reverences and desires to
perpetuate in Australasia more British in-
stitutions than does almost any other person
or paper in Australasia. It may have a
whole-souled contempt for Britain’s South
African war-policy and for the Australian
Fat Men who want to transplant that spirit
of butchery and boodle to these shores. But
against this it yields to no one in its admira-
tion of Britain’s income tax of Is. or
Is. 2d. in the £, and its local taxation
of, say, 6s. in the £, and the other means
by which it persistently toils at paying
off its debt burdens, and this paper is grieved
in its inmost soul to find that the
disloyal Fat Man of this country shows an
insensate hatred of British institutions when
these tilings are mentioned, and absolutely
foams at the mouth when it is proposed to
express our loyalty to the mother-land by
following its example in these matters. The
Bulletin lias serious thoughts of being the
founder and chief patron of a new loyal
institution—a British Admiration Society
whose business it will be to grovel to British
ideas, and urge this country to adopt them
in a spirit of servile reverence. And the
first British idea that the new society will
grovel to will be that of paying for all un-
productive public works—fortifications and
the like—out of taxation, and putting up
the income-tax to at least Is. in the £, in
order to create a fund for the repayment of
the public debt. Notice of other British
ideas to be grovelled to will be given in due
course.
The Grave Cradle Question.
A recent number of the Australasian Medi-
cal Gazette, referring to the gradual falling-
off of the N.S.W. birth-rate, advocates the em-
ployment of scientific means to obtain an increase
of population through a diminished death-rate.
It points but that the decline in the birth-rate a
due to the use of preventive checks to concep-
tion ; And that artificial prevention of con-
sequences ” will grow by spread of knowledge.
With the process of the suns the birth-rate will
fall to the level of that of France, in which country
Government endeavours to stimulate population
by giving bonuses to the parents of large families.
The journal just referred to quotes certain figures
from N.S.W. Statistician CoGHLAN'S recently-
published “Vital Statistics,” a very valuable
book of tables, with an exhaustive introduction
by way of commentary. This publication, the
first of its kind in Australasia, is not to be con-
founded with the “Vital Statistics” number of
the “ Statistical R gister” ; for the new book is
an absolute survey, and not merely a summary, of
the thousand and one items of information com-
prising this interesting branch of economic-social
inquiry.
The Australasian Medical Gazette shows
that in 1890 the total births in N.S. Wales
numbered 38,960; while in 1899 they numbered
36,461. During this period of a decade the
population had increased from 1,121,000 to
1,345,000 ; but the birth-rate had fallen from 35
per thousand living in 1890 to 27 per thousand
living in 1899. There were actually 2500 fewer
children born in 1899 than in 1890 ; while in 1899
there were 4329 deaths of infants who had not
reached the first year of life, and 5762 who
had not reached their fifth year. The Medical
Gazette insists that it is certain that half of these
perished from preventible causes. If, therefore,
2500 could have been saved, something would have
been done to counterbalance the decrease in the
birth-rate.
* * * *
The Government Statistician’s figures show that
of the whole number of deaths those of children
under five years is proportionately much more
than half the total mortality of persons of all ages
above five years. This is better realised from a
short table:—
Deaths Under Deaths Under Deaths all Ages
One Year. 6 Years. Over 5 veara,

1891 .. 4,691 .. 6,5X0 .. 9,776
1892 .. 4,245 .. 6,827 .. 8,583
1893 .. 4,640 .. 7,072 .. 8,950
1894 .. 4,248 .. 6,913 .. 9,257

• * * *
From the foregoing it will be seen that in 1899
the number of deaths in N.S. Wales of persons of
all ages over five years was 10,139 ; and the num-
ber of deaths Of infants under five years was 5762;
but, while the ratio was ih the first case only B'sB
per one thousand of the population over five, it
was in the second case 35‘21 per thousand of the
population under five. Is it possible to save any
of these thirty-five minus-five-year-olds in every
thousand from their almost consistently average
doom ?
« * • •
From 1890 to 1899 (both years inclusive) the
deaths of children under five ranged from 4940 to
7072 per annum, or from 29.17 to 39’94 per thou-
sand of all children under five ; while, for the
same period, the deaths of persons of all ages
above five ranged from 8442 to 10,373, or from 8 '54
to 8 ’97 per thousand of all persons above five.
The year of least infantile mortality was 1897 ; of
greatest, 1893. The year of least actual mortality
among persons above five was 1890; of greatest,
1898; however, the least ratio per thousand in this
group was in the year 1897, when it was 8'25 ; of
greatest ratio, 1891, when it was 10'03. During
the same period there died annually from 3801 to
4691 infants who had not reached the first year of
life (vide years 1897 and 1891), representing res-
pectively 102 and 118'9 per one thousand births.
The lowest ratio of deaths per thousand births of
minus-one-year-old infants was in the year 1897,
just quoted; the highest in the year 1898, when it
was 121 ’9. These figures are painful, both in fact
and in recital, but they are absolutely necessary
in order to understand the situation ; and from
them may be seen the terrific waste there is going
on in the infant-feeder to population in a country
with a steadily-decreasing birth-rate. After the
first year the trouble of rearing children gradually
diminishes. The figures representing the deaths
occurring during the year 1899 are eloquent of
this fact. Here they are: —
Under one year 4,329
One year, of age 844
Two years of age .. .. .. 282
Three years of age 174
Four years of age 133
Five and under ten years 374
All ages above ten ye ars 9,765
Total deaths 15,901
The excess of births over deaths was, in 1880,
2'39 ; in 1890, 2'29 ; in 1899, 1*54 ; or, taking the
mean annual excess of births over deaths in five-
year periods:—lBBo-4, 2'3; 1885-9, 2*27 ; 1890-4,
211; and 1890 onwards, 1'67. Actual excess of
births over deaths was, in 1890, 24,742; and in
1899, 20,560 —always diminishing. There is a
greater mortality of adult males over adult
females, and of male children over female children.
During the ten years which closed with 1899 the
number of females added to the population by
excess of births over deaths exceeded that of the
males by 15,041, or a trifle over 14 per cent. The
reason of this is that males, particularly in
country districts, are more greatly exposed to risk
by accident than are females ; while male infants
are more delicate than female infants, and are
more difficult to rear.
• * * *
Stress is laid by Mr, Coghlan on the effects of
illegitimacy on infantile mortality. Of the 4329
infants who died in 1899 before reaching the
first year of life, 3584 were legitimate and 745
were illegitimate; and of the 5762 children who
died in the same year before reaching the fifth
year of life, 4940 were legitimate and 822 illegiti-
mate. The proportional death-rate per one thou-
sandliving of each class was, therefore: Under
one year, legitimate, 105‘9; illegitimate, 285‘6;
total, 1187: under five years, legitimate 32;
illegitimate, 86'4; total, 35'2. These figures show
that at each age the death-rate of the illegitimates
was nearly three times as high as that of the legiti-
mates ! These figures are sufficiently appalling ;
nevertheless, illegitimacy fails to account for the
deaths of 4940 infants under five years of age.
* * * *
The number of illegitimate births in 1899 was
2609, equal to 7.16 per cent, of the total number
of births. Illegitimacy in N.S. Wales is making
big strides. In 1871 it was 3'88; in 1876, 4 - 08;
in 1881, 4’36; in 1886, 4’65 ;in 1891, 5 ’36 ;in 1896,
670; and in 1899, 7'16. Concurrently with ille-
fitimaey pure and simple, “undesirables” are
roug'nt into the world through the medium of
ante-nuptial conceptions. If all illegitimates are
taken as first-births, then of 56,163 first births re-
corded during the six years. 1893-8, the post
nuptial conceptions contributed 28,018, or 49'89
per cent; ante nuptial conceptions, 13,366, or
23'8; and illegitimates, 14,779, or 2331 percent.
There is little doubt that many of the ante-


nuptially-conceived are sedulously “not” pre
vented from dying, if they show any grave
tendencies towards surcease of sorrow in the realm
of the cold and silent.
• • * *
France, ever careful of her children, as befits a
country steadily becoming sterilised, manages
this question in a very public-spirited fashion.
Children are National Capital which she cannot
afford to waste. In all the great cities are huge
Government asylums designed for the reception
of “any” children —“and no questions asked.”
The despairing mother —whether deceived country
girl, abandoned mistress, deserted wife, hopeless
widow—can find a refuge for her child without
revealing her own identity. A little alcove in a
blank wall, a tiny turnstile that revolves inward,
and a bell-rope. Date, tattoo, or other
mark, description, name, any information
may be affixed, if desired, to substantiate
a future claim to the foundling—or no
information. And the child is taken, and
fed, and reared, and educated, and taught a trade
by a maternal government which acts as a foster-
parent to the baby derelicts. And thus might
N.S.W. settle the foundling difficulty, and save
much wasted population, if her Governments were
not so aggressively righteous and bound up
with the red-tape of soggy British tradition.
But still, even a very “righteous” Government
might do something by the passing of an Act
making it penal to feed babies on solid food,
patent slushes, and the contents of the iniquitous
feeding-bottle. Illegitimates die by their hun-
dreds, but legitimates by their thousands. Help-
less and ignorant mothers have, too, the abomin-
able vice of “ feeding ” the empty-bowelled infant
with a fiendish india-rubber arrangement inflated
with air, and called a “comforter,” at which the
poor little wretch sucks and draws with des-
perate energy, and bellies itself out thereby
with copious draughts of the circumambient air.
The chemist, or other tradesman, who sold such
an invention of Satan to a fool-woman should be
suspended—suspended any way the Law might
choose to direct. Thus something might be done
for the lives of even the legitimately born—and
done, too, by Act of Parliament.
* * * *
But illegitimacy is on the increase, and the life
of the illegitimate child is threatened as three to
one in the legitimate baby’s case. The old King
of Prussia, father of Frederick the Great, was
wont to scour the eartli for gigantic Guardsmen.
He placed all races and creeds under tribute, and
fought his battles with his foemen’s own blood
and bone. If militarism still continues to lift its
crimson crest, and the flesh-food for powder con-
tinues in some countries, and for various reasons,
to diminish ; mayhap, someday, a great statesman
may grasp the situation, and send his emissaries
far and wide, picking up the sturdy waifs of the
world’s breeding, whereof to make soldiers and
soldiers’ wives. The raw material, thus collected
in countless thousands, and with secret despatch,
would be trained in depots till ready for drafting
into army-corps. Battles could then be fought
without spilth of the blood of one’s own kinsmen,
and pleasure given to all. The Turks knew all
about it, and the famed Janissaries were among
the finest of European troops.
* * • *
But that day is not yet. Meanwhile the camp-
kettle of Death is daily filled with the tender
flesh of the “ not-wanted ” on the voyage of life.
What is now required is a mighty-brained crank,
a millionaire who would spend his wealth, not in
the collection of rare china, or rare buildings, or
yachts, or banquets, or the entertaining of princes
and other gilt rubbish ; but in the care and nur-
ture and education of the unnecessary waif.
There is a loud call on the part of humanity for a
Collector of Children, a Miser in the Conservation
of Infancy. Find this man we must ; and surely
among the multitudes of the faddists he can
be found, and with wealth, too, to give concretion
to his benevolent insanity ; and the waif-waste is
as good as dammed at its source.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Old Age Pensions.
N.S.W. Old Age Pension application forms are
now being distributed, and are obtainable at any
court-house, police-station or P.O. throughout the
State. After being filled up they can be lodged
with any clerk of petty sessions. As in Vic-
toria, the granting of a pension is hedged
around with many qualifications and reserva-
tions. The technicalities involved are quite
drastic enough to prevent many deserving old
people receiving a little necessary assistance in
their declining years, and more than sufficient to
unduly swell the cost of administering the law.
No one can obtain a pension if he or she has been
absent from the State more than two years in the
last 25 ; has deserted his or her lawful mate for
six months or upwards ; has been gaoled for five
years during the 25 years’ residence in
the State ; has been imprisoned for four months,
or four times, during the 12 years immediately
preceding the application ; is not of good moral
character—whatever that may mean ; has not led
a sober or reputable life for the last.five years—
whatever the legal definition of sobriety may be ;
has a yearly income over £52 per annum, or pro-
perty worth £390 nett; has transferred property
or income to a relative in order to qualify for a
pension ; or is a naturalised subject of less than 10
years standing before applying for a pension. In
addition to all these reservations there is a whole
list of deductions to be made should applicant
have anything at all saved, or have arranged for
board or lodging by living with a son or daughter,
or any other relative. Most of the deductions arc
direct penalties for thrift, and one wonders where
Fatman (who is always talking about encourage-
ment for ther-rift) was when those regulations
went through. In ICO years’ time people will
probably lay down their history books to marvel
that men were ever so barbaric as to put such
restrictions on the means of life and comfort in
old age. Just now the Old Age Pension idea is
new. In a little while the view will broaden, and
people will realise that it is a useless sort of
foolishness to waste money employing an army of
spies and officials to prevent some unfortunate
wretch who has saved a few pounds getting an
extra week. To enforce these restric-
tions “and prevent the Treasury being looted,”
Vic. Premier Peacock has enlisted a horde of
long-nosed females of jagged virtue, who will
prove a curse and a plague to the people who
have failed to provide for their old age. What
step will be taken to prevent N.S.W. Treasury
being looted is not yet known. Probably an army
of clerks and investigators will be engaged and paid
out of borrowed money, till the cost of protecting
the virtuous Treasury will be more than it would
have lost had it not been safeguarded. Under
present circumstances, it is certain much injustice
will be inflicted, and many old people, too proud,
or without the nerve or knowledge to push their
claims, will be bluffed out of the assistance they
are entitled to. In Victoria, hosts of them will
lose it through failing to takeoff their hats quickly
enough, or not addressing the Benevolent Society
lady investigator with the respect due to a person
of her pomp and circumstance. The whole tenor
of the proceedings in both Vic. and N.S.W.
shows that those who are administering the Act
regard it as another form of charitable relief, and
granted as a benevolent favor by the taxpayer.
Which is utterly wrong. There’s no charity in it.
It’s a provision made by the whole community for
the whole community’s old age. It is Old Age
Endowment without a costly system of book-
keeping to ensure that each member of the com-
munity shall pay his full contribution and no
more or no less. If the pensions are paid out of
revenue and spent in the country, the necessary
taxation to raise the means to pay them will lose
half its sting. Every man, pauper or millionaire,
is entitled to an old age pension from the
state if the whole community is taxed to raise
the money. Therefore, let him have it, and let
the scheme be what it was intended to be—a
system of national old age assurance. If a man
pays into an ordinary Assurance Society no one
dreams of deducting a pound from his endowment
for every pound he may have saved up; why
should it be done because the State collects from
each a premium according to his means, on the
understanding that he shall be insured against
want and privation in his last stage?

The Mildura Grovel Agent.
The Bulletin has received some printed matter
relating to the l)uke of York, the Mildura (Vic.)
Irrigation Settlement, J. F. Hogan, M.P., and
other persons whose bearing on the matter seems
rather mixed. The one document which can be
understood at a glance is a circular addressed by
John Vincent to the Duke aforesaid, informing
him that the Mildura scheme “signalised Her
Majesty’s Jubilee year in the annals of Australian
colonisation.” Also the circular begs to remind
the Duke of York that some five or six years ago
he acknowledged “in very kind terms” a com-
munication from John Vincent (of England)
offering an orange plantation of 20 acres at Mil-
dura to the Duke’s newly-born son, in order to
associate the “Royal House at so auspicious a
time with the agricultural prosperity of Australia
in connection with an enterprise ... of
almost imperial value and importance, &c.,
&c.” Then the circular goes on to state
that Mildura, including the 20 acres dedicated
to the Royal House, is in such a prosperous con-
dition that the loyal settlers have no need to
apologise for their temerity. The offer was
“ tendered in the firmest conviction of the success
of the undertaking,” for indeed, says John
Edwaud M. Vincent, “such a sincere belief, I
fully appreciated, could alone have justified that
offer, or even the association of the enterprise in
any way with the deeply revered name of Her
Late Majesty, which is so loyally borne by the
colony in which the undertaking was inaugurated.”
The final paragraph of this deplorable address
assumes a probability of “ Ybrk” going down to
Mildura to receive a “ hearty British greeting ”
from the inhabitants “ most of whom went out
directly from Great Britain &c.” (whatever “ &c.”
may mean in that connection) and inspect the 20
acres set apart for his Royal Infant Son. This
will explain why the poor, worried little “Duke”
visits the place where the dried apricots come
from—if he should visit it. J. E. M. Vincent,
“ late Chief Commissioner in Europe for
the Australian Irrigation Colonies ” is the
Power behind the Grovel. It is he who has dis-
covered that Ciiaffey Bros., the Canadian specu-
lators and founders of Mildura, were actuated by
a noble desire to “signalise Her Majesty’s Jubilee
year.” He, J. E. M. Vincent, was the man who
prepared in 1888 “an illustrated publication, of
which Her Majesty graciously received a special
copy,” containing loyal quotations “from the
vice-regal speech of Sir Henry Loch ” ; and if
there is a blessed Mildura knighthood among
York’s big bundle of ennobblements, Vincent is
the person who certainly ought to get it.
The Sydney Octopus.
The details of the Senate poll in N.S.W. give
some hard knocks to the great Freetrade victory.
To begin with, J. T. Walker, who topped the poll
with 80,000 votes, had over 102,000 votes cast
against him. Pulsford’s name appeared on
70,000 formal ballot-papers, and was omitted on
112,000. Yet he was elected. A very different
conclusion to Barton’s return for the Federal
Convention when he polled 100,000 out of 147,000
recorded. The big majority vote in Sydney and
environs forbade any hope for the Govt. Bunch.
In the city constituencies O’Connor was beaten
by J. P. Gray, the unsuccessful man of the Free-
trade bunch, by more than 10,000, and only his
well-known personality saved him in the country.
It would have taken Barton himself to have suc-
cessfully combated such a plump. View the re-
sult this way. Walker headed the poll in ten
constituencies (mostly urban), O’Connor in nine
(all country), and John Norton in four. Gould
was first in Werriwa, Neild in Wentworth
(his own patch), and the Labor candi-
dates in the Barrier. Thus the Freetraders
only headed the voting in 12 electorates out of
2G. A small table will show more clearly how
Sydney ruled the roost. This table gives the total
votes in the 11 districts! governed by the Metro-
politan majority—Sydney E., W., N. and S.,
Parkes, Parramatta, Illawarra, Wentworth, Dai-
ley, Lang and Werriwa. If the last is objected
to as not being a strictly city constituency
the result is practically unaltered. The four
candidates compared are Walker, who polled the
highest among the Freetraders ; O’Connor, ditto
of the Protectionists; Pulsford, who was the
lowest of the selected Freetraders to win a seat;
and W. P. Manning, the highest defeated Pro-
tectionist :
Town Votes, Total.
Walker .. .. 44,939 33,061 78,000
O’Consor .. .. 28.359 44,497 72,858
Pulsford .. .. 42,565 27,903 70,468
Manning .. .. 16,650 31,460 45,110
This clearly shows the Great Sydney Swamp.
O‘Connor is over 11,000 votes ahead of Walker
in the country, yet has to be content with fourth
place on the list. Manning, beaten out of sight
by over 20,000 for a place, polled 3000 votes more
outside Sydney and its influence than did Puls-
ford. In any future election for the Senate,
the Hare system or some such expedient will
be necessary in order that the country constitu-
encies may have some share of representation.

Tlie Fegan Scandal.
It is reported at time of writing that the Barton
Cabinet will take the discreet course ; and that J.
Lionel Fegan —the tricky political hack who
jumped-into the post of Federal Internal Under-
secretary, over the heads of able public employes
of long service in six States, because of Minister
Dyne's gratitude for his political utility—will re-
tire as gracefully as may be. Sydney Telegraph
attempts to make REiD-capital out of the report
by saying the Cabinet is in an awkward posi-
tion. There is no awkwardness worth mention—
certainly none which need go beyond the Cabinet
table. The Cabinet was not consulted when
Minister Lynk made the appointment; and the
Cabinet has a perfect right to tell Minister
Lyne that, in consequence of what has
happened, or may happen, the appointment
was unwise. Then Minister Lyne can
appoint a better man, without Fegan’s
disqualifications, and with the approval of the
Cabinet. So far from such action showing Cabinet
weakness, it will show Cabinet strength ; because
it will prove to the public that, one of its members
having blundered, the Cabinet as a whole desires
to set the blunder right. And the public; will
accept the action as a proof that it is not intended
to belie election promises, and that honest ad-
ministration is honestly desired. Fegan’s reten -
tion will certainly weaken the Barton Cabinet.
Fegan’s replacement by a more suitable man will
tend to strengthen the Cabinet more than if
Fegan had never been appointed ; because it will
show that the Cabinet is determined to go straight
in defiance of any crooked member.

Pertinently writes Wellington (M.L.) Evening
Post : —“ The language of exaggeration is now so
commonly employed by some leading personages
in the community and their immediate followers
in regard to all matters relating to the
Royal family that instead of stimulating
loyalty, they are repelling it
The principal offender against good taste and
commonsense in this matter,” proceeds the plain-
spoken Post, “is the Premier, and perhaps the
worst specimen of his pompous grandiloquence is
to be found in his cabled message of condolence to
the British Nation : —‘ Sad tidings reached us.
Grief, profound sorrow; we must bow sub-
missively ; God’s will be done ; terrestrial crown
surrendered, assured crown glory won ; heart-
felt sympathy all peoples will go forth bereaved
Royal Family ; earnest prayers offered our
Divine Master, strengthen them great hour
trial; they have lost loving mother, human-
ity true noble friend ; grief sorrow world-
wide prevails irreparable loss.— Seddon.’ This,”
concludes the Post, “is the language of ex-
travagance and insincerity, and is degrading to
the loyalty of a self-respecting people.” The
Bulletin says Amen—and finds it hard moreover
to conceive of anything more preposterously gro-
tesque than this attempt to pack profound affliction
and the loftiest of sentiments into the jerky utter-
ances of the economy-seeking telegram. Fervour
and frugality—a People’s Sorrow fired-off by
machine-gun, and the Patriot’s Lofty Soul
with one eye on the bawbees! What a
saving grace is a sense of Humour ! And
what revolting muck is produced by the cold-
blooded stringing-together, especially on such
an occasion as that of Seddon’s cablegram, of the
pharisaic shibboleths of the bourgeois politician
with the Sunday-school education ! How the
scholarly Agent-General Reeves, on whom, no
doubt, devolved at the other end the hideous task
of knocking his boss’s hypocrisies into shape,
must have grinned !
♦
N.S. W. State Labor-party loses another of its
brainier men—chased out by a crowd jealous of
his reputation and hungry for his seat. Fergu-
son, M.P. for one of the Broken Hill seats, is the
victim this time. He committed two crimes :
First, he accepted by wire the Labor nomination
for Kalgoorlie (W.A.) in the House of Reps., and
a few days later withdrew ; then he went up to
Bathurst to fight Pumpkin Smith as the Protection-
ist candidate. There wasn’t anything serious in
either charge, and, anyway, Ferguson bad a com-
plete answer to both. As to the Kalgoo/lie busi-
ness, he wanted to say that he withdrew because
he received information that there was no effective
Labor organisation there, and that Kirwan, a
good Democrat, with a powerful paper and a
thorough canvass at his back, had a mort-
gage on the seat. And as to the Bathurst
business, he wanted to say that the man
who ran on the labor ticket alone didn’t have a
dog’s chance of winning; and so, while signing
the Labor pledge, he also accepted the policy of
the Government, which, if it includes Barton and
Forrest, also includes Kingston and Turnfr.
He wanted to say these things in his defence ; but
the local Laborite bosses, nearly every one of
whom is seeking the Party’s nomination for one or
other of the Barrier seats, got in ahead of him
with a rule that candidates for the nomination
must not canvass; and they ruled that if Fer-
guson answered the widely-circulated charges he
would, according to the rule, be disqualified.
Whereupon he did the only thing he could —he
refused to be judged when only his accusers had
spoken ; he defied the local P.L.L., he answered
the charges, and he carried with him one of the
biggest and most enthusiastic meetings held at
Broken Hill. But the machine Labor-vote there
is exceedingly powerful; and though Ferguson
will win in the end, it will only be after a hard
and bitter fight.
♦
The Commission appointed, at the instance of
sundry Fatman representatives, to inquire into
the working of the Vic. Factories Act, got to
work the other day. Not one opponent of the
Act came forward voluntarily, but as the Com-
mission wanted to get both sides of the case—if
there were two sides—the Chief Secretary’s de-
partment’ kindly rounded up a few of that kidney.
They were an amusing crowd, and the most the
worst of them could say was that they had lost a
little casual, floating trade through having to
close their shops at a civilised hour. One enter-
taining draper-man objected to restrictive legis-
lation because he once wanted to get some pork-
sausages at 9.30 p.m., and the Act had closed
the shop at 9. This witness thought 10 hours’
work a day “ extremely reasonable,” also he
solemnly assured the Commission that when
a draper closed early the evening business
mostly vanished, and the people did without the
clothes they formerly bought after dark, which
indicates a thrifty but indecorous tendency on the
part of Melbourne citizens. Further, he would
allow shops to keep open all night as long as they
could do any business, though he objected
to extending the same privilege to hotels ;
and, finally, he shocked "his hearers by
asserting (what is quite true) that Great
Britain had been “ built up on unrestricted
sweating.” Next came a butcher, and several of
the biggest city tailors and drapers. These testi-
fied that the Act “ had caused a slight rise in
wages” (this was for shops only, which don’t
come under the minimum-wage clauses); “ that
early closing causes no loss of business, that time
being past ” ; “ that the fixing of a standard wage
would have no ill effect on the employer if every-
one were compelled to pay the same”; and,
lastly, from the largest tailor and men’s mercer in
Bourke-street, that “the Act had not only not
injured his business, it had done him good.”
Then the Commission adjourned to give the objec-
tors time to get their second wind.
Victorian employers evidently intend making a
concerted attempt to nullify the S' ite's Factories
Act. Following on the withdrawal of the woollen-
mill employers' delegates from the Wages Board,
their delegates on the Fel 1 mongers’ Board have
now withdrawn, because the chairman, by his
casting vote, reduced the houi’s from GO to 48 par
week. As the wages have not yet been fixed by
this Board, and as it is open to any employer who
wishes to work long hours to either pay overtime
rates or to employ double shifts, the fictitious
nature of the excuse is apparent. Lastly the
Clothing Employers omitted to nominate dele-
gates at all, without giving any reason. Mani-
festly the average Vic. manufacturer is a firm
believer in free labor and freedom of contract
—for the other fellow. He votes for Protection
every time, and takes full advantage of the policy
to protect himself against the cheap labor of
foreign countries, but, when it comes to extending
a share of that protection to his worker, he has a
pressing engagement elsewhere. Wherefore it
cannot be too strongly laid down that the policy
of Protection—to which the Barton Government
stands committed—includes equal protection to
worker and manufacturers. The natural corollary
of a Protective Tariff is a Minimum Wage. It
matters little to the worker whether he is sweated
by a foreign importer or a local manufacturer;
the only difference is that in the one case he can’t
get at the sweater, in the other he can or should,
and the Federal Parliament which legislatively
protects the employer must, if its professions are
to be of any account whatever, protect the worker
also.

This is how Brisbane Courier explains the white
victory in the Herbert electorate :
The Herbert consists of seven electorates —Bowen,
Cairns, Cook, Herbert, Mackay, Townsville, YVoothakata.
All of these electorates (two of them double seats) are
held by Ministerialists, with the single exception of
Cairns. The plain inference is that the Labor partisans
came to the poll and the electors of the other side largely
absented themselves.
There are several other plain inferences that the
Courier has (apparently) forgotten. One is that
a great many people beside the Laborites don’t
believe in a piebald Australia. Another is that
the sheet-anchor of the present rotten Govt, of
Bananaland is the property-vote, and that if that
property-vote be abolished the Philp Govt, will
go down like a house of cards. Another inference
neglected by the Courier is the very patent one
that the nearer you are to black labor the more
it stinks. The great support of the Kanaka,
during the late contest, came from Brisbane
where there isn’t any Kanaka; while the big
blows and most determined opposition to the
alien came from the workers of Wide Bay and
the Herbert, who have seen drunken Tommy
Tanna rioting down the streets, raising hell upon
earth.
Queensland Worker (April 6th)
Tiie Bulletin has no sympathy with the Labor move-
ment. The only one of the Queensland anti-Ivanaka
party for whom it said a word was M‘Donald,” &c., &c.
A very interesting statement —and an absolutely
untrue one. In the Bulletin issue of March
30th, for instance, occurs this passage :
To the people of Queensland the best possible advice is
to abjure the whole crowd of Kanaka advocates—Kanaka
Cowley most of all.
If this be not inciting the Queensland elector to
vote for the anti-Kanaka party, what is it ? The
Worker will find ample room for the exercise of
its powers of language in worrying the Northern
syndicates and endeavoring to push the Philp
Govt, into the paths of political rectitude, without
making abusive and mendacious statements about
other Democratic journals. The Worker is
highly delighted at the recent Labor victories in
Queensland ; but that organ must remember that
without Federation those victories were not
possible, and The Worker from first to last
denounced Federation as a system under which
Democratic victories were impossible.
♦
Complaining of the prevalent scarcity of labor in
country districts and its superabundance in
Melbourne, a dairy-farmer writes to the Age
wanting to know why, as the Factories Act has
put a minimum price on labor, the Govt, doesn't
fix a minimum price for butter, say 9d. per lb. The
inquiry is a trifle previous, but that doesn’t affect
the argument. Parliament has fixed a minimum
wage for labor of certain classes only, but it hasn’t
protected all workers by any means. It hasn’t,
so far, protected the farm laborers or the butter-
factory employes, and that is the chief reason
why the farming person cannot just now get
enough assistance at the irnpe he paps. But Par-
liament protected him and his butter industry,
by means of bonuses, long enough to establish it
so firmly that he now obtains a steady and higher
average price all the year round than he did when
lie ran things in the old free-and-easy style. Also,
it still protects him by instructing him in the
manufacture and supervising the shipment of
butter, and in other ways preventing him from
spoiling the sweet thing he has got. And, as this
protection and State organisation and supervision
has raised his profits while increasing the supply,
it wouldn’t be a bad notion to apply the same
remedy to his helot —the agricultural worker.

“ Karnie ” on a vital question :
Dear Bulletin. — The Commonwealth riot, the visit of
the gaudily-dressed Imperial troops, and the despatch of
the various contingents for S’Africa, has swelled the ranks
of Sydney prostitutes to an extraordinary extent, but
a still greater social scandal is the city’s child-
prostitution. Recently (March 13) while passing
the corner of Bridge and George sts. about 10.30
p.m., I saw a couple of lusty brutes of “jack-tars”
bundling four little “ flower-girls,” all of tender age, into
a couple of cabs. The sailors jumped in with the girls,
and the cabbies drove swiftly away. I walked over to a
policeman watching from the opposite corner. Said to
him, “This is something awful.” “Yes,” he said.
“ Have you no power to interfere?” “No, I could not
say for what purpose they were being driven off, and
unless they were followed, no case could be made out
against any of the parties concerned.” Now, I heard
some of the conversation between the sailors and the
girls, and certainly it was from no philanthropic motives
that they were bundled into the cabs. Again, some days
before, a little after 9 o’clock, myself and a friend, on re-
turning from a walk along the sea-wall past Circular Quay,
observed a diminutive girl sitting under the rocks sur-
rounded by three greasy Lascar seamen, one of them on each
side of her, and the other in front, trying to hide her
from our view. My friend rushed across the road, and
dragged the little girl out of their midst. On question-
ing the child, she said that one of the lascars had first
given her sixpence to buy some fruit, and then afterwards
offered her half-a-crown to go up the steps of the “ Tar-
peian Way,” one of them to go ahead and watch, while
the other remained on the steps for the same purpose.
The girl said she refused to go up the steps into the lane
with them, and they then induced her to sit down where
we discovered them, and behaved indecently to her.
Questioned farther, she said she lived with her mother,
had no father, didn’t get too much to eat, had only dry
bread for breakfast and nothing since but the fruit she
had bought with the sixpence! Said she was 12 years
old, seemed rather an intelligent child, with nothing of
the slum look about her, and rather pretty. Going home
by tram we told several passengers all this. They treated
it lightly, and some said that the mothers of' some of
these children are in the habit of hawking them round
the streets nightly for purposes of prostitution. And the
N.S.W. churches send missionaries to China 1

ATHEN.EUM Hall G.B. Biograph.
On Saturday night Miss Antonia Dolores
gave the last of her Sydney Song Recitals in the
Centennial Hall. The audience was vast, and
when Miss Dolores came out from under the
great organ she looked like a rather plump
girl standing on the steps of a gigantic wedding
cake. Organist Sykes sat him down at the grand
piano, there was a ripple of music, and Miss
Dolores commenced to sing an aria of Massenet’s,
in which Salome, the wicked daughter of
Herodias, tells of her burning passion for the
doomed John the Baptist. A song of in-
tense passion passion that had never
known restraint, and was capable of any-
thing. Salome was evidently a very excitable
woman. When the clear, crystal notes had
died away applause stormed through the vast
hall like the beating of the surf. Then
came the Song of the Cross, from far away down
the dead centuries, when in 1095 the Crusaders
started for Jerusalem. ’Twas a sombre, terrible
creation, and as it flowed forth from the lips of
the singer it breathed of the resolution of
fods and the ferocity of devils. Sung by
lolores it was grand—sung by an army of
dour fanatics it must have sounded absolutely
terrible, and filled with awe the lands through
which the wild and holy hordes passed
on their way to Jerusalem. But there is
a dismal probability that these hordes didn’t
sing at all, and that the Song of the
Cross is a purely modern imposture—though being
devoid of musical culture, the present pen-person
advances this opinion with humility. Mr. Clar-
ence Newall gave several effective piano solos.
On Sunday night Miss Dolores left for Adelaide,
where she will warble from April 24th to May
3rd. She will then go to Melbourne and Sydney
to sing at the State Concerts till the 31st, after
which she will tour the piebald State of Queens-
land.
* * * *
The announcement that Prince Charming
and Cinderella will cease to pantomime at Sydney
Royal early in May is causing a considerable rush
to view the gorgeous dresses, the winged ballet,
and the shapely figure of the gemmed poten-
tate whose wedding fills the theatre with the
odors of Araby. The Butterfly ballet is the sort
of thing a man of taste would like to keep on the
premises to brighten up wet Sunday afternoons.
Those butterflies are of an interesting species.
This paper really grieves that the songs and book
are not up to the hand-painted drapery and
ch-'rminr' pink letrs and other outward attrac-
tions. George Musgrove has done a good thing
tor the play-goer, and an evil one for the play-
manager. Unless future draperies and legs, are
up to the Musgrove standard they will be sniffed
at. And the Musgrove standard is exacting and
costly. It will take a lot of cash to maintain
shows on that glimmering pinnacle.
