Nellie Stewart will signalise the close of
her “ Cinderella ” season in Sydney in a manner
that should win her the respect and love of every-
body. To-day (Saturday, April 27) Prince Charm-
ing gives a matinee for the benefit of the Poor
Children of Sydney. With a large and generous
liberality Miss Stewart will give, not merely the
profits, but the gross receipts of the show to those
charities that are maintained for the benefit of
the young. All the expenses are to be paid by the
Lady of the Turquoises. On the same night, in the
Centennial Hall, the artists and singers of Sydney
will tender Mr. Tenor Woodhoiiso the Night in
Bohemia,” mentioned in last issue. Mr. Pack’s
benefit eventuates at Sydney Palace on the after-
noon of Thursday (25th).
# * # »
The story of “ Florodora ” is another ex-
aTriple of the great truth that success is a matter
of luck, and that luck generally falls on the
shoulders of the undeserving and foolish—where-
fore this scribe has great hopes. In “ Florodora ”
all the people who plot and scheme and work get
next to nothing, while those who “ let her slide ”
get everything. Brownlow Abercoed ;s a most
unenterprising individual, and yet he gets the
Briton’s share of the boodle in the shape of a
charming bundle of drapery known as Dolores, a
vast fortune, and a fine island, and he recovers
his ancestral halls, which had been bought
by robber Gilfain. Widow Palotta Holy-
rood works as a Kanaka is supposed
to work in a canefield, and if she gets any-
thing it is only old, worn-out Gilfain when he has
presumably lost his money. Miss Chetwynd Gil-
fain, truly, gets the penniless guardsman she
hungers for, but then, her dancing is a dream, and
entitles her to receive anything she can wish for.
Presumably daughter Gilfain inherits her floating
grace from old man Gilfain, who shakes a respect-
able leg when he likes, and he generally likes
between 9.30 and 10 p.m. every evening, just
in front of Abercoed Castle. There are points
about Gilfain as an employer, and his thoughtful
action in providing six nicely-dressed English
ladies to sing “I must love somebody, and it
might as well be you ” to his six dapper secre-
taries, is much appreciated.
# # * *
While the melodrama still goes on show-
ing us pictures of the faithful old servant who
attends the defrauded hero into exile without
wages or beer, the comedy drama, as in “ Why
Smith Left Home,” takes a leap into the future
and reveals how the servant who has learnt the
merits of trades-unionism will hold a rebellious
family in subjection by the mere possession of a
platform and the frequent enunciation of its many
ptanks. Thus, in the Palace the d.c. trembles for
the lessons which are nightly conned by the gal-
lery up above. Probably, when the Duke and
Duchess come to this country, and the poor rela-
tion in the backblocks begins to trouble the land
and worry his town brother for the loan of the
best bedroom, the astute local relative will apply
to the registry for a cook warranted to kill an
aunt in two meals and a mother-in-law in three or
four, like the treasure that rejoiced the heart of
the much-enduring Smith at the Palace Theatre,
adjacent to the Marble Bar, in Pitt-st., Sydney.
** * *
Sydney Lyceum still continues with the
badly-jointed play wherein the good old squire
allows the faithful servant to insult his guests and
use the drawing:-room as a study and a convenient
place for dressing-up and frightening the other
menials with ghost tricks. The stage keeps re-
ligiously to the manners of some twenty years
back, as when the massive adventuress curtseys
to the floor when introduced to the gentleman she
is about to poison. In the average melodrama
there is always one person who says, “I pray, sir,”
whereas nobody ever preys nowadays except with
an “e.” The maniac’s den which the wicked
husband thinks suitable as a rendezvous for his
wife still fails to excite much horror in the heart
of the audience, as the knife which descends, with
intervals for rests, is obviously something that
would crumple up at the edges when it touched
the neck of the lady below. It isn’t a realistic
knife, and its edge was obviously made in
Germany.
** * *
Sydney Empire issued a long proclama-
tion of the company’s intention to give its audi-
ence “ wit without vulgarity and fun without
coarseness,” afte% which certain people discuss
the boarding-house flea, an animal that doubtless
forms the staple subject of conversation in polite
society, and is constantly discussed in the gilded
halls of the nobility. Fred. Bluett, a new
importation, is after the style of the
Tivoli hero famed for stretching his neck
out to fathoms five or thereabouts, save that
Bluett leaves the neck-stretching out. Heba
Barlow as yet makes an indefinite impression, but
seems a nice girl on the whole. Will Stevens’
“ Soldier’s Story ” is a good item, knocking hard
at the Jingo who does brave deeds at the war to
which he doesn’t go. This sort of thing is much
to be preferred to the boarding-house flea item.
The Empire is advised to adopt the moral of that
legend so well rendered by its own John Fuller
and Amy Blackie, and like the young man with
the flag—go higher. The young man aforesaid
was no doubt a very impractical person, but,
any way, in the vast elevation at which he perished
there were no fleas.
** * *
The funeral of Queen Victoria proceshed
on its canvas background in Sydney for the last
time on Saturday night, when ladies wept loyally
in bright-hued evening-dresses and with their hair
done up in the newest style—that is to say, the
style which makes the head at the back look like
a well-grown onion. The hall was dark and the
cinematographic pictures were dark, and the rain
pattered drearily outside while somebody on the
platform described the wanderings of what he
called the “ cauffin ” round various parts of
London. An hour or so of funeral procession is
about the most melancholy entertainment pos-
sible, and it is sad to realise that the people who
tried to brighten the dirge-like music with
applause were rebuked by the man who said
“cauffin.” Later, the Queen of Holland was
married per Cin., and the social atmosphere
oleared somewhat.
* * *
At Sydney Tivoli, Miss Maggie Fraser
does some decent footwork, that occasionally wins
her something like a demonstration. The Clarence
Sisters, two nice little girls who have grown up in
the Tivoli, are now ripe for careful training how
to use their undeniably good arms and feet; at
present, they are rather wooden. Marie Lloyd’s
experiences as idol of the Rose and Crown, where
an admiring coster described her as “a rorty
Eiece of crackling,” are as popular as ever. Her
usband’s sketch, “ The Lambeth Walk,” is clear
proof that Mr. Alec Hurley is not like the usual
star’s husband, who manages for her by collecting
her screw and licking the luggage labels. The
new feature at the House of the Curtain is the
Les Theires family—four clever children, who
do contortion business and bicycle tricks.
** * *
The Monologue Man at Queen’s Hall,
Sydney, is neither remarkably bad nor singularly
good, but the show upon the whole displays con-
siderable staying-power of a kind. It may not be
brilliant, but it is plodding and persevering.
Rodney led off his entertainment by what lie
referred to as some of “that patriotic business”
in a tone that would have drawn bricks from the
audience a few months back. His song, “York,
you’re wanted,” was, if not a courtly, at least a
police-courtly, manner of expressing to the heir of
the Empire this nation’s desire to see him step
forward and show himself. Some of Rodney’s
pantomimic sketches, more especially those of
Eersons swimming, are capitally done, but his
urlescpie of the “Six Hundred” is ancient fun,
and his mock ventriloquism is not only ancient
but poor. Rodney is a hard-working and indus-
trious entertainer, but some entertainers who toil
far less are quite as well appreciated.
*# * *
Miss Bertha Dickens, a Queensland young
lady with a promising soprano voice, will make her
debut in the Centenary Hall, York-street, on
Saturday evening next. Miss Dickens has been
studying with Mr. Bethune, the well-known
singing-master. She will be supported by well-
known artists: Mr. Henry Weir, M. Henri
Staell, Herr Gerard Vollmar, and also by her
sister, a promising violiniste.
A scoffer tells about the recent Wagner
concert at Melbourne Town Hall: —
It was the drollest study in human self-deception. It
started with tremendous applause for Marshall-Hall as
the inflexible German school-master took the platform.
The youngest of the crowd —limp maidens and pale-faced
boys in the incipient moustache stage—roared and
shrieked at their pedant saint, like Salvationists adoring
General Booth. Then they settled down to discover
something beautiful in that dreariest of funeral
items, the Trauermarsch, from “ Gotterdammerung,”
given as a belated reminder of Queen Victoria’s decease.
The overture from “Tannhaiiser” lessened the strain
upon their make-believe, for “ Tannhaiiser’s ” intentions
are melodious as a rule, and when treated by 100 instru-
mentalists (of very various quality) it grows exciting
towards the finish. Besides, Marshall-Hall supplied a Key
to the Overture in 40 lines of small print, as for instance :
—“The wild throng then disperses, and their commotion
ceases ; a voluptuous plaintive whirling alone now stirs
the air, and a horrible murmur now pervades the spot
where the enrapturing magic spell had shown itself, and
which now again is overshadowed by darkness.” The
audience read the conductor’s narrative in a furtive man-
ner, and appeared to take it all for Gospel. Therefore, when
the brass-band had finished blaring, and God and Nature
had ceased to “embrace each other in the holy uniting kiss
of Love” their enthusiasm grew frantic. Next came a
truly agonising experience Wotan’s Asbchied from
“ Die Walkilre.” Charles Tilbury, looking like a puffy
little man in a frock-coat, rather than a German god,
asbehieded in English, or what sounded like English, to
those at the far end of the hall, although his broken
dirge words were printed in German on the programme.
Apart from Tilbury’s dull solo, this fragment of a musical
narrative was a mere piece of orchestration, as far as 49
out of every 50 of the listening ears could possibly under-
stand it. The few accomplished musicians present, other
than bigoted Wagnerites, would willingly admit the
fact. And yet the multitude professed to feel happy
whilst Wagner’s skilfully-manufactured yards of dingy
sound were being uttered to the glory of Cant.
* * * *
“Lady Inger of Ostrat,” for the first time
on the English-speaking stage—so the advt. said,
and it probably spoke truth—came to pass at Her
Majesty’s, Melbourne, last Saturday. Inveterate
theatre-goers, and others not inveterate, who take
an interest in dramatic novelties, supplied a large
first-night audience for this soporific example of
“ Ibsen’s early style.” Up to the time when the
present scribe reached for his hat and made a bee
line for the last train, the patience of that audience
seemed deserving of praise. Ibsen’s early style is
pretty much what might be expected from his
later style. Tediously dramatic, oddly humorless,
and organically diseased, “Lady Inger” was
written before Ibsen had fairly started to re-
construct human nature. In its native Norway
this long-winded tragedy has a historical meaning,
and the names of the characters are familiar to the
ear, and their expletives fit the case; even as
“Marry, come up” and “Grammercy” seem
suitable to an Elizabethan discussion in English
drama. Also, the mummers have the true mea-
sure of their parts, no doubt, and the stage
manager gives the narrative a chance to make
itself intelligible.
* * * *
“ Lady Inger,” as produced on Saturday,
was all words and no sense, for which this scribe
blames the performance rather than the author.
Ibsen’s weighty dialogue was well-translated, one
supposes, and in that case the Nance O’Neil Co.,
or the stage manager, must have maltreated it.
“A definite story” is claimed for “Lady
Inger,” but the plot that dragged its weary
length from act to act on Saturday—five
acts, with very short intervals for yawning—was
blurred and indistinct. The points were not
accentuated. The situation at 11.25 p.m., when
this scribe left Miss Crawford in night gear sitting
upon the knee of a mailed knight in a banquet
hall, was exceedingly cuddlesome but quite un-
accountable. If her mother, Nance O’Neil, came
along to banquet, and caught the giddy girl on
the hug, so to speak, there must have been a hor-
rible explosion at Ostrat Manor. Nance’s part
was adorned with much violent language, includ-
ing a loud, conventional “Jesus Christ!” which
sounded like the cry of a perturbed spirit on
washing-day. Nance was mistaken for a ghost
as she walked into the first act.
* * * *
An air of superfluous mystery pervaded
the performance throughout. M ‘Kee Rankin even
was a seeming spectre until the drama unfolded
the secret of his exuberant identity. He popped
up suddenly in a corridor when Nance was talking
to herself about her ancestors, whereupon she
shrieked “Bleeding Saints!” or words to that
effect, and brought the curtain down. Ibsen, in his
early days, was evidently crude in his objurgations.
Subsequently Rankin evolved himself into a well-
fed conspirator, who lay dormant in the attic or
somewhere for 30 years. He was plotting to throw
off the Danish yoke, as far as one could understand
the purpose of the drama. Kingston impersonated
a Danish agent for the Yoke with a terrible name
among the ladies. There had been some talk of
him having ruined one of Nance’s daughters, and
the sole surviving daughter was undoubtedly
mashing him under questionable circumstances
when this report closed. As to whether Nance
intended to slay Kingston for the good of her dis-
tressful Norway, or was going to relent for the
sake of her illegitimate son Plimmer (if Plimmer
was illegitimate) or show mercy on account of the
erring spinster who sat on the Danish deceiver’s
knee, the present scribe can say nothing. More-
over, he isn’t deeply interested.
* * * *
“Lady Inger,” for the first time on the
English-speaking stage, will not run later than
Friday, if it lasts until then. There are three
big parts, of the most conflicting emotional order,
in Ibsen’s tragedy, and perhaps they were sanely
constructed, but, anyway, the three principal
artists —Nance O’Neil, Kingston and Miss Alice
Crawford —didn’t hold their respective characters
together on Saturday. The last-named damsel
has no pretensions, as yet, to undertake a task
which demands great physical strength and a
thorough experience in stage business. She cannot
even “walk the stage” for romantic tragedy
purposes, and yet she has the dramatic in-
stinct, which the tutored Nance lacks, and her
performance of unsophisticated Mabel Vane m
“ Peg Woffington ” was admirable for its natural-
ness.
* * * *
On Saturday they produce “ Macbeth ” at
Her Majesty’s, and then all the faults of Nance
O’Neil's starring in other tragedies will be for-
given her. “Macbeth” is a matter of scenery,
supers, music and Shakespeare, and it seems
bound to draw good houses for a few weeks in a
city which hasn’t seen King Duncan more than
once during the last 20 years.
“ The World ” of Bland Holt is fulfilling
his fondest expectations at Melb. Royal. “ The
World” belongs to the ancient order of melo-
drama which strove to be consistently improbable
all the way through. Modern Drury Lane plays
have their literary leanings, but B.H.’s latest re-
vival simply revolves on an axis of untruth, and
the gods see that it is good. John Brunton con-
tributes largely to the general effect. His rolling
sea, threatening to slop into the orchestra and
rock the fiddlers on its cold damp bosom, is quite
a masterpiece of deceit, also the conversion of the
dim Pacific Ocean into a bright dry Garden Fete
is cleverly managed. Likewise the lunatic asylum,
where patients get driven mad by special con-
tract, is an impressive scene owing to the
desperate character of Albert Norman’s escape.
Four warders bar the way to Albert’s liberty, and
for some time he can’t do more than overthrow
them in detail, but at last he knocks them all
down at once, and is free to prove his long-lost
identity in the eyes of God and man. The
defeat of those four Boer warders by a gallant
British hero would not have been possible if the
bad young brother and the joyful bill discounter
had taken the precaution to chain their victim to
his bed-post in the refractory ward. That was
where their villainy showed its weakness. They
desired to be moderately merciful, and because
they were infirm of purpose Nemesis overtook
them and virtue triumphed in the end. Painful
to notice how the miscreants of melodrama in-
variably come to grief through neglecting to do
their very worst when the occasion warrants it.
** * *
At Melb. Princess’s, where the sacred
lamp of grand-opera burns unsteadily, and the
“ Lohengrin ” chorus sometimes needs shaking up,
Miss Ella Russell strikes a rather high note of suc-
cess in “IlTrovatore.” The newprima-donnais not
so young and slender as she was. A good deal of
the original color has departed from her respected
voice, and she sings nowadays with more care
than fluency, but she is an Artist who makes a
decided change for the better in “ Trovatore,” and
puts a little new life into “ Faust.” Seeing that
Miss Coomber, the one really sweet and interest-
ing member of Musgrove’s co., has dropped out
of all the bills since she scored a success as
Elsa, The Bulletin feels grateful for Miss
Russell. Also, this journal continues to
approve of tenor Berthald, who makes musi-
cal love to the Wagnerian heroines more
effectively than Louis Arens could make it.
Lempriere Pringle, the new Tasmanian basso
from England, is likewise acceptable on account
of his tall figure and good vocal range, although
his singing hasn’t the roundness and smoothness
of Tilbury’s. To-night (Thursday) “Lohen-
grin” is the bill, to-morrow “Maritana,” with
Madame Slapoffski in the name part, and on
Saturday—but the production of “LaTraviata”
is probably doubtful. On Wednesday (May Ist)
veteran Armes Beaumont, a warrior who has the
scalps of many benefits dangling from his girdle,
will be given a complimentary benefit by the full
strength of the Princess’s forces. _

At the Athenaeum Hall the G. B. Bio-

another week, and when the programme is
changed the Pope will perhaps be a leading fea-
ture on the screen. The G. 8.8. is known to have

the Vatican, and a good many people would be
glad to see the result; also they wonder what tune
the band plays when the spiritual presence is
being reeled off the cylinder.

A Townsville (Q.) correspondent writes :
The Hawtrey Oomedy Co. has been playing in “ The
Message from Mars ” and “ Tom, Dick and Harry ” for a
week and has done fairly good business. You might not
know that we have a brand-new theatre, which, however,
does not meet with Hawtrey’s approval, as you can’t hear
very well in the back seats (the pit). In order to remedy
this defect William had sounding wires installed. The
curtain is a glaring atrocity of advertisements, intimating
where you can get the best boots to drink, and how you
can buy whisky that will require to be re-soled and
heeled after six months wear. “ Mars ” was a little above
the heads of certain Bananalanders. They wanted the
play explained to them, and the serio-comical, imagina-
tive, metaphysical play in which the large grey man
exercises a spell over Horace Parker is too soulful to stand
explanation. Hawtrey was wise in producing “ Mars’.’first
as it might not have survived after “Tom, Dick and Harry,”
which was put up on Wednesday and finished the season
to good houses. The three red-headed, red-bearded,
blue-eyed men who are responsible for all the trouble in
this terrible farce were in the best of form and lived up

The Austral Otis Engineering Co. have now
completed and handed over their contract for the supply
and erection of a bucket dredging plant for the Ovens
Valley Gold Dredging Co., Pore Punkah, Victoria. The
work was done in record time, and the plant started off
and has continued to work without a hitch. Messis.
Richardson and Blair, the engineers for the Ovens Valley
Co., express themselves delighted with the class of
machinery supplied and the expeditious and satisfactory
manner in which the contract has been carried out.*

Most readers of The Bulletin will have heard
that Luhrig Vanners have proved a phenomenal success
on Broken Hill sulphides as also on low grade gold and
copper ores. All Barrier Mines remodelling their mills on
up-to-date lines include a number of them. The South
and Junction companies each have about a dozen, no
slime treatment plant being now considered complete
without these excellent machines. In Queensland tin
concentration is the latest accomplishment, the Irvine-
bank Mill having introduced Luhrig Vanners with success-
ful results, the example having been followed by the well-
known Ooolgarra Tin Mining Co., who are installing a
number of Luhrig Vanners in connection with their new
Tin Dressing Mill. Messrs. W. &J. Lempriere, Melbourne,
are the general agents for the Luhrig Appliances.*

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Royal Bank of Australia (Melbourne) again
shows, in its balance-sheet for the half-year ended 30th
March, 1901, a material improvement. This institution
wrote off £150,000 capital (out of £300,000 paid up) and
£26,521 reserves between 1892 and 1896, and paid no
dividends during the four years 1893,1894,1895, and 1896.
Starting fair at the end of 1896, it has since progressed as
follows:
Total
Profits. Dividends. Reserves.
March, 1897 .. £3398 3 per cent. = £2250 .. £ll4B
September, 1897 .. 3625 3 „ 2250 .. 2523
March, 1898 .. 3865 3 „ = 2250.. 4138
September, 1898 .. 4088 3 „ 2250 .. 5976
March, 1899 .. 4315 3 „ = 2250 .. 8041
September, 1899 .. 4624 3 „ = 2250.. 10,415
March, 1900 .. 5843 4 „ = 3000 .. 13,258
September, 1900 .. 5863 4 ~ = 3000 .. 16,121
March, 1901 .. 6488 5 „ = 3750 .. 18,859
The profits are equal to £7 16s. 3d. per cent, per annum
on the shareholders’ funds as at the beginning of the half-
year (capital £150,000, and reserves £16,121). Under the
circumstances, the further increase of the dividend to 5
per cent, is probably justifiable, though great caution is
still necessary, and no further increase should be thought
of until the profits show a very material further improve-
ment. When the Royal Bank paid its last dividend
before the great cataclysm (in September, 1892), it had
£307,659 of shareholders’ funds (capital, £300,000;
reserve fund, £5OOO ; and undivided profit, £2659). The
shareholders’ funds are now £168,859 (capital, £150,000 ;
reserve fund, £15,000 ; and undivided profit, £3859).
The earnings are now much larger in proportion to the
capital than they were in 1892, and they are almost as
large in the aggregate ; the reserves are more than twice
as strong ; also, much more attention seems to be paid to
the necessity for strengthening the institution. But, all
the same, the shareholders’ funds are £138,800 less than
they were before the year of the great breakage (the
Royal itself didn’t break, but it felt the disaster none the
less), and the need for making good this loss should
always be kept in view.
As compared with previous half-year the Royal
Bank shows a large increase in note and bill circulation,
deposits, advances, cash reserves, reserves of State securi-
ties—in fact, it shows a very material improvement all
round. State and municipal securities (£30,000) repre-
sent much more than the whole reserves, and if this
means that it is intended to keep the reserve fund
separately invested, it is a highly commendable idea.
Total of cash and liquid securities is £2os,s6o—equal to
over 36 per cent, on all direct liabilities to the public.
Affairs are looking remarkably well with this little bank,
as with a good many other banks just now ; and if only
sufficient preparation is made for the next lean year—for
the next repetition of 1893 —there is no reason why its
prosperity should not be permanent. The same remark
applies to a good many other banks.
• * * •
The Bank of Adelaide, whose returns are con-
sistently good, shows an unexpected outbreak of extra
prosperity for the year which closed on 25th March, 1901
—why any bank’s accounts should close on the 25th of a
month is mysterious. The year’s profits and dividend
compare with previous returns after this fashion :
Total
Profits. Dividends. Reserves.
March, 1892 .. £31,116 7 per cent. = £28,000 £155,501
„ 1893 .. 30,976 7 „ = 28,000 158,477
„ 1894 .. 30,763 7 „ = 28,000 161,240
„ 1895 .. 31,049 7 „ 28,000 164,289
„ 1896 .. 31,225 7 „ 28,000 167,514
„ 1897 .. 31,047 7 „ 28,000 170,561
~ 1898 .. 33,401 7 „ = 28,000 175,962
„ 1899 .. 33,026 7 „ = 28,000 180,988
„ 1900 .. 38,413 8 32,000 187,401
„ 1901 .. 48,097 8 32,000 203,498
Profits are equivalent to £8 3s. 9d. per cent, per annum
on the shareholders’ funds as at the beginning of the half-
year (paid-up capital £400,000; reserves £187,401); which
is a very handsome figure and much above the usual level.
The Bank of Adelaide didn’t suspend in 1893—in fact it
went on paying its regular dividend and increasing its
reserves, and didn’t turn a hair in the great downfall.
It has liquid securities (of which £400,021 are State
securities) amounting to £1,187,568, which is equivalent
to nearly 53 per cent, on all liabilities to the public, cash
and State debentures alone representing 33 per cent.
This is a record such as hardly any other financial insti-
tution in Australasia can equal, and in its small way the
Bank of Adelaide certainly looks something far beyond
the average in the way of solidity. The policy of keep-
ing such a very substantial second reserve of State
securities is especially commendable ; not only is the
whole reserve fund thus invested, but nearly half the
capital as well.

The Colonial Bank (Melbourne), for the half-
year to 31st March, 1901, shows a further expansion of
profits; and raises the dividend on preference shares to 5
per cent. The Colonial Bank reconstructed in 1893, and
agreed to pay 4) per cent, interest on its extended
deposits. In March, 1895, interest was reduced to 2j per
cent., and after March, 1898, things so far improved, that
the rate was raised to 3 per cent., at which figure it still
remains. In 1899, dividends began to be paid on prefer-
ence shares. The record during the 3 per cent, interest
period has been as follows: —

.fronts. Dividends, &c. Reserves.

The reserves are certainly increasing with some rapidity,
and the profits are equivalent to £6 6s. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ funds as at the beginning of
the half-year (preference capital, £304,044; ordinary
capital, £116,876 ; reserve fund, £15,000 ; and undivided
profit, £2946). The ordinary shareholders’ capital is
now such a mere shadow (this half-year it stands at
£126,497) that it would only require £10,764 to pay a 5
per cent, dividend to ordinary and preference share-
holders alike, and as the earnings would cover this and
leave £2954 over it i 3 probable that the ordinary people
will shortly receive some reward for their long waiting.
They have lost their old reserve fund of £287,500 ; their
original capital of £406,250 ; about £170,000 (the writer
speaks from memory) of new capital which they paid
up ; about seven years’ dividends ; the greater part
of their interest in the bank (which now belongs
mainly to the new preference shareholders);
and the £126,497 of additional new capital which
they have put up and which is now intact
has, as yet, brought them no return whatever. Certainly
the old shareholders of the Colonial Bank have had a bad
time. And they never got any very large dividends in tne
good days as a set-off against these later losses.
If the management adheres to its present cautious
policy, there is no apparent reason why the Oo
should not become a really strong bank. It never
very strong one before 1893 ; the capital of £406,
reserve of £287,500 were nothing remarkable for an affair
of its size ; but a continuance of the existing system
might make it, inside another 20 years, something
far more substantial than it ever was. During .
half-year another £240,000 or so of reconstructed deposits
have been paid, and only £123,991 remain to •
Long before the end of 1901 the Colonial should
to clear off this balance. Despite this large repayment
deposits are about £llO,OOO more than they were six
months ago. There is a reasonably solid cash reserve o
£485,526 and a very weak second line of defence in the
shape of £13,920 worth of State and municipal securities.
The reserve fund isn’t separately invested in State
securities, but, to a great extent, mixed up in the gene
business. Taking one thing with another, however, t
Colonial Bank looks better than it has done at any time
since 1893, and certainly no sacrifices have been spared in
wiping off the loss made in that memorable disaster.

amount. How long it will be before further disaster
overtakes this surplus is another question. The assets
are valued at 110,530, but £6028 consists of an “interest
under will at book value ” which apparently yields
nothing at present, and some £2500 of property only pro-
duced last year £l2. The other £2 of income was
“ commission.” And the directors who supervised the
earning of this £l4 got £5O in fees for doing it.
* * * *
The season fights for the B.H. Water Supply
00., lately in great danger of Govt, opposition—the
matter is, in fact, still before the Public Works Committee.
From 25 million gallons a month the consumption has
gone down to nine millions, the local rains having
knocked kite-high most of the figures furnished to the
P. W. Committee, which were calculated on the then-ex-
isting drought-basis. Same committee has lately decided
that the far more awful monopoly of the Silverton Tram
00. (dividends = 45 per cent, per annum) ought not to be
disturbed. All Broken Hill’s anti-monopoly schemes, in
fact, seem to be going wrong, and the moral of it all is—
don’t embrace the syndicate unless you are satisfied to
have the syndicate always with you.

The periodic gold-robbery happened on the
German liner Kaiser Wilhelm der Grosse this time, some-
where between New York and Cherbourg. The cracks-
men got away with £12,000, which is a record swag since
the gold-lifting began in ’77. Previous to this, £5OOO was
the usual sum, but apparently the thieves have improved
their transport and are going in for a small fortune at a
stroke. Since cabled, however, that a steward found the
cash or most of it behind the cornice in the second
saloon. Mail-steamer gold-robberies will no doubt con-
tinue, till, as advocated in this paper when £5OOO
mysteriously took unto itself legs and quietly walked off
the Alameda, the treasure is carried in the most public
place in the ship—the saloon—in two or three steel cages
one within the other, with brilliant electric light playing
through the bars by which the gold-prison is surrounded.
By the way, the Kaiser Wilhelm gold has been found-
hidden in the second saloon.

Editor Bulletin. — The recent case of bullion-
snatching ex N.D.L. s.s. Friedrich der Grosse again calls
attention to the injustice of making the ship’s officers re-
sponsible for safe delivery. Instead of shipping the trea-
sure at night with the utmost secrecy, a dapper bank-
clerk drives up to the wharf in broad daylight and full
view of all loafers, and with much show of key-
production the precious freight is locked away out of
sight. Then as if this publicity was not enough, the
press on the following day announces the amount
of the shipment. In the case of Australian shipments
made in Melb. and Sydney, intending snatchers have
(supposing no previous knowledge existed) two to four
days respectively in which to catch the vessel carry-
ing the “ splosh.” Owners and agents are always ready
to ship these consignments on account of a freight
amounting in some instances to 1 per cent., but, should
delivery not be made, the unlucky skipper bears the
blame, and the “sack” usually follows. What with
running to time-tables, and responsibility for lives and
vessel, the shipmaster of to-day has quite enough on his
mind without adding responsibility for treasure-ship-
ments. Again, of all the places to bestow treasure, the
usual ship’s specie-room is about the worst. Buried down
the after ’tween decks, out of sight of everybody for
hours at a stretch, it’s no wonder locks are picked. Why
not, as a Bulletin correspondent once suggested, build a
strong iron cage on deck, or put it even on the upper
bridge, in full and constant view of the officer of the watch?
As things are at present, the wonder is not that the stuff
occasionally gets “ nicked,” hut that it so frequently
escapes. Skippers would at all events be thankful for
the stoppage of the newspaper announcements about
shipments of specie and bullion.—H.B.T. [May be some-
thing in it; though the artists who do these things have
carefully-arranged sources of information—outside any
press announcement or publicity of shipment. ]
* * * *
“Esmi” : Be bank-clerks’ salaries (B. 13/4/’01).
The Dec. previous to closing its doors in the Great Smash,
one of the reconstructed banks granted close on £lOOO in
increases of salary to its manager, inspector of branches,
accountant, and sub-accountant. Not one penny increase
was given to anyone else in the service. Deputationised,
the manager blandly explained that the claims of the staff
would be considered at the end of the following half-year.
In the interval, the bank went bung, and no increases
were granted up to the time I left the institution. This
bank prided itself on its unparalleled generosity in not
reducing salaries when some kindred institutions did ;
hut, seeing that such a proceeding meant relinquishment
by the bosses of their newly-acquired rises, their large-
souled benevolence may reasonably be doubted.

E.F. : Considering that hundreds of shopkeepers
don’t issue coupons, that deputation to Vic. State Premier
denouncing the system would naturally be large, more
especially if each active protestor brought his family with
him. Perhaps The Bulletin, which disapproves of
coupons, will kindly let me remark, in a soft English
whisper, that shopkeepers seem to prosper by devouring
their competitors. The coupon-discount allowance to
customers is a fair square deal. Likewise the counter-
lunch at pubs., and any other candid policy of beggar-
your-neighbor. Instead of encouraging certain shop-
keepers to howl against the coupon system, why not
start a crusade against excessive gas-jets and expensive
advts., whereby Jones flares and fibs Brown into the
Bankruptcy Court?
* * * •
Broken Hill milling plants, the best of them,
have not been saving more than 70 per cent, of the lead
and 35 per cent of the silver ; and some of them have not
saved more than 55 per cent, of the lead. The rest of
these metals, with about 92 per cent, of the zinc, ha 3 gone
into the tailings and slimes. So long as zinc sold at £2O
or more most attention was given to making some use of
this metal, though without much practical success.
Lately, when the slump in zinc and the raising of ocean
freights put an end to the zinc experiments for a time,
attention was turned to getting out some of the wasted
silver and lead from the tailings; and nearly every
B.H. mine is putting in “tables” (of about a dozen
different patterns) and vanners for this purpose. These,
in the jargon of the newspaper report, constitute the re-
treatment plants. Some of the poorer mines are basing
their future almost wholly upon these mill-extensions —
the Junction, for instance. Tailings which have been
re-treated at this mine (its re-treating plant has been
started, though the main works are still idle) retain only
4 per cent, lead, 2oz. silver, and 4 per cent. zinc. This re-
treating is a slow process, though not very expensive ; as
a rule, no further crushing is required, and it is the
crushing that costs the money. Block 14, Block 10, the
South, and the North are working in the same direction.
* * * •
One explanation given of the prolonged slump
in lead—the price keeps well under £l3, as against £lB
during part of last year—is that some Spanish mines
are held under lease from the Govt., and that as the
leases are about to expire the output is being increased,
and other steps taken to depress the market in order that
the royalty, or whatever it is that the Govt, gets, may be
reduced. Writer doesn’t know anything about this.
Statistics, however, show that the output for Spanish
mines in the first half of 1900 (later are not available) was
lower and not higher than it had been. That the lead
market is easily knocked out has been proved many
times; not later than towards the end of last year the
market was beared as soon as Block 10, the third biggest
producer at B. Hill, invited tenders for its output for 12
months or more. Meantime, as there seems no likelihood
of an early rise much beyond £l3, work at the B.H. mines
is being remodelled. In addition to the re-treatment plants
which are being put in most of the co.’s are taking
men off the poorer ore-faces ; and some of them —Block
14 especially—are sorting the ore before hauling it. Block
14 can thus keep its mill going only two shifts instead of
three. The South, whose recent developments have been
good, has fortunately a large number of good ore-faces
available, so that it is maintaining an output of ore worth
22£ per cent, lead and 10 or lloz. silver. Though the
profits of the B.H. Prop., Block 10, and the Sulphide Cor-
poration are most seriously affected, these mines are not
troubled as the others are. Of the two mines that are
shut down, or nearly so—the Junction and Junction
North—the former, helped by its small output of first-
class stuff and its new plant, may be able to make ends
meet ; but, on its past record, the Junction North (still
in the labors of reconstruction) seems quite hopeless until
either the markets move or somebody finds out more
about sulphide-treatment than is known so far.
* * * *
B.H. Prop, has the masonry work of its coke-
ovens at Bellambi finished, and is hurrying on this and
other schemes designed to reduce costs.
* • * *
White Cliffs opal miners have got the all-round
decrease in tribute for which they have been battling;
instead of 25 per cent, they are now to pay the co. which
owns most of the best blocks 15 per cent, of gross receipts.
Not that this fully satisfies them. The fact that the field
can only be worked by entirely independent tribute
parties of two or three men, proves that it ought never to
have been leased—it ought to have been treated just like
an alluvial goldfield. And so the men will not be satisfied
until the leases are cancelled, whether with or without
compensation is locally the most-debated point.
* * * *
N.S.W. coal estimates : Twenty-three years
ago, by the late O. S. Wilkinson, Govt. Geologist : Ex-
cluding seams under 2£ft. thick, and allowing one-fifth
for loss in working, 78,198,000,000 tons within 4000 ft. from
surface. Ten years ago, by Prof. David : Including only
the unworked areas of the Palaeozoic fields, and excluding
seams under 3ft. thick, between 130,000,000,000 and
150,000,000,000 tons within 4000 ft. from surface (the coal-
measures west of Gunnedah are Triassic). Present, by
Govt. Geologist Pitman : Assuming a thickness of only
10ft., deducting one-third for losses, 115,346,880,000 tons.

“ L. J.F.” : There is nothing the matter with

* * * *
“ S.S.” : The trouble over the appointment of
a searcher at the Clarence United mine, Eaglehawk, is
only a repetition of an old grievance that has cropped up
on Vic. fields a thousand times since the searching of
miners was first introduced at Ballarat about 30 years
ago. As a precaution against gold-stealing it has not
been found worth the trouble. To be effective, it would
have to he as thorough as the searching of niggers in
African diamond-mines, and if that plan were attempted
Australian miners would probably start hanging directors
to gum-trees. In some cases the establishment of a
searcher only led gold-stealers to adopt cuter strategies,
in others it meant squaring the searcher and stealing
with impunity. At mines I have worked in the searching
was a mere perfunctory pawing of the men’s working
clothes in the dressing-room by a sullen who
needed all his faculties to dodge the vicious practical
jokes of which he was the standing victim, and meanwhile
those who wished to get away with shotty, waterworn
gold could do so with little or no trouble. It was in this
district that a cute “ K.G.” (Cousin Jack) lifted much
alluvial gold with the assistance of an imitation wen
attached to his head behind his ear. The real gold-stealer
never kicks up a row about the searcher; he has no par-
ticular sensibilities, and the searcher doesn’t interfere
with his pickings and stealings

Westralia has a Sunday Labor and Mines Act.
But when the other day the Workers’ Union, at Kal-
goorlie, drew attention of the Mines Department to many
local infringements, it was staggered by the reply that,
“ while the Minister regrets to hear that the provisions
of this Act are not being complied with, it does not ap-
pear that it was the intention of the Legislature that this
dept, should he responsible for seeing that the
provisions are carried out 1 ” And the dept, pro-
ceeds :—“ Indeed, as the inspector of mines would in all
probability be called upon as a witness in any prosecu-
tions, it seems undesirable that he should take the initial
proceedings.” On the same principle, of course, no
policeman should arrest any drunk, because he probably
would be a witness ; the policeman should always, there-
fore, wait for somebody to arrest the drunk. without
seeing him.

Minister Lyne, evidently with The Bul-
letin’s remarks on the Fegan scandal under his
eye, makes brave professions—
“ I am determined,” said Mr. Lyne, “to dissociate the
Federal service from the time-serving indifference which
has been so characteristic of the State services.”
And how does he begin? By appointing an
indifferent time-server, name o’ Fegan, to a first-
class billet. What an incitement to time-servers ?
What an excuse for indifference among those who
realise that no zeal will serve you if you are not a
Minister’s friend !
It is insufficient, he holds, to pompously declare that
there must be no political influence, and that intelligence
and attention to duty will lead to promotion.
Well, The Bulletin is not a Minister ; it can
only talk, and point out the right principles to
follow. It remains for Minister Lyne to act —on
the wrong principle of Fegan.
Federal Ministers thoroughly recognise the fact that
political patronage and promotions on a basis of seniority
alone must be firmly shut-down upon. William Lyne
goes further. He desires to organise a Federal service
that will be free from the many obvious blemishes of the
State services. He recognises that there are many good
officers in the departments taken over, and that they
should be given an opportunity of showing their abilities,
hut he is far from believing that all the organising and
administrative capacity and intelligence ip Australia are
monopolised by the civil service.
The Bulletin is glad to find Minister Lyne’s
voice endorsing its own expressed sentiments.
But, unluckily, while Minister Lyne’s voice is the
voice of honest Esau, his hands are the hands of
crooked Jacob appointing Fegan.

The presence of the plague has caused
Duke George to abandon any visit to S’Africa.
Which sets one thinking how the insidious ad-
vance of the disease in Perth (W.A.)at one end of
this continent and at Brisbane at the other
will strike the Roval visitor and the authorities
who control his movements. The Duke's em-
phatic instructions that every stoker on board the
Ophir should be vaccinated caused serious trouble
m Portsmouth. And if he dreads small-pox, how
will he regard the plague ?

The coming visit of the Yorks has had one
good effect, anyhow'. Not long ago Melbourne’s
handsome Yarra Boulevard was at a standstill
and, apparently, likely to remain an eyesore for
about a generation. Now all is in apple-pie order.
Flower and shrub-covered rockeries border the
drives and approaches, with green plots planted
about here and there, and seats under healthy
young trees. The old swamp above the bridge
has been turned into a trim lawn, the factories on
the West side are all hidden, and even the boat-
houses are be-flowered and painted.
* « * *
Melbourne is not “ doing it handsomely ”
for its inter-State visitors as Svdney did.
All but a fraction of the visitors have
to hustle for themselves, and accommo-
dation in the leading hotels has gone to
as high as £2 2s. a day. Melbourne lias the bene-
fit of Sydney’s experience in this respect, and
recognises that, no matter what is done for people,
they always consider that more should have been
done. An important notability from one of
the States (not Leg. Council President
Zeal), visiting Sydney in January, lived
like a fighting-cock, at the public expense,
for about a fortnight. When his visit was
over he forwarded to the Treasury a claim for
every penny he had squandered in cabs and tram-
tares during his stay, and was much annoved when
payment was refused.

“Some country medicos evade
their responsponsibilities in a curious
way,” writes an embittered back-
blocks correspondent. “ When they
have a serious case, where the pay is
not very certain, and it is inconvenient
to send the patient to hospital, they
wire to Sydney for a trained nurse to
come up at once. Nurse arrives to
find the case (typhoid) in a two-roomed
hut occupied by the rest of the family ;
no convenience of any sort; and most
meagre instructions from the doctor,
except to follow the usual course till
he comes in 10 days or so. The case
may last a month or six weeks, and the
nurse has to put up with all the respon-
sibility lone-handed ; do all the wash-
ing ; live on salt beef and damper (or
thereabouts); and in the end if she gets
her railway fare she thinks herself
lucky. Yet doctors consider they are
often sweated!

Dear Bulletin. —Why isn’t a
little patch for a public burial-ground
left in the country districts ; or if there
is, why is it not made use of ? Here in
Bangalow (N.S.W.) is a privately-
owned R.C. cemetery with about half-a-
dozen graves in it. The other day an
old man died, but being a Lutheran and
poor, of course he couldn't go in with
the rest, so—not to handicap him too
much, I suppose—they dug a hole out-
side the fence on the public road and
covered him up without ceremony or
service. We still missionise the hea-
then, you know.—A.B.
Another death under chloroform. This
time it was the result of a throat operation in a
Sydney private hospital of high repute. The iden-
tity of the eminent medical practitioners concerned
in the accident, as well as the precise locale of the
hospital, was considerately suppressed by the
daily papers —which presumably mean to hence-
forth deal with every chloroform fatality in the
same kind way.

“A boom in feminine smoking is spread-
ing in the West End,” writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent—“all owing to the ex-
ample of Royalty. The late Queen abhorred the
smell of tobacco, and many a laughable story is
told of the devices attempted by her guests to
interview ‘Lady Nicotine ’ ‘unbeknownst’to their
hostess. By a reflex action. Queen Alexandra is
heavy smoker —for a woman. She is credited
with 10 or 12 cigarettes daily, and I have good
authority for saying that her Majesty never visits
the theatre without enjoying a cigarette between
the acts ; not, of course, in the Royal box, but in
the ante-room which is always set apart for the
regal visitor. The Princess Christian and the
Duchess of Saxe-Coburg are her sister-devotees.
What a shock to the popular impression of the
Queen’s rigid propriety ! ”

away.

* » * *
Under ail Act passed in Maoriknd last
session every town, district, or borough having a
population of 2000 or more persons, is compelled to
erect public abattoirs within a year after March
31st. The Act also encourages other local bodies
to do likewise, and its evident purpose is to reduce
the number of private slaughter-houses to the
lowest practicable minimum. Despite its excel-
lent climate tubercular diseases claim by far the
largest share of victims in Maoriland, and it is
rightly presumed that carelessness in the slaughter
and disposal of meat is largely the cause. Hence
the legislation.
A number of the Brunswick (Melb.) bacon-
curing and pig-slaughtering establishments con-
demned by the Board of Public Health are bring-
ing political influence to bear on the Vic. Govern-
ment to over-ride the decision of the Board. The
Brunswick Council has been telling the Minister
that it sees no reason for closing up these filthy
disease-beds. The Council argues, with typical
stupidity, that if the places were as bad as the
Board of Health made out, the Council’s health-
officer would have reported the matter. They
don’t say that one of the health-officers of another
council sometime ago informed the Board that if
he wanted to keep nis billet lie dare not condemn
places he knew were spreading corruption through-
out the city. Health matters should be taken
right out of the hands of councils, many of the
members of which are interested in piggeries and
slaughter-houses. Dr. Gresswell, the chairman
of the Health Board, was present when the Bruns-
wick councillors tried to hustle the Minister, and
he remarked that some of the premises in question
were so utterly filthy that he could not under-
stand any councillors defending them. If Dr.
Gresswell knew a little more about councillors lie
would be astounded if they did not defend them.
* » * »
Sydney is threatened with the abolition of
the hotel counter-lunch. Hard on the swell who
invests fid. on a pint of ale and a couple of
shillings’ worth of “tucker” ; but it will not be
an unmixed evil. The archaic boiled horse and
the alleged German sausage will disappear; and
the 3d. hash-emporiums will come to the fore.
Seriously, the London system, whereunder you
get at any public-house counter neither more
nor less than what you pay for, and where you have
a large assortment of light refreshments to choose
from, would be infinitely better for the hotel trade
and the self-respect of the public than present
Sydney methods. Perhaps someone will try it
presently.

Areally “sudden” occurrence happened
in Melbourne lately. A young woman, wishing
to dispose of the customary undesirable in-
fant, took tram to a distant suburb
immediately after her confinement, left
the just-born baby (of course it died)
on a vacant allotment, and went
home to continue washing the
breakfast-things and getting the
dinner ready. The newly-born
was promptly discovered and
detectives laid on the trail.
A shopkeeper’s name on some
wrappings gave a clue, and
on calling at the correct
house the damsel herself
opened the door. She was
hardly more surprised than
the policemen, who were
so demoralised by the
rapidity of the whole pro-
ceeding that they took
her to the Women’s Hos-
pital instead of to the
lock-up. Also, a day or
two later, a casual sort of
girl was out with her young
man for an evening stroll,
in Castlemaine, when she
complained of illness
and sent the youth
for help. On his
return she carelessly
remarked that he
needn’t worry, “as
she was all right
now.” She had been
confined in the in-
terim, the youngster
being afterwards
found, quite naked,
in a drain near by.
She, too, proceeded home
and got the family supper
as usual, an accouchement being
evidently no more of an
“event” than breaking*
a plate or killing a fly.

tary band when return-
ing from a funeral: “’E
duuno where ’e are ! ”
Noticeable how unanimously and fre-
quently the Melb. dailies refer to “that dis-
tinguished rascal” since C. J. Madden thus
characterised lawyer Grey (who has now been
brought to Australia from Fiji, where he
was captured). But they said precious little
about the gentleman till a private victim
forced the law into action. And now they are
doing their best to render Grey’s chance of a fair
trial a trifle worse than “Buckley’s.” By the
way, Vic Crown Law authorities, who for two
years carefully ignored the alleged swindles of
lawyer Grey—of Geelong—the other day went to
great expense in prosecuting an old-age pensioner
at Prahran for alleged misrepresentation. The
case fell through ; but it came out in evidence that
the ancient’s pension had already been cancelled !
The law is severe upon “ false pretences ” exactly
in proportion to the degree to which poverty
might be pleaded as an excuse.
Commentary on the Salvarmy’s treatment
of those whom it “ rescues ” by a young woman at
Fitzroy (Melb.) Court : Relegated to a Rescue
Home previously, she had run away on account of
the too-copious hymn and over-frequent prayer.
Charged with having neither billet nor private
income, she was quite willing to go to service with
civilised people; but the beak was shocked at her in-
gratitude and sent her to gaol for threemonths right
off. If the performing animal known as the Jay
Pee is allowed to fill Melb. gaols with people like
this, there will be no room for the crowds of mags-
men and spielers who are to accompany the Jook.

One of those old women so shamelessly
and frequently gaoled in Victoria, during the last
few years, for the crime of poverty (and deprived
of pension rights) turned up at Melb. City Court
t’other day, on the old charge of “no lawful
means.” There is plenty room now at the
Benevolent Asylum ; but the P.M. put her away
for six months “as a kindness.” “ Blessed are
the merciful! ”
* • * *
The Law in Victoria has hitched up its
pants, tightened its belt, and started after a missing
delinquent at a forty-knot rate. The man in
question was to have married his girl last January,
but melted away a few days “ previous.” Later
eventuated the customary baby—only obis time it
was twins—and as the poor mother died the State
became compulsory foster-mother. Just here
arise several nice points. The absentee—when
he becomes a presentee —can’t be charged with
deserting his children, because when he started
migrating there weren’t any youngsters to desert;
also he can’t be sued for maintenance, because
the infants have no certain knowledge as to
their parentage, and anyhow they as yet don’t
understand the nature of an oath. Altogether,
the Law seems to have taken on a tough contract,
but it is pleasant to see it hustle itself sometimes
—even if the quarry doesn’t happen to be a de-
faulting Gent One or an absconding trustee.

A desire at present simmering in the

Woman reformers is to create a crusade against
the State running smoking-carriages.
The Greek Archbishop of Jerusalem has
started for Australia. Branches of his church
now exist in several Australian capitals, and he
desires to establish them in due form. As regards
garments and entourage he will fill society’s eye.
Archimandrites and other solemn dignitaries will
accompany him, and gold and silver vestments
will make a dazzling spectacle.
• * • *
Melbourne Argus, railing against the
rascally unemployed for refusing work, quotes an
instance afforded by Andrew Rowan. This
wealthy vigneron wanted 20 men for grape-pick-
ing, and out of 150 at the Labor Bureau only eight
were so pressed by poverty that they accepted the
wages offered—nine whole shillings a week and
found. Making the extremely-liberal allowance
of 15s. (Bs. would be nearer the mark) for “found,”
this works out at 4s. a day for about 12 hours’
work. And Eatman’s organ abuses these
dainty unemployed for declining to try and keep
a wife and family on 9s. a week !
* » * *
Callousness and meanness marked the
attitude of the authorities in regard to the recent
disaster to the s.s. Federal. When news of
the wreck reached Melbourne everyone was
too busy preparing paper arches and
tawdry decorations for the reception of the
Yorks to think about the unfortunates who
might be battling with the sea in little boats, or
perishing on the barren beach of Gippsland. A
few policeman stationed a hundred miles apart
were asked to search along the coast, although it
is well-known by the police and harbor authorities
that none of them could get within scores of miles
of where the men would be likely to come ashore.
The facts which have turned up lately go to show
conclusively that some of the men did reach
shore alive, and that others may have been
tossing about for days before they were swallowed
up.

“Brew” (a surgeon) on a recent Sydney
daily-newspaper paragraph :
Yet another “ cancer cure ” —the list stretches out
towards infinity 1 This time it is the “ sun-glass treat-
ment” of a certain Dr. H. T. Webster, who claims to be
able to destroy cancerous growth by “ the concentrated
rays of the sun.” In other words, a burning-glass is
focussed upon the growth, and a somewhat milder edition
of the old cauterisation treatment (long discarded as
actually injurious) is placarded forth under a new name
to attract the wretched victims of a horrible disease to
yield up their last coppers in the futile hope of cure. Not
only is the treatment useless from a curative or amelio-
rating point of view, but it is actively injurious. Every
surgeon knows that irritation produces rapid growth in
such tumours, and it does not make any difference
whether such irritation comes from caustic dressings, the
knife, or the latest “ sun cure.” An unsuccessful opera-
tion for removal is almost invariably followed by enor-
mously rapid increase of growth, and the repeated burn-
ings would produce a similar effect °" an £T. "
ing malignant tumour. Small growths of a
nature might possibly disappear under such treatment,
but at present the only hope for the victims of mal b
growths lies in free excision by the knife.

Concerning dogs in Sydney :
Dear 8.,-You say (29/12/’.00): “What is wanted in
Sydney is a still stricter administration of the Dog Act.
Right. At present it isn’t administered at all. lhe ac®
requires every C.P.S., under a penalty, to keep a list or
dogs registered in his district hanging in his office tor
public reference, so that anyone annoyed by his neigh-
bor’s cur can see for himself whether the brute is regis-
tered. Yet not a O.P.S. in or about Sydney keeps such a
list, failing which the police have to go round once a year
from door to door asking to be shown registration re-
ceipts If the list were kept, the man on the beat, when
he finds a dog or dogs are kept on certain premises, could
see at once by the list whether or not they were regis-
tered The fact is, the Act should be strictly adminis-
tered' all the year round, and not merely for a week or
two in Nov. or Dec., and the officials of all grades should
be made do their duty, or be fined as the law directs.
The Sydney street dog is indeed a curse ; but a yearly
fortnight of police hard swearing will neither abate it nor
make people register their dogs, which are now turned
into the street when the raid begins and taken back when
it is over, thus being safe for another year. It s not
the administration of the Dog Act, but its non-adminis
tration, that’s the cause of the trouble.—D.G.

“F.R.” : In a down-South train the other
day a pompous doctor concluded an eloquent
address to a young man on the evils of smoking
with the remark —“ And, mark this, lam a medi-
cal man, and I say that in nine cases out of ten
you will find that the diseases of smokers are due
to the use of tobacco.” Removing his briar from
his mouth the young man replied : “And 1 think
you will also find, doctor, when you see a man
with a black eye, that in nine cases out of ten it is
due to not minding his own business.” a *\ en
there was a deep silence till somebody asked for
the loan of a match.
* *
South Melbourne, Albert Park,
South Yarra, and St. Kilda are being
haunted by an eccentric with long fair
hair, a roving eye, and a winning smile,
who has developed an original kind of
craziness. The crank’s clothes are old,
but in excellent repair, and he displays
the gallantry of a beau of ye olden time.
He knocks casually at the front door of
a house—any house, humble or palatial
—and, if a woman answers his sum-
mons, he throws his whole soul into a
profound bow and a smile of beatitude
and admiration, and incontinently offers
her his hand and heart, proposing an
early marriage, and a honeymoon trip
to Atlantis or somewhere therearound.
If a male answers the door, he politely
asks to be introduced to the lady of the
house, and he makes his honorable pro-
posal in the presence of a husband with
the airy complacency of an agent who
is trying to sell a sewing-machine or
persuade the housewife to have her
photo, enlarged. If the poor devil is
kicked out, as sometimes happens, he
quietly gathers himself out of the dust,
carefully rearranges his toilet, and tries
next door. Really, the gentle maniac
is a boon and a blessing to many spin-
sters in the suburb mentioned, whose
previous prospects were much out of
date, and the real wonder is that the
cracked knight - errant has not been
snapped-up by some neglected virgin.

A CLEAN SWEEP.
“To shut off the rather unpleasant view of the
west side of the St. Kilda Road, the City Corpora-
tion has erected a bank and planted it with
various shrubs, plants, etc., the whole being sur-
mounted by a tasteful green fence. These im-
provements have been accompanied by the raising
of the tram-track.” —Daily Paper.
The St. Kilda Road is handsome with the mansions of the
rich,
And its flower-banks in their towering eminence ;
And they’re so proud of the d n track
Now they’ve gone and raised the tram-track,
All unpleasant views they’ve shut out with a fence ;
Just a simple little garden paling fence.
Since a Royal Duke is coming and his Royal Duchess too,
We must have a show regardless of expense ;
So the Fatman and the Sweater will both wish for nothing
better
Than to shut off all their factories with a fence:
Hide their overworked employes with a fence.
Happy thought ! They’ve found the secret—how to hide
their dirty clothes,
Lest they give His Royal Highness some offence
Let them stow away by sections the result of their
elections,
Hiding Fraser and M'Eacharn with a fence.
Putting Knox and Sargood safe behind a fence.
In New South Wales, at any rate, they’ve never need to
hide
One man whose deeds are always Future Tense,
George Reid, although a sly dog, too long has nursed his
Dry Dog,
He is pretty safely hidden on the fence
He is pretty well forgotten on his fence.
When the Jook goes off to Queensland
There they’ll be no whit behind
In showing everything—but common sense ;
But the Public men who’re blacker than JFEacharn’s
loved Kanaka,
Can they shield them with a greenish-tinted fence ?
Put them safe behind a badly needed fence.
One last word—it may be useful, just to show the Royal
way,
If the princeling with those knighthoods would commence,
Then each M.L.C. who slumbers, and political back
numbers
Might be Sirred and safely put behind the fence ;
For the country’s sake might sleep behind the fence.
M. M.
