A WOMANS LETTER.
Sydney, April 23, 1901.
My Dear Arini,—
Nine-tenths of the Sydneysiders asked to
Melbourne to help worry the Yorks at functions
are wondering as to the why and wherefore of
their inviteß. Some of them never got asked to
anything bigger than a church social before and
some never went to anything so respectable. The
Citizens’ Committee, here in Sydney, has been
asked by Govt, to help decorate the streets.
Why can’t Govt, and a committee let the Duke
see our streets? Why disguise them in drapery
(and champagne)? C.C. has now £l3OO in hand,
and has allotted seats for the State concert.
I have seen the album to be presented to the
Yorks (“amidst intense silence and strained
attention ”), and am now wearing smoked glasses
in consequence. The dazzling thing’s outside is of
solid gold 12in. x 18in. Running from end to end
and side to side is the Southern Cross in blue
enamel. Kangaroos and emus embellish the bind-
ing. Inside are views of Australia, and the cost
of the whole is 400 guineas.
Coonong Station, where the Duke will shoot,
belongs to one of the biggest wool-kings in the
world. He, M‘Caughey, is also a big man with a
big moustache, and his sheep are of a big breed,
and he’s in the big Upper House. That part of
the Murrumbidgee land on the edge of the salt-
bush country grows everything on a big scale. One
thing the Duke will have, and that’s range. The
country is as flat as a tennis-court. What will
he shoot ? Rabbits, hares, and wild birds on the
lagoons, probably. It will be very tame. What a
good thing no one thought of National Park, or his
Mildness might have been taken there to kill deer.
I think I can hear some of the Coonong party
talking to the Duke. “ Your ’lghness, I went over
this country in the old lonely coaching days,
when I’ve been the only passenger, and I’ve been
that lonely that I’ve talked to an imaginary per-
son on the opposite seat, then changed to that side
and replied to myself.” “Is it possible,” says the
Duke, feeling for his return ticket “Yes, your
’lghness, and when I got to an hotel and got to
bed, I had to spring out with a yell and my hair
on end. Up comes the landlady very apologetic,
‘I quite forgot,’ she says, ‘that I put that hen
and clutch of eggs to set in that bed. There, I’ve
taken her out, Mister, you get in again.’ “ Hope-
toun,” says the Duke, “ where have you put the
flask?”
The Duke of York is a sufferer from the brain
trouble known as philately. It impels you to
collect stamps and paste them in books, and you
dream and talk and think stamps. Sydney
Philatelic Asylum—Club I mean—is in a fine
frenzy over the Duke’s “ gracious condescension ”
in becoming a patron of the institution. Members
are going to signify their gratitude by an address,
and will lay their rarest stamps at the ducal feet.
Gentlemen presenting the address will each wear
a rare stamp pasted on the forehead.
Concerning the invitation cards to be issued by
the Commonwealth Government when the Yorko-
logical time arrives : I have seen the keyblocks of
two designed by Ashton et ills and Longstaff et cie.
The first you will get for the evening reception at
Exhibition Buildings, Melbourne, the second for
a grand concert. Ashton’s design shows a
matronly Britannia and a maidenly Australia
in robes of, respectively, crimson and gold,
and blue, fronting each other from opposite
sides of the invitation sentence. All the
lettering is of gold, and bearing in mind the fes-
tive objective of the card, the artists have used as
background trailing grape-vines. Longstaff’s
(Norman Lindsay is the co.) design lets you into
the opening of Parliament. Britannia again, but
standing against the cliffs of Dover ; girlish Aus-
tralia again, but backgrounded by a section of the
Old Man Plain, with one gum and a few sheep.
The colors are mostly weary pinks arid fainting
greys and ochres. Such a modest-looking card,
and we ought not to affect too much of the simple
dandelion when we know that we shall be a bold,
bright sunflower under the sun of York.
The much-admired new chrysanthemums at the
recent annual Sydney Show were Lady Lygon
and Success. The first is a big white flower, which
is only “new” by virtueof certain tiny horns on the
petals. “Success” —a dense, yellow dahlia-like
flower—is mathematical in the regularity, curve,
and position of its petals. Chrysanthemum-
ming becomes a sort of religion, and its
decidedly funny to hear a group of solid
beefy-looking men prating of “depth,” and
“cup” and “heart,” and wrangling over the
momentous fact that one petal in a “sport” of
“ golden gate ” (one petal out of perhaps 6000) was
“ straggling.” The prize-table—pink roses, white
chrysanthemums, and maiden-hair fern—looked
cold and elegant, but under its cloth was show-
ing some hideous pink calico, a hint of purple,
which completely spoilt the pink of the roses.
Antonia Dolores —what a satire is that second
name on her laughing, sunny face ! —sang to a big
afternoon audience last week. The programme
was again strictly classical, the kind of programme
to which some listen in rapturous silence, and
which others those with souls that grovel
—wish was more intelligible and ballady.
Whilst the musical Elect are listening with
closed eyes, slightly-dropped jaws, and fingers
tightly clenched round their programmes,
the Philistine is counting the organ-pipes.
She counts them accurately, then multiplies the
sum by the six pot-plants on either side of the
Stein way, and just as her arithmetic is about to
end in headache she begins to worry over the
inner meaning of some of the ears of the people
in the front seats. (Max Nordau' has said such
things about those kind of ears.) Or she starts
glancing backwards and sees what seems in-
separable to a matinee recital—straps of library
books, grass-bags, fancy-baskets, and parcels and
mushroom hats. Sometimes the looking back-
ward will be in fancy only. When Mdlle.
Dolores thrilled “.Ah, lo so ” from “The Magic
j lute the Philistine saw herself, a child of nine
having a music lesson on that very air from a lady
™than incipient beard, a long wooden knitting-
needle and a Skye terrier. Every few minutes
she would . crack the pupil hard on the knuckles
with the timber needle and kiss the terrier loudly
t .^ ee . n his ambushed eyes. To this day that
Philistine hates Magic Flutes, Skye terriers, and
ladies with incipient beards.
But there was no soul in that audience so low as
not to be thrilled by the Echo Song and Pensee
D Automne. After hearing Mdlle’s. I’ll flout
that hitherto wonderful echo at Leura for ever-
more.
Mdlle’s. dress of buttercup brocade had a
yoke of pale blue A large jet butterfly
with outstretched wings shivered and shimmered
on her bosom. She wears her hair Madonna-wise,
and so adds an apparent eight or ten years to her
appearance. To every good there’s a recoil of evil.
If you guard against going bald by plastering your
hair down, not frizzing and building it up on top,
you look middle-aged when you’re still under
twenty-five.
I see that Sydney Comedy Club is advertising
that it will give performances in aid of charities.
If it would do something for that most worthy
charity, the Women’s Hospital, the players might
have a good chance of a nimbus each in the great
Hereafter. Six years ago this hospital started
with one bed in a four-roomed house. Now it’s
only a terrace house, in Crown-street, with about
20 beds, and it’s very poor. It has no hot-
water service, and it does its sterilising in a fish-
kettle, and it’s not. rent free. Six hundred babies
have started life in this hospital, and scores of
poor women’s lives have been saved by its surgical
ward. The other day, it got £6O from a garden
fete at Mrs. G-eorge Cruickshanks’. That means
another £6O from Govt., which is generous of the
Govt. (I wonder what it costs to obstruct the
streets with the horrors known as Venetian masts).
Mrs. Wood, whom we so loved as Essie .Tenyns,
sends the institution a case of fruit every week,
and Mrs. Baumann gave it the latest thing in
operating-tables—all heavy plate-glass and bronze.
Our medical Mayor and Dr. Watson Munro have
given it their services from the first, and the
matron—the last I saw of her she was cuddling a set
of week-old twins, just the length of a tablespoon,
and looking hurt because the visitor said they
weren’t pretty.
A burst blood vessel and a stringent medical
command to take a year’s rest, are the reasons for
Tenor Wood house’s “Bohemian Night.” The
night will occur this Saturday, at the Town Hall,
and promises much. Everyone who can sing,
dance, act, and lightning-sketch better than any-
one else of the same name will contribute. And
the Mayoress is taking an arduous interest in the
ticket selling.
Sydney must have been short of medical attend-
ance during the four hours that 112 doctors were
dining at the Australia last week in honor of their
colleagues who had served in S’Africa. Dr.
Sydney Jones was in the chair, and grouped
around him were Drs. Fiaschi, Thomson, Roth,
Foreman, Perkins, Mullins, Graham, Bundle,
Prof. Wilson, Major Evans and Dr. Creed. Dr.
(Mayor) Graham’s speech was forcible and pa-
thetic. Dr. Beattie balanced it with a humorous
one. The menu was not ornamented with red-
crosses and ambulance waggons, as some persons
averred. The browse started with Canapes au
Caviare (which sounds nice), the toasts were few,
and the music was of a throw-down-the-gauntlet-
who’s-afraid character. “Ye Gallants of Eng-
land,” “Lo’, My Courage,” and “The Toreador.”
Friends of the manager of the Federal line of
steamers, who is going for a trip, banquetted him
the other night to the music of bagpipes, and
one of the after-dinner speakers told a bagpipe
story. . A young Highland soldier, shot all over,
lay dying in a crowded hospital tent on the veldt.
His commanding officer said in effect, “ Anything
I can do for you shall be done.” “ I hae but one
wish,” replied the Scotsman—“to hear the skirl
of the pipes once mair.” A piper was ordered to
skirl up and down outside the tent, and he skirled
and skirled, and the thistle lad got better and
better. By dawn the piper was played-out and
the wounded man was out of danger and asking
for parritch. But—oh, the pathos of this but !
all the other patients were Dead. Now’s the time
for a Scotch doctor to start the bagpipe cure.
Last week Sydney insurance-managers, sixty
strong, revived their custom of an annual picnic
by going to National Park. The chairman and
secretary of the outing put their managerial
freight into a special train at Redfern, then went
to look for stragglers. In their absence the Purple
Moth—the name of the special—flew off and the
innocents inside knowing nothing of the arrange-
ments, and scarcely sure of their destination, got
the Moth tethered up at Sutherland and tele-
phoned back for their leaders. They were
answered, “We’ll truck them in a horse-box.”
One of the managers raced down from town to the
Park in his motor car at 20 miles an hour. Mr.
St. Vincent Welch was chairman at the luncheon,
Mr. Danvers was in the vice-chair, and the chief
amusement of all these very solid gentlemen was
to watch the motor race round and round the
oval, and then do stump-jumping tricks.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The distribution of invites to the ‘ Yorkeries’
promises to be a matter for angry inquiry
when the Jookanjuchess have departed, and
when the whole work of the Reception Committee
is judged by certain personal results. Already
one hears two or three committeemen named as
‘ working the pasteboards ’ for their own little
ends. If some of the Melbourne guests of the
Government happen to get ‘ spotted ’ by the more
influential of the rejected ticket claimants, the
fat will be in the fire, that’s certain. The eye of
righteous indignation is not likely to miss many
opportunities for complaint. Somebody will have
to explain the appearance of Blank, Dash, and
Asterisk among the chosen ones in the event of
them turning up at the various functions. But in
scores of cases the tickets which ought not to have
been issued will simply be framed by their proud
possessors.
“ A Government Inquiry Bureau for invited
guests— i.e., guests who are strangers to Mel-
bourne —has been opened on the ground floor of
Prell’s Building in Collins-street, at the corner of
Queen-street. This seems to be the one item of
Govt, hospitality that can claim the virtue of
economy, thanks to the business tact of its origin-
ator, Mr. Moore aforesaid. In the first place,
Mr. Prell let the suite of rooms to the Bureau for
a nominal sum. Then they were furnished on the
hire system—reception-room, ladies’ boudoir, and
writing-room, with attendant attached. A clerk
and a type-writer represent the only other work-
ing expenses, and the smart little show has an
honorary manager in Mr. J. A. Levey, youngest
brother of the almost-forgotten George Collins
Levey.
“ Jenkins of the Beard groans that he will be
reduced to the size of a bodkin if people keep on
pestering him for invitations to the May shows.
He seems to have a really bad time, for the
majority hold that the opening of the Federal
Parliament will eclipse all other York displays—
and if it doesn’t it ought to. Shoals of letters
reach the bearded one’s private residence, his
club, his various places of call in the city, and
arrive in cart-loads at Parliament House impor-
tuning dear Mr. Jenkins not to forget his faithful
old friend so-and-so.
“A letter to the Herald from Mr. Byron
Moore disturbed the equanimity of a good many
city speculators who have been fixing seats over
the verandahs on the line of the York procession
route. These stand-builders, as a rule, forgot to
give them the necessary slope, or ‘pitch.’ Early
birds who booked front-row places can make their
minds easy, of course, for they will be perched
over the outer margin of the verandah where they
can look down upon the street by simply tucking
in their chins. Folks on the second line of obser-
vation will need to crane their necks a little, and
as for the others they will see no procession at all
unless the angle of the structure is scientifically
acuted. The standlords must consider the re-
quirements of their back-row tenants, or there
will be weeping and wailing and gnashing of
teeth when tne pageant comes to pass, and much
employment for the lawyers afterwards. Some
of the seat-holders who do obtain a full view of
the Jookanjuchess will probably bemoan the cost
of the entertainment, but how about the unfor-
tunates who part with 20s. a head and get never
a glimpse of a future Sovereign in return for their
outlay ?
“The Governor-General and Her Ex. have had
the regular vice regal carriage accident in Mel-
bourne. Ever since Lady Barkly died, as the
result of a carriage accident in ’57, it has been the
correct thing for every Governor’s wife to
have, at least, one chance of sudden death from
this cause. Lady Brassey had a good old smash-
up ; her hubby had several. Lady Hopetoun,
during her first term in Melbourne, had a first-
class accident. Now she has had another accident
—at the corner of Princes Bridge and Swanston-
street, only a few yards from where Gov. Barkly’s
wife received her death blow. Following pre-
cedent, there is a fair chance of the Yorks giving
the newspapers a sensation to write up from the
same cause. Nobody can represent the Crown in
Victoria without a carriage accident.
“ With black frocks for indoor or out the Coun-
tess of Hopetoun is wearing scarlet posies.
Already this tip has gone the rounds, and the
fashionables of metamorphosed Melbourne have
deposed the chic cluster of violets for the red
blossom. In the same way, Mrs. Corbett’s tulle
cravat has found a thousand imitators, and now
there’s not a smart chin in Toorak without its
under-frame of white or black tulle.
“Local women apparently pine to pose as dis-
tant connections or near friends of mourning
Royalty, so determined are they to sadden Com-
monwealth pageants by supererogatory trappings
of woe. The Governor-General’s cheerful voice
crying in a funereal dress-scape preaches that,
though widow-like weeds are free to all, still
official marms alone are expected to luxuriate in
full sartorial grief. However, official or not, Vic-
toriennes as a rule scorn mere complimentary half-
measures, and will greet the Cornwalls in Welcome
toilettes of all-black, piebald, or steel-grey. If
we’re nbt careful we’ll end by out-mourning the
Departed’s own relations. Mrs. Cornwall herself
is provided with white skirts for the tropics, a
suit of softest grey cashmere, its grey silk vest
appliqued with white, and its Empire belt caught
with a buckle of mauve enamel. By the way, the
Visitor’s Australian outfit seems rich in boleros,
almost every gown boasting a fanciful little mem-
ber of the coated family.
“ By this time the elder Hope boy under his
tutor’s wing will have started back to school at
Eton. The military doctor who pulled the then
very-ill Governor-General through the trying
journey from Madras to Australia goes with the
travellers as far as India. If Hopey, junior,
stayed here long his head would swell beyond hat
size. Local scribes write up the youngster’s
‘chubbiness,’ and his pony, Sundowner, and
chronicle solemnly how, when the family were
travelling from N.S.W., the engine had to halt
near Melb. because ‘ the children ’ had neglected
to move into the car where attendants were
waiting to get them out of travelling clothes
into festive toilettes. The younger boy is a quaint
miniature of his dad. In pompous photographic
groups wherein ‘ Lord Hope ’ looms large as the
heir, younger son ‘ Charlie, ’ seated on the ground
hugging his small knees, looks exactly like little
Paul Dotnbey. The first Sunday in Melbourne,
the diplomatic Earl took both his lads to Scots
Church, and afterwards led them behind the
scenes and re-introduced them to Meenister
Marshall, who some years back, baptised ‘ Charles
Melbourne.’ Can one picture a scene more after
Sandy’s heart ? There was kirk intilt, Sawbath,
Meenister, Cairns catechism, ‘ the quality,’ siller
—wi’ whusky and bonnie lassies, ft would have
been from a Scotch point of view a very heaven
on earth.
“ Two or three paragraphs in the Australasian
have suggested that the Cabinet’s selection of
Stonington (the late Mr. Wagner’s place) as a
home for the new State Governor was influenced
by Mr. M'Culloch, whose handsome son, Sam,
married into the Wagner family. But it is well-
known that Stonington recommended itself. Mr.
Wagner spent £BO,OOO on the property, and the
Government, in beating the rental down to £lOOO
a year, with the option of purchase for £40,000,
drove a hard bargain with the trustees. None of
the other properties on the list of proposals were
at all tempting.
“On the principle of ‘throw plenty of mud
and some of it’s sure to stick,’ some Meib. people
have been writing their names in Hopetoun’s
visiting-book over and over again. The hope is
that by ‘ calling often ’ they will not fail to be in-
vited to some function while the .Took is on
exhibit. But, in fact, the oft-callers only make
themselves a nuisance, and Wallington’s soul
groaneth from its overburdened depth, ‘ Better
Not! ’ All who have called more than once have
been put in a black-list. At Govt. House, people
with two entries in the book are known by the
name by which Ada Reeve used to call her plush
cycling knickers— ‘ Twicers.’
“ A young matron on Melbourne Block exhibits
a costume which at first sight paralyses the male
person. Like the mass of fashionable Melb.
women to-day, she wears no corsets, and her blouse
consists of alternate rows of opaque and trans-
parent material. The two upper spaces of lace
follow the usual custom and allow a liberal allow-
ance of the lady’s shoulders and bust to be seen,
but beneath the lower ones is placed some material
the exact tint of her skin, so that the effect is
that of a woman with the upper half attired in one
semi-transparent garment and nothing else.
“ The cou pies that canoodled at the moonlight
M.C.C. concerts are repining that by an inexor-
able decree they are declared ‘off.’ Such an
opportunity as it gave even the Naicest and best
brought-up ! It was so simple to say ‘as we have
the tickets, and there’s really a good programme
we may as well go, ’ and it was as easy as sneezing
to pair-off at once to the grounds, and wander to
Yarra’s brink in the mellowy light. A programme
was secured and taken home to musical parents—
and that closed the evidence for the defence. Only
the cold silent stars know the amount of spooning
that went on at these alfresco nocturnes.
“A returned Yictorienne has been treating
cronies to a peep at her complimentary half-
mourning, prescribed by London experts. A
skirt, meant for small dinners, is thick, of heavy
black satin, plain to the knees, from there to the
feet all broad, flat tucks hemmed with white silk,
while an inner flounce of white silk peeps occa-
sionally from under its train. Two bodices were
sent home with this skirt. Of these the black
satin one, liberally treated with black lisse, em-
broidered in seed-jet and silver, has a filmy, float-
ing Empire scarf, ending in fairy-like fringe.
Here and there on the chemisette and under-
sleeves of white silk-muslin gleam small buckles of
black enamel, with a crystal in the heart of each.
“The second blouse, built to look cool, is
creamy lace, put on loosely over ivory satin.
Narrow white ribbons thread the lace, and a wide
black-lisse sash, knotted on the hip, falls to the
skirt-hem, in broad ends, embroidered with
raised roses of white chiffon.
“ The trinkets worn with these costumes are a
long fine chain of gold and black enamel links,
black enamel ring with diamonds and small side-
combs to match. Or else necklet, crown-brooch
and bangle of ebony and pearls. White shoes
have their toes delicately embroidered with seed-
jet, and black ones are similarly treated with
‘ pearls.’ A sable cape is lined with deep purple
velvet. The tall fair, slim wearer of these win-
some weeds is accused by the envious of smiling
through her patriotic tears, whenever she nears a
looking-glass.
“Prof. Franklin Peterson, Marshall Hall’s suc-
cessor, has accepted the position, evidently, and
begins to direct a few round-arm blows at. the
martial bard. He talks, in his lectures, of some
vague person with ‘ opinions exaggerated and dis-
torted by the want of historical study,’ ‘ looking
upon himself as the apostle of light in a dark age,
and his disciples and admirers as the followers
and worshippers of the only true God,’&c., &c.
Musical circles look for quite an interesting and
instructive time between the rivals. A bored
people are always ready to cry, ‘ Blessed is the
pacemaker.’
“ Melbourne’s middle life has furnished a
humorous romance. A pretty and popular girl
was leaving home just before Easter on a visit
(ostensibly) to a relative up-country. Mater-
familias saw her off, gave her her blessing and a
large cheque, and enjoined her to write soon. She
did, too soon almost. The artless young thing
changed lines at Ballarat and proceeded to an-
other part of the country, whence she wrote her
mother to announce that she had joined her hus-
band in her ‘ new home.’ After this bolt from the
blue, she proceeded to say that she had been
married three years, but had waited till her
youthful spouse got his final degree (in his pro-
fession) before she ‘ let on. ’ Tableau !
“Mrs. 0. Vallange, wrapped up in profuse fox
furs, was the one occupant of the Vice-Regal box
at the Flemington races on Saturday. The
“ Cliveden” and Byron Moore’s parties excepted,
all the fairest frockers sported the colors of the
rainbow—Mrs. Herbert Power gleaming in a
splashy frock of black, blue, and pink. Mrs.
Lloyd was foremost with a brick-red cloth winter
frock. The dressy Dowager had her model figure
encased in an uncreasingly-fitting frock of black
and white with a heavy frosting of sequins.
Captain Wallington, swagger in greys and fawns,
looked in at the race-meeting before the finish.
He was rushed on the terrace and pump-handled
to such an extent that he grew weary of raising
his tiny boxer (that looked like a pimple on his
lean head).

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Cardinal Moran declines the invitation to
Melbourne to bow to the Duke. Primate Smith
and precedence are again the trouble.
A Melbourne apothecary boasts that he
has recently reduced a certain very fat N.S.W.
Freetrader’s avoirdupois 22lbs. in a month by
means of his anti-fat specifio.
In S. Australia they still write Q.C. and
not K.C., in consequence of Judge Boucaut’s dic-
tum that the little bit of alphabet remains for six
months after the sovereign’s death.
J. J. Miller, the “ Ironbark Bishop,” who
has so often worried Lyne and others at election-
times, has fallen again—at the hands of young
M’Laurin—at The Hume. J. J. Millar and F. B.
Freehill are the champion election-losers of
N.S.W.
The most incredible thing The Bulletin
has heard for a long while is that Baxter Bruce,
the cheerful Sydney solicitor, so well-known in
north and south, once upon a time recited tem-
perance pieces and was the prize youth of a
Brisbane Band of Hope.
The fist of S. W. Griffith, Queensland's
C.J., constitutes the champion puzzle. Recently
two barristers in open court got His Honor’s notes
to read, but mournfully passed them back to S.G.
The Chief blandly explained “ They are my rough
notes.” He was quite right.
None of the Gavan Dutfies are absolute
duffers, as is usual with the progeny of jaynius.
There is Attorney J. G. Duffy, a member of Vic.
Cabinet; Barrister F. G. Duffy, K.C.; and the
third, C. G. Duffy, has just vaulted into the clerk-
ship of’the Australian Senate.
John Forrest having intimated that, as the
Commander-in-Chief will have a salary of £l5OO
and travelling-expenses, a man of S’African ex-
perience may be induced to accept the position, a
coterie of Sydney busy-bodies are pantingly
hoping that Baden-Powell will fill the bill.
Robertson, M.H.R. for Robertson,
N.S.W., returning thanks for his election :—“ He
was sorry that the editor of that poor, miserable
little sheet, the Gulgong Advertiser, was not
present. That ignorant individual, like his supe-
rior officer, Mephistopheles, had evidently gone
down to hell again.” Does this forecast the tone
of the House of Reps. ?
That good journalist, Mr. A. S. Rathbone,
for years London correspondent of Sydney D.T.,
died there last week. He was formerly editor
and proprietor of Auckland (M.L.) Observer,
which he founded, and up to the time of his death
was connected with the London representatives
of Maoriland Associated Press. Mr. Rathbone
came of the well-known Liverpool mercantile
family of that name.
HIGH ART, AS AT PRESENT IN
MELBOURNE.
The dust-box of genius is seen in Sum-
mers’s colossal sitting statues of Victoria, Albert,
Albert Edward,. and Alexandra, at Melbourne
Public Library. He got his two thou., and Big
Clarke got his bart’ey. After that, Summers
would be. quite content to see the statues relegated
to the lumber-hole would indeed prefer it.
Poor little Queen Victoria is a Minerva, King
Edward an Achilles. Nordau’s “ lie of monarchy”
is perpetuated by the fiction of monarchy’s
sculptors.
The Bulletin’s old friend, Blessings-of-
Poverty Jefferis, one-time shepherd of the Pitt-
street (Sydney) flock, wherewith is mustered
“Mister James” Fairfax, has just retired from
the pulpit of Adelaide’s chief Congregational
Church. The ancient platitudinarian, after 25
years’ service with a long break a little past half-
way, had become too deadly-dull and Conserva-
tive even for the pulpit. So he goes back where
he can sit near the House of Lords.
Empress Frederick (daughter of the late
Queen Victoria, is slowly dying of cancer in the
uterus and the kidneys. Her husband died cf cancer
in the larynx. The old Empress Augusta (wife of
William I.) of cancer in the stomach. And the
present ruler of Germany (it as as yet only
whispered) suffers from the same terrible com-
plaint. The diseased part is placed by his loyal
subjects mostly somewhere about the brain ; but
the fact seems indisputable.
The main reason for electing C. A. Lee
(who, by the way, is on the brink of 60, and a
native of Balmain) to lead N.S.W. Opposition,
with the probability of early landing in the State
Premiership, was his pelf. Mr. Lee made money
at Maitland and Tenterfield, and is now able to
devote all his time to politics. A well-to-do Free-
trade politician in active service is a rarity. Most
of them spoon-feed the country with one hand
and fight for their own tucker with the other.
One of the penalties a Premier pays for
owning or controlling a newspaper is that its
constant adulation of him turns his friends into
disgusted foes. “The distinguished Mr. See.”
“Mr. See, with that wisdom which has charac-
terised all his utterances since the first day
N.S.W. had the good fortune to receive him as a
member of its Parliament.” “ Mr. See, as every-
one knows, stands immeasurably above Mr. Reid
in ability and good sense.” And all that sort of
thing.
About a candidate for President of the
Senate :
Chaffey Baker is playing a deep game for a billet, and
expects Bartonian support for the Senate Presidency.
All the deadly enmity towards Kingston vanished some
months ago, and the chief boss of S.A. National Ass even
had the audacity to adopt an ultra-Radical ticket at the
recent election, including adult-suffrage and a “ White
Australia.” Baker’s somersault has disgusted his friends,
particularly the Nat. Ass. He is sacrificing-LC(K) a year
as President of S.A. teg. Council for the chance of a simi-
lar billet in the Senate. Baker is just 60, intensely ambi-
tious, and personally unpopular.
Premier Seddon lias never yet been ad-
mitted to membership of that very select coterie,
Wellington Club. Since the outburst of Jingo
fever, however, a strong section, including some
of the very bluest-blooded, has declared for his
election. The Premier was actually invited
recently to a quasi-military function at the Club,
but he didn’t attend, although so certain was his
presence regarded that a most exalted Person was
purposely absent lest precedence would have to
be yielded to King Dick.
John Norton’s residence at Randwick ad-
joins that of cricket-celebrity Harry
Moses, a fact which John was not aware
of when he first honored the racing
suburb with his household gods. John
Norton’s bull-pup is also named Moses,
and one day when the gentle creature
annoyed the erudite John, the latter
talked to him for some minutes in Nor-
tonian language. Mr. Moses, on the
other side of the fence, being unaware
of the Christian name of Norton’s
beauty, took it all to himself, and
poking his head over wanted to know
what the deuce he had done to rouse
the wild man of King-street. Explana-
tions followed.
“ Arthur Wellesley Beaconsfield
Brown, charged with false pre-
tences ” . This sort of thing is
now a common feature of police-court
reports, and points painfully to a
connection between the high-falutin
name and the erring way. Arthur
is usually an elaborately-dressed youth
with a large bump on the rear-head
and looking for all the world as if he
had christened himself. His mother
was probably a vain and silly person
who endowed him with her weaknesses
as well as with the overwhelming name.
And in trying to live up to said
name and his own conception of him-
self Arthur is compelled to hunt up the
needful on the “ crook,” to get it fast
enough. And then cometh the sprint-
iug copper and the iron bars from be-
hind which the dull red eye of repent-
ance looketh out.

case), to Pilcher, K.C. (about to com-

now. I am not interested in this action.
I don’t know whether you have ever

you are not engaged in ?”
A quarter of a century ago Archdeacon
Wilson, who died lately at Dubbo, N.S.W., was
an energetic and able Sydney pressman. He was
more literary than journalistic, and at that early
period worked hard to make a “picture” paper
popular. The Illustrated Sydney News, whose
chequered career yielded many good numbers, was
edited by him with something approaching enthu-
siasm. He took to the church,'however, and pro-
bably to surer peace of mind. In later years he
generally tried to persuade journalistic aspirants
to devote themselves to some other walk.

by such a practical suppoiter as was the one which
waited on the N.S. W. Railway Commissioners, to
urge the abolition of the Great Zig-zag and the
duplication of the Western railway-line across the
Blue Mountains, at a cost of £700,000. W. W.
Young, M.L.A. for Bathurst, who spoke for the
interests of his own town, explained to the Com-
missioners that, having driven their locomotives
for 19 years, and many of them across the line of
which the deputation was complaining, he was
quite acquainted with the difficulties and dangers

Died recently from the effects of exposure to
the recent heavy tropical rains, Lindsay Crawford,
a man with as varied an experience as anyone of
the Northern Territory. Successively he was
bushman, in charge of a section of “the over-
land,” pioneer cattle-farmer on the marvellously-
grassed Victoria River Downs, cattle-shipper to
Java and Singapore, and manager for the mys-
sterious Anglo-French Exploration Syndicate. A
magnificent specimen of a man, on whose broad
shoulders financial ill-luck seemed to have made a
permanent residence.

The fact that the Lmpiess Frederick of
Germany is dying of the disease (cancer) that
killed her husband is written of, in S.M. Herald,
as “one more link in the chain of evidence that
this terrible scourge is more contagious than is
generally admitted.” But this is not necessarily
a case of direct contagion, since both the late
Emperor and the Empress may have derived the
disease from a common source. The Bulletin
hears of a house, in a fashionable but low-lying
Sydney suburb, in which three successive occu-
pants have died from cancer.

About Senator John Downer :
Perhaps the liomeliest-looking mail in the Federal
Legislature will be John Downer, who facially is almost
milk-white skin and blood-shot eyes. Nature built John
Downer for a champion bruiser, but circumstances made
him, like three other brothers, a lawyer. John has been
a prominent S.A politician for *23 years, representing the
same district from the very start, and he became a Q.O.
in 1877. An absolutely straight man of great grit,
he never loses a personal friend. He has been thrice
Premier of S.A., and it was his Ministry which carried a
Protective tariff in 1335 Though just on 6J, Downer
married a handsome and charming Sydney girl two years
ago, and has fully determined to live on until he is 100.

Ven next you have bad eggs, nefer you boil dem.
Dey must be scrambled. Now, you hear !

lie the rumor that Christian de Wet has

great captains have been a “bitoff.” Alexander

The (immigrant) girl whom I got from
the ship Duke of Norfolk,” writes the wife of the
owner of a Q. wayback station, “was just too
superior for anything—very pretty, and with hair
done in the latest style, and given to borrowing
Keats or Shelley in her spare moments. She
tended the children fairly well, but complained
bitterly of Australia. ‘ Then why did you
come out here—lowering the wages, and taking
bread out of our mouths ? ’ demanded the cook,
who had views on immigration. ‘ You’ll have a
lot more of us as long as the lying advertisements
appear in “our” papers.’ retorted the girl.
‘ Come to Queensland ! Our young men want
wives. There are happy homes waiting for enter-
prising girls,’ &c., <fec. And she showed me some
clippings from an English paper to that effect.
However, she married very soon, a strapping
young ring-barker, and _moved «on with him and
his tent to the next station. One can foresee what
she will be in a few years’ time—sunburnt, weary-
eyed, the mother of many children, the baker of
bad bread in heavy-lidded camp-ovens, and no
time for Shelley or Keats and day-dreams. But
her children will have the most poetical names in
the world.”

He badly wanted to get the rest of the snake, so

right through and round again to be swallowed.
This worried the traveller, and he sat down and
thought violently. Then lie -went away to look for
a cork to keep the young ones inside with. After a
bit he forgot what he was looking for, and coming
back to the log, from which the snake had now
crawled out, he was surprised to see that the
reptile had tied a knot on its own tail to get over
the difficulty. Waving the old bottle-neck to us
aa satisfactory evidence of something or the other,
the traveller went further West.

“D.C.”: Near Healesville (Vic,) last
summer, came across a pair of snakes in the
road. Killed the male, but female got away. Re-
turning along the track half-an-hour later, found
that the dead snake had disappeared. Fol-
lowed a broadly-marked track across the road and
found that female had returned and had dragged
her mate into a hollow log. Seems to show that,
during pairing season at least, snakes are sensib’e
to feelings of affection.

“A.M.” to The Bulletin, from Maori-
land:—
The M.E. butter-factory boom is not such an all-round
blessing as it seems. It pays the capitalist who owns
the land all right, but means the very worst kind of
slavery to the actual producer. Before the boom, farm
hands were paid £1 weekly and board for working eight
hours a day ; now the same class of hands get from 10s. to
15s. weekly, working 14 hours a day in all weathers. On
the other hand, land that cost from £4 to £6 an acre for
grazing purposes, now lets for £1 an acre yearly for milk-
ing. It is not a pleasant fact that many settlers’ children
get up before daylight, milk until breakfast, travel three
or four miles to school and back, milking again until dark,
and, as often as not, ankle-keep in mud in both stock-
yard and paddock. Small wonder that the poor little
morsels of sweated humanity often fall asleep over their
evening meal. There was some talk of legislation in their
favour, but it died out. When a settler has to pay heavy
rent, and interest on his stock and plant, he gets too
tired to agitate about anything.
Serve them both right. The “hands” should
start a union, and use the Arbitration Court,
while the selectors should agitate, tired or not, for
a gentle application of the Closer Settlement Act.
People who are too tired to agitate deserve all
they get.
• * * »
From a coal-mine on the Illavvarra line,
N.S.W., 14 men were riding homewards on a
skip, provided, by way of break, with a wooden
lever and a wire on the front wheel. The way
was all down-grade, and the break gave way,
whereupon the skip went off with a jump, and
gained headway at every yard. About a quarter of
a mile on was a “flat,” or siding, on which stood
a “set” of 14 empty skips. A young engine-
driver who was on the travelling skip knew the
points were open on to the flat, and balancing
himself on the edge as it approached, bounded off
the skip, actually raced and heat it for 15 vards,
and threw up the points-lever in the nick of time.
The skip pulled up safely a mile on. The 13 men
(lucky 13 for once) whom he had delivered from
death presented him with a gold watch and chain.
* * * *
Re horse-sense. I know of an ancient
moke in a N.S.W. colliery that was very intelli-
gent, but nevertheless through getting a sudden
kink in his intellect caused a bad smash. He
answered to the name of “ Soldier,” and was used
for bringing out the loaded skips. As each set
came out he would range up alongside and move
at whatever pace was required, while his driver
shifted the big engine-rope from its hook and
hooked Soldier on instead to take the set away.
Sometimes the skips would come out fast, and
then it was smart work to snap the catch open,
release the rope, and hook Soldier on. Soldier
was considered infallible, however, and so he was
for over eight years. Then one morning, instead
of ranging up as usual, he wheeled round, let fly
both heels into his driver’s stomach, and dropped
him insensible across the line in front of the
rapidly-moving, loaded skips. The skips reached
the man before his mates, and they buried his
dismembered remains next day in a blanket,
there is a profound moral in this for every citizen
who employs a soldier.— Bags.

“ VV.H.I.” : Be R. L. Ste reason's mystical
phenomenon (“Vale,” B. 26/l/’01). There is a
scientific explanation of this. When at rest, the
beating of the heart becomes slower and the body-
temperature sensibly declines—markedly so with
Europeans who have lived for some time in the
tropics, and whose normal temperature is half a
degree above the European normal. But, about
an hour before dawn, both temperature and
number of heart-beats increase, and it is at this
DROUGHT-TIME AT DIRRANBANDI: “THE GENERAL MANAGER INSPECTING
THE RUNS.”
juncture—the physiological “dead-point”—that
very aged persons and those suffering from wasting
disease usually expire. Again, the earth-tempera-
ture (under normal conditions of cloudless sky and
absence of wind) gradually sinks from radiation,
but this thermometrical depression ceases when
the refracted heat-rays from the still unseen sun
begin to warm the atmosphere, accompanied by
an almost imperceptible wind. When the physio-
logical and phy'siographical dead-centre occur at
approximately the same time, the curious sensa-
tion mentioned by Stevenson and others is experi-
enced. Naturally, in heated rooms, or on cloudy
or windy nights, the conditions are unfavorable to
the chilly, shivering sensation referred to.

“ Yarrum ” : Wollongong, for the size of
it, has fewer pubs, than any other town in
N.S. W. Certain leading lights there —for reasons
unconnected with Cold-Tea—oppose the granting
of every license asked for. Consequently,
accommodation is inadequate, and holiday-people
give the place the go-by. By the way, Temora —
on a population-basis—is the best pubbed of all
N.S.W. towns.

A middle-aged Austrian (a recent immi-
grant) turned up the other day at Woolerina
Station (Q.). He was unable to speak English ;
his clothes were in tatters, and his feet bandaged
with old bags in lieu of boots. He had been
three days without solid food, and was refused
tucker at an adjacent station. He carried a
crumpled piece of paper on which was written in
English, “ This man’s an Austrian, direct him to
Grenvelle.” He presented it to an employe of
that station, who showed his Christian fellowship
by adorning it with this scrawl:
This feller is a turner, sind him on to ell.
No wonder Australia sends contingents to
slaughter Boers!

At the District Court in Rockhampton
(Q.), recently, while a Chinaman was being tried
for perjury, another Chow was being examined
through an interpreter. “ Ask the witness,” said
the barrister, “did he say this in the lower court ”
—intending to read extract from depositions.
But the interpreter at once rattled off a question
to the witness, despite the frantic efforts of Judge
and counsel to stop him, and to explain that he
hadn’t yet heard what he was supposed to have
said. Then the witness plaintively warbled some-
thing in reply, and the interpreter turned to the
barrister and said, “He says yes.”
A daring and successful
gaol-breaker of the early N.S. W.
days was one Wilson. Once
while he and several other con-
victs were being conveyed from
Portland to Melbourne in a
schooner, Wilson succeeded in
tampering with the irons of all
to such an extent that, by the
removal of a piece of wood
(painted black) which had been
placed in the severed portion of
the link, every man would be
free. When the vessel reached
Warrnambool (where she was to
ship another batch) Wilson and
the others were lodged in the
local gaol; but during the night
Wilson cut a hole in the cell-
door (three thicknesses of hard-
wood) large enough to allow of
the passage of a man’s body, and
by the morning they had all
flown. Wilson could have eluded
pursuit easily enough had he been
by himself ; but his fellow-esca-
pees, trusting to his knowledge
of the bush, hung so closely to
him that the whole batch was
caught soon afterwards. On
another occasion, when being
brought to Melbourne in charge
of four policemen, he jumped
overboard as the boat was com-
ing up the Yarra, and, carrying
281 b. irons, was caught in the
reeds and perished. Had he
managed to get into clear water he would pro-
bably have got away, as his arms were free and he
was a strong swimmer.

“X.X.X.”: T’other day, fishing on the
Hawkesbury (N.S.W.), I picked up a lost heavy
line. When I hauled the whole tackle on board
the jaws of a well-matured shark were hanging in
grim death to the hook. The rest of the brute was
absent, and seemed to have been just severed.
Shark had eaten shark.
“ A.E.G.” to The Bulletin :
In cases of abdominal inflammation, the following hint
might be useful to bushmen and others far from medical
aid. Split a chicken in halves ; and, whilst the halves
are still hot clap them on the abdomen. In a short time
it (the poultice) will be quite green. Repeat applications
until there is no greenness observable —when the in-
flammation will be gone. Once, came upon a blackfellow
at last gasp, and cured him with a chook and a half. I
got the tip from an English whilom medical-man for —
poor devil! —a glass of rum !
The foregoing smacks more of Celestial than of
English methods—despite the beef-steak cure
for black-eye. Split rats, chickens, frogs, mice,
&c., are great applications in Chinese medi-
cine for inflammatory conditions of all kinds, and
whether by reason of the faith element or of the
slight counter-irritation produced, they seem to
have held favor from time immemorial. The
theory that they ' “ draw out the inflammation ” is
utter bosh, but the continued warmth may do
some good. Writer has seen a poultice of split
mice in a Chow’s rheumatic elbow, but hot fomen-
tations would have cured it quicker. In bowel
inflammation the bushman had better use the
chicken for soup, and pin his abdominal faith to
a rousing mustard poultice, with a dose of castor-
oil or Mr. Cockle, and subsequent flannel belts.

“ Ita ” : Travelling once in the West, I
happened upon a writing tacked to a tree about
100 yds. in front of a selector’s hut; and was sur-
prised to find it was a marriage certificate. Fur-
ther on I came on a man pulling a bullock out of
a water-hole, and he told me that the placard wa3
the “ marriage lines ” of himself and wife, and
had been posted up to rebut a slanderous state-
ment that the two were only “living together,”
and so clear their characters before the public.
Inquired about said public; and found that the
only neighbours within a radiuslif 20 miles were
a German couple, who were frankly “ living to-
gether,” and a bachelor cow-stealer.

One of the most remarkable results of the
present protracted drought is that a leading
N.S.W.- stock-and-station agent has on his hands
for sale 5000/oi<7 - -year-old maiden ewes. Such a
thing has never previously happened within his
experience.
The N.S. W. Aborigines’ Board reports
“ that a full-blooded black has just secured a
divorce from his wife, also a full-blood.” And a
few years ago the blackfellow was his own divorce
judicature, and his trusty waddy would have been
his “ decree absolute.”
THE SWEET BACK-COUNTRY TOWNSHIPS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh ! the sweet Australian townships, lying, scented on
the plains
Green and verdant. Rivers foaming, brimming with the
plenteous rains 1
Oh ! the sweet back-country townships, quivering in my
reeling brain,
lixed and rooted there for ever, festering in the endless
train.
Milparinka, Tibooburra, Oobar, Louth, and Gunnigal,
\ antabulla, Purnamoota, Gol Gol, Moorna, Booligal,
bus ton, Ivanhoe, Wanaaring, Parlamutty, Pooncarie,
Pitarpunga, Goombalia, Barringun and Mootherie.
Goats and jam-tins, flies and cursings, heat like Hell, and
dirt and sand—
Sand in waving, quivering ridges, stretching out on either
hand.
Crows in dozens perched around you, croaking at a dying
lamb. '
Arid, God-forsaken country, all summed up in one bitr
D— n. 6
(Please excuse a fresh lot of townships here-r-by a special
dispensation of Providence, the supply is limited—
about one to 10,000 sq. miles.)
In my dreams ye haunt me ever, and I start up in the
night.
Eyeballs glaring, lists fast clenching, life-blood freezing
with the fright.
Dreaming I am still firm planted in that brazen, blinding
glare.
May such phantoms fade for ever is my fervent, heart-felt
prayer.
Booroondarra, Willyama, Tilpa, Toorale, Buckanbe,
Girilambone, Bulla Bulla, Nyngan, Hillston, Nymagee,
Tarrawingee, Timbirebungie, Tooraweenah, Tocumwal.
May the Heavenly Powers above us send down fire and
blast you all.
N.B. w.

THE BEST NATURAL APERIENT WATER.
Hunyadi Janos
For CONSTIPATION.
Professor D. LAMBL, of Warsaw, Professor of Clinical Medicine at the University, writes —
“ Hunyadi Janos Bitter Water, besides being an excellent general aperient,
has proved specially efficacious in the treatment of chronic constipation,
venous obstruction and congestion, haemorrhoids and obesity.”
Average Dose :— A wineglassful before breakfast, either pure or diluted with a
similar quantity of hot or cold water.
CAUTION.
Note the name “Hunyadi Janos,” the signature of the Proprietor,
ANDREAS SAXLEHNER, and the Medallion, on the Red Centre Par’
of the Label.
THE NEW CABINET.
It is safe to say that public opinion is divider!
as to the merits of the Cabinet just completed by
John See to the order of N.S.W. We ourselves
think that, though it is by no means perfect, it
might be much worse. We must admit, however,
that we have seen much better Cabinets in Sydney
lately, viz., at Sada’s Sale of Eastern Novelties
now on at 197 Pitt-street. Pretty Cabinets,
handsome Whatnots, Bamboo Suites, Fancy
Screens, Carpet Squares, and about thirteen
hundred and twenty-seven pretty and quaint
Oriental Ornaments are here marked at figures
that must command a ready sale. Therefore, we
say, ’Urry up ! P. O. Sada and Co.’s Showrooms
are just opposite the Imperial Arcade, in Pitt-
street.*

A LIKELY TALE .
WOULD-BK SELLER: “Shure it’s a ehplindid mare she it, intirel y!”

“ T.G.F." : Be tame ’roos (“Sydney
Side,” B. 23/3/1). At Nanonia Station was a
pet kangaroo that was very fond of his cup of tea.
This was given him on the floor, as otherwise he
would have been responsible for a large crockery-
bill. When he found the dogs asleep he
would hop up to them, playfully smack one on
the nose and bound off. The dogs would follow
full speed, and “Paddy” would be pace-maker
for a quarter of an hour or so. He went out one
morning, however, and made the pace for some
strange dogs ; but they were “playing for keeps,”
and when they collared Paddy they slew him.
* » * *
“R. Mac”: Talk about miracles! Two
men were well-sinking recently on the Downs
(Q.); and, one day, when they were down 100 ft.,
the bucket was being sent up, when in some unac-
countable manner one of the iron drills (3ft. long
and 2in. thick) became exactly balanced on the
small hook attached to the end of the rope, and
was thus drawn safely up the 100 ft. Imagine the
consternation of the man at the windlass when he
discovered what he was hauling up !
* * * *
“ Wallaroo ” : Two Mew England (N.S. W.)
border-farmers, weary of the
drought and lured by the pros-
pect of 235. per bag, hired wag-
gons, loaded-up with. unpaid-for
corn, and started over the worst
road in Australia for the Sun-
baked west. Then it rained ; and
when, after mishaps innumerable,
they finally attained their goal,
the grass was everywhere so
luxuriant that they couldn’t sell.
Left the grain in storage and
hurried home to plough their land
with horses so reduced by the trip
as to be practicallv useless. The
cocky-carrier and his team are a
sight to remember. He invari-
ably moves in wet weather and
persistently overloads. Gets stuck.
Other men come and together they
attack the overtaxed animals with spreaders,
saplings and “language.” Have known them
not make 12 miles in 3 days ! The Govt,
road men are the most consistently cruel to
horses. A party of them, lately camped near
Inverell, had to cover their animals with
bags to hide great raw holes into which a man
could thrust his fist—the result of repeated
thrashings with shovel and spreader. Though
the brutality of these men was several times
reported, the police stolidly refrained from
interference. Saw one batter-victim turned
out to starve with raw fly-infested patches
everywhere, and a great evil-smelling hole
over the root of the tail —a sight I will never
forget 1
“D.H.R.” : I know a man who was once
minding a flock of ewes and green lambs, when a
black boy ran up and said that a big snake had
taken a lamb and crawled up a dead stump with
it. Took an axe, felled the stump, and found
therein a carpet snake 12ft. 3in. long, and a small
lamb, dead. The stump was 14ft. high and 2ft. in
diameter.
• * * *
“Go West, young man ” ; and Br’er
Rabbit goes. Full-grown, he has been caught
now at Kanowna. But the lonely digger, fed up
on tinned-dog, instead of dreading the invasion,
thinks seriously of petitioning for a close season
to give nature a chance.
* * * *
The Afghan moves on—he has become a
teamster now in Westralia, and takes to the horse
far better than the average bushman does to the
camel.
* 9 * *
In spite of “the break-up of the drought,”
as the fall of patch)’ thunderstorms is always
called in the city paper, the Darling from Bourke
down is abormally low—a mere gutter in many
places.
The prayer of the N.S.W. oyster-bed
lessees : “ Give us, 0 Minister! a datum-post and a
licensed surveyor.”
An incident suggestive of hideous possi-
bilities occurred during the journey of a special
train near Benalla (Vic.) on April 9. The fireman
went mad and attacked the driver, who stopped
the train the better to defend himself. The
lunatic —one of the oldest and most efficient em-
ployes in the service—was secured with difficulty.
The Gainsborough Duchess (whose picture,
stolen long ago, has just been recovered in
America) was one of the two intimate friends,
Georgiana and Bessie, both of whom had the luck
of wedding the same Duke of Devonshire.
Georgiana was the heroine of the stolen picture, but
Bessie Was yet handsomer. Once she spoke en-
thusiastically to Gibbon about his history. There-
upon he flopped down and proposed. Historians
are poor game, but a Duke is snapped like a bird.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the Well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the iargest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.

LAND SOPRANO.
Cabled that an ambushed Boer, nea
Petersburg, “ got the drop ” on a party of six Tas-
manians—killing two lieutenants with successive
shots. He was himself afterwards killed.
The Norddeutscher-Lloyd S.S. Co. con-
tinues to build ocean-monsters. The 15,000-ton
Kronprinz Wilhelm, now well-advanced, is to be
followed by a 19,500-tonner, 707 ft. long, and of
38,000 h.p. The White Star Line, however, still
holds, with its 20,800-tonner, the size-record.
From N.Z. Times :
The yacht Ariadne, ashore at the mouth of the Waitaki,
would have fared no worse had she stranded within reach
of the natives of New Guinea. The hundreds of sight-
seers who have visited the vessel have rifled her from
stem to stern, and helped themselves to “souvenirs” of
their visit. Many portable and attractive articles, from
brackets to binnacle lamps, have been openly carried off
by these freebooters. The owner, on arrival at the scene
of the wreck, will be painfully apprised that he is in a
free country.
Martin Place, Sydney, is to be finished off
at last. The Bank of Australasia, having bought
the big oorner which the Equitable would have
grabbed had Yankee cuteness been only half what
it is cracked up to be, is about to erect thereon a
palatial building, designed by the same architect
(Mr. Edward E. Baht) who designed the Equit-
able buildings in both Sydney and Melb. The
pile is to be 87ft. high, 20 less than the Equitable,
which will tower up behind it like a great central
keep. The style is to be Italian renaissance ;
material, rock-faced trachyte, with polished
columns on the upper floors. The Bank
will occupy the ground floor and the
basements, and the other three storeys
will be let for offices. The windows are
to be a special feature, but the massive entrance
from -George-street is to be sublime. Great
trachyte steps, flanked with two huge columns of
Swedish granite 21ft. high and 3ft. in diameter,
hewn out of the solid rock. From turret to foun-
dation the building is to be fire-proof, and the
most modern and comfortable of elevators will
whisk passengers from floor to floor.
Editor Bulletin. —Some members of the
N.S.W. force here in China, have been boasting
to us Brtish Tommies about drawing 7s. 6d. per
day pay. as against our poor Is. 6d. Yet these
same men have also been bragging about how they
robbed the store in which the collected loot is
kept to be sold, later on, for the benefit of all the
British forces that took part in the relief and
defence of Pekin and Tientsin. Thus, these
gentry were not above robbing the men whom
they contemptuously call “common soldiers.”
Pekin, 26/2/1. 12 Bat, R.F.A.

In America, four States legislated against
Trading Stamps, or, as they are more generally
known here—coupons—but the Supreme Court
ruled that such Legislation was unconstitutional.
An Act was passed in Illinois, making it illegal
to use the National flag in advertising. This Act
was also declared unconstitutional. The Supreme-
Courts of United States carefully guard against
interference with the liberty of the subject.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
R. E. O’Connor and John Norton were
the only N.S.W. Senate candidates who polled
over 1000 in each Federal electorate.
Several defeated Senatorial candidates
intimate that their crowds, being the less intelli'
gent, gave most of the 38,000 odd informal votes.
It may be so, but there is acid in the consolation.
The Federal election returns offered a
good many insults to the personal character of
Australian Liberalism in permitting a number of
notorious “rotter” candidates to save their
deposits.
Out-back in N.S.W., where a letter or two
is neither here nor there, it is plain that in the
ballot for senators Socialist Moiling polled scores
of votes that were intended for Millen—who, with
Donald Macdonell, was about the best-known
wayback man on the paper,
1 The cost of subjugating my country will
stagger humanity,” said Kruger. The.cable tells
that the Boer war has already cost one hundred
and forty-nine millions sterling. Before it is
done, it will probably cost £200,000,000, not to
mention the price in blood.
The Lyne-Fegan stench is another proof
that it is better for a Cabinet to be cursed with a
clever rogue than a blundering fool. The former
at least has the sense to run straight when it pays
to do so ; while the latter is certain to do the
wrong thing at the worst possible time.
Lieiit-Col. Byron, late of Queensland, now
on York’s staff as an extra A.D.C., writes to
British Imperial Review advocating the imme-
diate raising of Australian military and naval
corps to an offensive footing. The Boer racket
makes some people think they own the earth.
If Kooyong (Vic.) election had been a
duel between Knox and Rogers, writes a Bulle-
tin correspondent, the latter would have attained
the majority that Knox won by, owing to his
being a “Holy Roman,” but in the fear of Fink
the R.C. vote was ordered solid for Knox, thus
throwing out Rogers. “Mysterious are thy ways
O Lord ! ”
The fact that Thomas Grlassey found a
place in the array of Queensland Senators was
due to the peculiar pickle in which Q. Democrats
found themselves. If they did not put in Glassey
they knew perfectly well they would, by neglect-
ing him, put in Cowley or Thynne or Annear, or
some other of the alien-coddling gang. Conse-
quently they gave the old shuffler another chance.
IN THE FEDERAL ARENA.
THE ARAD: "'Ere yer are, sir; the Twister.
Full account of Fegan’s appointment.”
THE REID: “ What, so soon? Hooray! I knew
that fool Lyne would make the ‘ bloomer ’ of
the Cabinet. He’ll be my Federal dad yet.”
Sydney and suburban population has in-
creased to 488,968 —a gain of 102,109, or 26‘39 per
cent, in ten years! Sydney is a great non-pro-
ducing institution, steadily sucking the life-blood
which should go to nourish the producing indus-
tries in the country; and, once the stream of
borrowed money stops, the country will find it out.
Federal elections left 11 vacancies in S.A.
Parliament, and already about 30 candidates are
out for the seats. Seven vacancies are in the
Assembly, and the remainder in the Council. So
far a great majority of candidates are Tories, and
the Labor Party has therefore a better chance of
capturing a majority of the seats for Democratic
nominees—who, unfortunately, are a very poor lot
just now.
A political prophet, in Drake’s Progress,
forecasted Bananaland’s senatorial election with
much accuracy. The prophet tipped Daw-
son, Drake, Higgs, Ferguson, Glassev and
I hynne, adding a footnote to the effect that the
Ja,st might be rejected owing to his black-labour
advocacy. The six prophesied -(with the excep-
tion of I hynne, who was replaced by Stewart),
won easily.
The principle of rating on the unimproved
value of land is making rapid headway in Maori
land. By the present law, a certain number of
the ratepayers of any rating district may demand
a poll on the question ; and if a majority votes for
the system it is put into operation. Already
several counties have decided in its favor. The
first few boroughs in which it was proposed
rejected the idea, but latterly several have
adopted it,
Lithgow (N.S.W.) Sandford, the plucky
candidate whom Joe Cook knocked out in the
late contest for the House of RepS, is' under'
written agreement with his workmen (iron-
workers) to advance their wages from the date
the Commonwealth applies a protective duty to
their products ; and it is stated in the agreement
that the advance is to equal the percentage of the
duty imposed.
Immediately C. A. Lee, M.L.A., for Ten-
terfield, had been elected Leader of N.S.W.
Opposition, it was found that he had publicly
declared against any reduction of the number of
Members in the State Legislature. Which was
awkward, in that both the “Great Dailies” had
already insisted on this reform. However, the
new Leader politely withdrew all his previous
utterances on this subject, and the Party became
united again.
S.A. Education Minister Batchelor was
Leader of the Labor Party when he joined
Holder’s Ministry, and now resigns a billet of
£lOOO a year to go into Federal politics. Batchelor
is one of the few S.A. politicians who make a
financial sacrifice in entering the Federal Legis-
lature. In all probability Tom Price, the present
Labor Leader, will succeed him by joining Jen-
kins’ Cabinet.
The S. Aus. National Ass was cocksure
that its deity R. Chaffey Baker would top the
poll for the Senate ; and it was a bitter pill when
Symon, whom the Ass had thrown over, landed
easily on top. But now they are explaining that
H. Chaffey Baker, the big man’s little kinsman,
a candidate for the other House, got lots of votes
that were intended for R. Chaffey B.—in fact,
H. Chaffey B. himself magnanimously made that
explanation when the poll was declared.
“ Where the carcase is,” &c. Two Labor
candidates for the State Parliament, in addition
to Cann, M.P., were wanted at Broken Hill; and
15 men were nominated—only one of whom is a
working miner. His chance from the start was
Buckley’s ! For the rest there were the Labor-
party’s salaried Mayor, and the Labor-party’s
municipal stink-inspector and his assistant, and
the ditto’s ex Mayor, and the man who does odd
jobs about the Labor-party’s offices, and a salaried
Labor secretary or two, and the man who runs
the ditto’s local weekly paper, and the man who
canvasses for advts. for it, and several barnacles
who do still less.
There is no
disguising the
fact that the
men who have
first grasped the
handle of the
C ommon weal t li
windlass are al-
most all Impe-
rialist in word
and deed. Says
Internal-Minis-
ter Lyne:
“ This new na
tioti is riot only
willing, but
eager to help to
bear the heavy
burden of em-
pire.” Says Ex-
ternal-Minister
Barton : “ The
firm of John
Bull Liability
Limited is now
John Bull and
Family, Liabi-
lity Unlimited ”
—which is some-
thing to chew over. And so on, with most of the
rest. This does not necessarily prove that the
Commonweath has been called into existence to
give effect to the desires of Jingoes or Imperial-
ists—who have simply rushed the handle, just as
bounders rush victuals at social functions. Memo :
We’ve seen a handle lay a man stiff when the
windlass got away from him.
Mournful wail of Brisbane Courier :
“The deplorable fact has to be admitted that
Queensland’s representatives in the Common-
wealth will, by a majority, support the hostile
attitude of the ignorant southerner.” That is to
say—the well-informed Queenslander will back up
the ignorant foreigner. When the well-informed
and the ignorant agree upon a matter surely the
verdict can’t be very far wrong.
John Haynes, Archer, and J. C. L. Fitz-
patrick, M’s.L.A., all three of whom were sup-
porters of the Lyne administration last session,
were present at the election of C. A. Lee as leader
of N.S.W. Opposition. As they naturally will
be members of his following, this defection will
count a loss of six votes to Premier See on a divi-
sion. Added to which the new member for the
ORTHODOX ABSURDITIES.
TRANSMONGRIFIED F ATMAN: “ Shop! Laryest size
winys and halo, please.”
Hume, G. N. M'Laurin, is an alleged Opposi-
tionist.
Editor Bulletin. —Re site for Federal
capital: one important matter has not received
sufficient consideration, I refer to the water supply
for a great city. Orange with its altitude is com-
pletely out of it; in fact, Bombala is the only lofty
place where a pressure for fountains with an un-
limited supply can be obtained at reasonable cost.
The whole of the upper waters of the finest stream
in the Commonwealth, the Snowy, are there avail-
able, and the high watershed, I presume, still
belongs to the State. —B.
That lovely North Australian League has
actually got as far as interviewing the Melb.
Russian Consul with a request that the White
Tsar should be urged to subsidise a line of steamers
from Port Arthur (where Chow labor is wonder-
fully cheap) to Port Darwin. A list of the
“qualifications” of these syndicators shows they
have already mopped up a large portion of this
much-exploited country. Bradshaw holds 5,000,000
acres, Gum: a whole large island, the N. Aus.
Syndicate 2,000,000 acres, the Western Roper Syn-
dicate 2,000,000 acres, and so forth. If the Federal
Govt., as an administrative act, doesn’t take over
the N.T. very promptly there will soon be nothing
but the blacks and the boundary lines to take.
Most N.S.W. country members owe their
seats (this is a direct consequence of the absence
of a Local Govt. Act) to the amount of work they
put in, not only in the Govt, departments, but
actual favors done in the city for their constitu-
ents. As for big jobs, there are scattered over
N.S.W., gaols and other buildings procured by
log-rolling M.’sL. A., for which there is no use
whatever. Cooma gaol cost £40,000 originally,
and has never been asked to contain a single
prisoner ! A hospital was erected three years ago
in the north of the State, with Govt, assistance.
With sick people all round, it has not yet been
occupied by a solitary patient. But the member
got the money “ circulated in the district,” which
was “one to him.” No wonder the country mem-
bers write to Sydney Telegraph protesting
against upsetting the system by which they politi-
cally live. - -
Though widely reported lately that J. H.
Carruthers had refused the leadership of N.S.W.
Opposition, he was finally a candidate for the job
and bitterly resents the election of C. A. Lee,
M.L.A. for Tenterfield, over his head. The
alleged reason for passing him over was that he had
listened to the voice of the charmer in the person
of Premier See in the latter’s efforts to form a
Coalition Govt. But as the cabal against him was
prior to the negotiations objected to, it is under-
stood that the reason for his rejection was not
that which is alleged. One thing is evident. If
the remnant of the Great Freetrade Party now
in the Assembly desire to form a Govt, after
the approaching general election, it would, on
the face of things, have a much better chance of
success under Carruthers than under Lee. If
the whole of the engineering since the demise
of the Lyne Cabinet were made public, it
would furnish edifying reading. It seems a far-
fetched idea to cast off a man because he had
refused to cross the floor of. the House when
offered the bait of office. Surely such a refusal
mast indicate the loyalty of the individual con-
cerned. But, as stated, the “ unanimous ” choice
of C. A. Lee was arrived at on account of reasons
very different to the ones reported to the press at
the end of the meeting.
Bananaland is a neat little country.
Just at present a considerable section of
the Federal election results is unsettled,
simply because Bedourie (near S.A. border)
results are unattainable. Bedourie was
visited some weeks ago by a flood that
broke up approaches and destroyed the
telegraph line, and it is calculated that
about eight weeks must elapse before
tidings can be obtained from that charming
village. Consequently the fate of six sena-
tors and one representative (Maranoa)
hangs in the balance. Bedourie, by the
way, polled 12 votes last State election.
“ The Old Cove ” to The Bulletin :
“ Remarkable allegations ” in connection with
the N.S.W. land administration should surprise
no one. They are but the inevitable sequence of
our later legislation, which has substituted govern-
ment by administration for government by-
statute. The wide powers of discrimination—
without appeal —now wielded by the Lands Minis-
ter and his nominees have rendered suspicions in-
evitable, and have stifled that free expression of
opinion and fact, by the governed, which is our
only safeguard for just administration. No man
interested in land matters knows when this dis-
crimination may have personal application, and
from prudential motives “lies low.” Whatever
disadvantages attach to hard and fast administra-
tion bv statute, in land affairs, are more than com-
pensated by the manifest advantages of fixed
statute law ; and the sooner the wings of the
administrators are clipped by Parliament the
better for the independence of the community,
and the honesty, morality, and efficiency of the
Lands Department.
The Queensland Commonwealth
elections have settled permanently the
chances of one-man-one-vote in that State
so long as the gang in possession can help
it. The Federal figures show that at least
15 seats in the State Parliament would go
to the Democrats on an equal-value vote.
Signatures to requisitions have not
been particularly expensive in Melbourne,
of late. Seven-and-six per 100 is now the ruling
rate.
With the multiplicity of elections looming
ahead, the State legislatures should give early
attention to the S.A. enactment that all elections
shall be held on Saturdays—in the country at
least. Men working in the bush and on the roads
almost invariably resort to their nearest township
for Saturday evenings and Sundays. The recent
Federal election was held in several States on a
Friday. Either these men must lose a day and
a-half. or ride back miles on Friday night for the
half-day’s work. If elections were held on
Saturday such voters would lose only the half-
day, and have Sunday for recovery after “signi-
fying the same in the usual manner.” Also, why
should civil servants, already well favored with
holidays, and mostly residing next door to the
polling-booth, have a full day at public expense ?
S.A. Federal returns, on completion, show
that all over the State just 40 per cent, of those
on the rolls voted. Nowhere did half of those
entitled to the vote use it (though one country
district came within a fraction). The Northern
Territory polled only 30 per cent. This is ex-
pected, owing to distance and difficulty of travel,
but it is strange to find East Adelaide, which
comprises half of the city itself, coming next to
lowest. The informal vote all over the State (which
voted as one district) was only two per cent.
But then S.A. has the sensible “make-a-cross-
opposite-your-name ” system, which requires &
thick-head to go wrong, instead of the “strike-
out-all-the-other-men ” plan, which needs an
almost-genius to go right when there are 44 to be
struck.
Dan O’Connor’s apology for the Fega.,
appointment is more than a defence of Fegan— it
is a beatification of D. O’Connor.
Just on 12 years ago I had the honor to accompany
Lord and Lady Jersey on a northern tour. We spent
three days in Newcastle, during which time we visited all
the public buildings and all their great operative indus-
tries—principally connected with coal-mining. Among
those great industries was a large smelting works. I
shall never forget, on entering the place, the intensity of
the all-prevalent heat, and much I marvelled at the
physical endurance that could live and seen) to grow
healthier under such tropical conditions. Yet, beside the
very mouth of the furnace stood a sturdy, square-
built man, in the bloom of health and youth— Mr.
J. L. Fegan, to wit. An interesting conversation
took place between her ladyship, the Countess of Jersey,
and this gentleman, Mr. J. L. Fegan, in singlet and mole-
skin breeches, relative to social conditions. After her
ladyship had shaken hands with Mr. Fegan and thanked
him for his courtesy and information, she remarked to
me, “That is a man of no ordinary intelligence, and I
would expect to see him at no distant date occupying a
seat for a working man’s constituency.” And thus things
happened. Her ladyship was a prophetess, for two years,
afterwards Sir. Fegan was elected for the electorate of
Witfkham, and this seat he has triumphantly held for a
decade of years, till he resigned to fulfil the high position
which he now occupies.
[But he doesn’t yet occupy it—and may never do so. J
“ The objections to Mr. Fegan’s appointment,”
proceeds Daniel, “which have emanated from
Mr. Ashton, appear to me to proceed from Potts-
Point and Darling Point. Can it be that so pro-
fessedly a thorough-paced democrat as Mr.
Ashton will, for one moment, take exception to
the appointment of a fellow democrat?” Now,
Dan O’Connor professes to be a democrat, and
suppose Lyne had appointed him as Under-Secre-
tary for Home affairs ! Would it have been a
crime to object ?

NEWCASTLE.
Chamberlain, who is suffering from some-
malady or other, the other day prepared to visit a
German watering place, He had to be officially
warned not to insist in his determination, as the
Govt, would not be responsible for preserving
him safely from the infuriated mob. “Judas” is
a name to swear at, on the continent just now.
W. R. Boothby, S.A.’s chief refcurning-
officer, has conducted every election in S.A. his-
tory. He worked the first election under
responsible government 47 years ago. Only one
of the candidates, Samuel Davenport, is now
alive. Boothby has also outlived the staff which
worked under him on that occasion, save only one
—Ebenezer Ward.
Read what VITADATIO is Doing.
A Woi)d«rf6l CAre.
VITADATIO
SCORES AGA.IKT.
One Year and Ten Months
a Sufferer.
BEAD THIS TESTIMONIAL.
Adelaide, July 12, 1900.
Mr. S. A. Palmer.
Dear Sir,—lt is with great satisfaction I give you this
testimony to the curative powers of VITADATIO. I have
had a long and complicated illness, which kept me on my
back for one year and ten months. My illness commenced
with what the doctor pronounced to be Gastric Catarrh,
then the result of a severe crushing I had
received from a horse I had previously been shoeing
developed itself, and my condition was as follows:—I
suffered severe internal pains. My stomach would not
retain any food I swallowed, but would throw it up again.
A clot of blood formed in one of the veins in my leg, and,
gradually working its way upwards, finally settled in the
region of my right groin. My body and legs now became
terribly swollen, my right thigh hard and knotted, and I
became quite helpless and unable to move. Medical skill
and attention did not seem able to relieve my condition,
so a friend advised me to try VITADATIO. Soon after
taking it a quantity of dark, offensive matter was brought
away'from me, and I felt that VITADATIO was going to
do me good, so continued taking it, the swelling of my
body and legs began gradually to go down. I then
doubled the quantity of each dose, and the improvement
was more rapid, and I was able to get about on crutches,
then with two sticks. Now I am able to attend to
business matters, and feel really splendid. Many who
saw me during my illness never expected to see me re-
cover, and I think after my case VITADATIO will cure
almost anything. I feel the pleasure of being restored to
health very keenly, and trust my experience with VITA-
DATIO will be the means of other sufferers trying it and
being cured like myself.—l remain, yours truly,
B FREDERICK TOWNSEND,
Blacksmith, Lower Mitcham.
Witness : Joseph Tyler, Lower Mitcham, Adelaide.
12 July, 1900.
For further particulars,
S- A. PALMER,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.
