At a London fire King Edward offered
a reporter a cigar. The scribe put it in his
pocket. H.R.H.: “ Don’t you smoke ? ’ ‘‘ Yes ;
but it’s not every day I get one of your R.H. s
oigars.’’ Whereupon lie got another.
There are still 400 U.S. military pensioners
living in Ireland, the bulk, of course, being men
who fought in the Civil War. Their scale of pen-
sion is infinitely better than that paid by Britain.
One man who lost a leg, for instance, gets £!• a
month, and every three years a grant of £lO to buy
a new artificial one. And why not ?
Writes “ Puyyaka” to The Bulletin
In reply to “ Medi-Cuss ” (8., 23/3/’01) : The injection of
cocaine and other anaesthetics into the sciatic nerve is,
and has been for some years, a well-known mode of treat-
ment for sciatica. Whitta and Gowers use a large injec-
tion-the full contents of an ordinary hypodermic syringe
—containing a moderate dose of either morphia or
cocaine, according to circumstances, and distributed in
four or five places along the nerve. Billroth, the German
surgeon, lias used large-volume injections of osmic acid
in similar fashion witn excellent results. The action is
threefold, embodying in one operation the good effects
of acupuncture, of local-pressure ansesthesia, and of local
and some general drug ansesthesia. Sciatica being due
to inflammation of the nervi nervorum —tensory filaments
distributed over the surface of the great sciatic nerve,
which itself is mainly muscle-governing in its functions—
and not to interior disturbance of the main trunk,
stretching of the whole nerve is probably the
most efficacious treatment in the long run, but the
writer has seen excellent effects from intra-neural injec-
tions. Distilled water will often give as good results as
an anaesthetic solution, thereby proving that the puncture
and intra-neural pressure are important factors in the
treatment. This scribe has seen few cases of sciatica
which have resisted a course of treatment beginning with
thorough blistering along the nerve-track, going on if
necessary to intra-neural injection, and if the trouble
persists terminating by a surgical operation where the
sciatic nerve is exposed and thoroughly stretched for ten
minutes or so. Any return after this probably means
some central mischief, such as tumour-pressure. The
sciatic nerve can also be stretched without cutting down
on it, by certain movements of the leg effected by the
surgeon, the object in both cases being to tear down
adhesions of the nervi nervorum aforesaid.

The Bulletin knew it would come ! Dr.
Ross, M.L.A. for Molong in N.S.W. Assembly,
writes to Sydney E. News proposing that the
Jook of York, out of his boundless ignorance of
Australian geography, shall select the site of the
Federal capital :
Now that the Duke and Duchess of York are shortly to
arrive on the shores of Australia (where, no doubt, a
hearty and loyal welcome awaits them), to open the
Commonwealth Parliament of Australia in Melbourne, it
would not only be a noble, but even a graceful and
deservedly dignified, befitting, and becoming act on
the part of our authorities and the Commonwealth
to invite and leave the settlement of this important
matter in the hands of the Duke and Duchess of York (to
be known as the “ City of Clarence ”or York)—an act and
public function that would tend more to cement the
Empire and to commemorate their visit to these colonies
for all time to come, one of tenfold more general and en-
during importance.
And yet a day or two later the same Andrew
Ross, M.D. wrote a letter to the press girding at
titles !
Patents for Inventions —Arthur Griffith,
International Patent Agent and Consulting
Engineer, University Chambers, Phillip-street,
Sydney. Patents, Trade Marks, Copyrights,
obtained throughout the world.
Westralia sends to the Senate one of its
orators in “Ned” Harney, whose flow of language
even Dan O’Connor might envy. He won the
gold-medal for oratory at Trinity College, Dublin,
and hasn’t much brogue. Kirwan (editor of Kal-
goorlie Miner), representative for Kalgoorlie, is
a dull and humorless speaker, but he is a good
democrat, and has done a lot for the digger—
especially the alluvial man ; and he certainly did
more than anyone else to bring Westralia
into the Federal States. Ewing, of the State
Assembly (brother of Tom Ewing, of N.S.W.),
is a pedantic but able debater and close reasoner.
His brother is one of the N.S.W. members
of the House of Representatives. He married
Chief Justice Stone’s daughter, and has ambi-
tions. Alex. Matheson has improved greatly in
his speaking, but his high Scotch birth makes
some people chary of him as a Democrat, though it
was under that designation he won a Senate seat.
To his credit be it said, he has voted solidly for
all democratic measures in Groper Land’s Upper
House. Solomon, member for Fremantle, is a
decent Hebrew, and that is all. He has lived
there from the Flood, been its Mayor innumerable
times, and knows all the electors by their Chris-
tian names. He is a poor politician, and as to the
others none of them are likely to emerge from the
ruck.
Somebody writes in M.A.P. that he used,
30 years ago, to meet in a London pub. billiard-
room a mild middle-aged person whom everybody
called “champion,” and whom he erroneously
supposed to be the local John Roberts, until he
discovered that the man couldn’t play for sour
apples, and that he was, in fact, no less a personage
than Mr. Dymoke, Hereditary Champion of
England. The present holder of the “office” is
a young farmer; but, in the face of innumerable
stalwart policemen, it was not necessary for him
to battle for King Ned’s diadem.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
G. G. Stead has a couple of fliers in Cruciform
and Neuschikoff, who notched Ist and 2nd money in Can-
terbury (M.L.) Champagne Stakes and Challenge Stakes.
Stead has had wonderful success in these two classic
races, having won the “Champagne” nine years in suc-
cession, and 12 times in all, and the “ Challenge ” six
times since it was established nine years ago.
• * • *
Boreas, who accompanied Advance to Melb.,
just missed Auckland R.C. Century Stakes (1000 sovs.) on
his returning to M.L. Battleaxe snatched the Purse on
the post, paying £lB 15s in a field of six. Big gun Sea-
horse hadn’t been up long enough to last a strong mile
and a half. As sound as a bell, old Nelson’s son should
be nice and mellow by next Cup time.
* * » *
After years of battling M.L. odds-and-tote-
betting fraternity has triumphed over Canterbury
Jockey Club. Latter forcibly ejected the undesirable
parties, and proceeded against them on summons for
trespass, but defendants escaped on ground that they had
“a color of right” to visit the property. Then the club
proceeded civiily against one Patterson and got a small
damages verdict; whereupon, bookie appealed. Point is,
that the racecourse is a reserve vested in trustees, who
leased it to the 0.J.C., ordaining that charge could be
made for admission, &c. The club drew up a regulation
prohibiting bookies, but, as the trustees in their deed
didn’t bar anyone, the Full Court, by a majority, upheld
the appeal with costs of two courts.
• * * *
The most humorous decision of recent M.L.
Trades and Labor conference was its objection (based on
a desire to ensure greater attendances at the T. and L.
sports) to the granting by Govt, of tote-permits for
races on Labor Day. To be consistent, the Col. Sec.

Sydney owner-trainer, “ Teddy ” Keys, secured the well-
bred Positano—Recline colt for M.L. sport Mr. J. B.
Reid, cheap at 60gs.
• • * •
Recently-dead M.L. crack colt, Nobility, was
by St. Leger—Lady Emily, and belonged to Mr. E. J.
Watt, who got him as a yearling for 125 gs. Nobility
gathered in Egmont Cup, Normanby Stakes, and Wan-
ganui Cup, Said that running him last Spring while
suffering from severe influenza was the primary cause of
his death.

Handicapper Henrys, who acts for a host of
M.L. clubs, big and small, is one of the best-paid racing-
officials in Australasia, his income aggregating £l4OO a-
year.
* * * »
Teddy Hodgkins, killed at Adelaide, was best
known as John Crozier’s jockey, though as a lad he
rode for W. A. Long’s stable in N.S.W., and also in
Wimmera district. Deceased was admittedly the best
horseman in the Wheat State. Among many races won
by him was Adelaide Cup (in ’9O) on Shootover, Onkapar-
inga Cup (’9B) on, Gnullo, S.A.J.C. Derby (’99) on Gunga
Din, A.R.C. City Handicap (’99) on Security, S.A.J.C.
Goodwood Handicap (’93) on Britisher, V.A.T.C. St. Kilda
Cup (’99) on Security, and V.A.T.C. South Yarra Handi-
cap on Bloodstone. “ Teddy ” was up on Dick Hammer-
hand, who ran fourth in last Melb. Cup. Hodgkins, by
the way, affected the Tod Sloan forward crouch, and
always finished in the centre of the track, thus minimis-
ing'the possibility of being blocked. His funeral was an
immense one, and was largely attended by fellow-riders
in their colors. The five chief clubs in the State have
undertaken to provide for the widow.
• * * •
Toryism and big-liead seem nigh akin. At
Rosehill and HaWkesbury racecourses, on each horse-box
is painted the name of the occupier, a piece of attention
much appreciated by racegoers. Randwick, so far, has
not followed suit, a fact much commented upon at
A.J.C. autumn meeting. But it is perhaps too much to
expect the A.J.C. pure merinos to follow the lead of the
proprietary track.

Crack lepper Skylark made new figures for
Sydney Agri. Society’s Ground last day of show. With
Mrs. Elliott up, he “ took off ” about 10ft. from the brush-
wood hurdle, cleared the water as if propelled by a cata-
pult, and landed well—3lft. 9in. from the take-off. Pre-
vious “ best” was Greygown’s 31ft. 4in., four years ago.

At recent N.S.W. Agricultural Society’s show
at least 200,000 people paid during the seven days. Gate
totalled £7282, as against £4723 for ten days last year.

* • * *
Frank Leleu, just dead in London at 73, was
one of the old school of racing-men. He was credited
with having, when younger, picked up a notoriously bank-
rupt acquaintance - in the small hours of the morning,
taken him to a coffee-house, and lent him a few shillings
to play hazard, no other opponent being available.
Result—Leleu, without possibility of winning anything
except a bad debt—lost £2OOO.

“Sport ” under English Hunt Club rules : Two-
days’ meeting at Derby in March, 14 races, six of them
selling races (any surplus on sale of winner divided equally
between owner" of second horse and promoting Co.)
Two races out of the 14 worth as much as £lOO. First
day, not a steward on the ground, second day, two
present, but left early, so that a glaring case of pulling in
the last race went without inquiry. Result, handsome
div. to the Limited Liability Co. that owns the course.
Following upon Levanter’s appropriation of
Hurst Park (Eng.) Steeple last month, owner (Major
Edwardes) secured £lO,OOO to £BOO about the old M.L.
lepper s chance in Liverpool Grand National (4J miles)
and many judges tipped him certain to fill a place ; but
he finished fourth. That the wager was justified is evi-
denced by London Referee saying “ Levanter now
fences like an Englishman ” —a big compliment, as ’Ome
sporting scribes are not over-addicted to marmalading
foreign products. The club-footed Oban (same stable)
scored at s£’s in the Brook Hurdles at Gatwick. How
Oban chucked up .his dead-sure “ Cambridgeshire ” and
downed the ultra-clever Robinson ring will not soon be
forgotten.

*.* * m
Tj l6 bruising match, Lenton v. Bob Turner,
filled Golden Gate A. C. (Sydney), Saturday, and caused
hundreds to crowd out on the footpath and into the road,
after the gates were closed, clamoring for admission.
Lenton was tipped as a “ cert.,” and heaps of money went
on him at two’s, but after the first round he wasn’t in it
for points, though he stood much hard thumping. He
turned out to be a second “ Ironbark ” Burge, for often,
when appearing to go completely, he’d come again and
show up as fresh as at the start. His backers must have
depended upon this to win their money, or at worst save
it, for the conditions insisted upon provided that
only a knock-out or one combatant withdrawing
should decide. The way articles were drawn up abso-
lutely deprived the referee of power, and he
would have been almost as much use miles away, yet the
two parties worked up a “ barney ” as to who should act,
that lasted long and sickened everyone present. The
police intervened in 16th round, whereupon the exciting
but all one-sided mill ended without a verdict. Waterloo
(Lenton) and Pyrmont (Turner) suburbs were in oppo-
sition, hence the big house and extraordinary interest.
Turner never fought better, and should have knocked his
man out had there been enough powder in his punches.
Ooach-builder bulky Tom Taylor (once Peter Jackson’s
pet pupil) had Lenton in hand, and tipped him a “ moral,”
but fighters themselves are notoriously bad judges in this
respect.
* • • •
The black man would have a big grip on Aus-
tralian bruiserdom if allowed his own way. Felix claims
the heavyweight, ‘‘Starlight” the middleweight, and
Tom Mitchell, the lightweight supremacies. Modesty
never has been a conspicuous characteristic of the son of
Ham—especially the pug son—bar Peter Jackson.
• * • *
“Jab.”: Much of world’s champion-bruiser
Jeffries’success is due to the fact that his methods are
more defensive than aggressive, though he’s not at all
averse to cutting loose occasionally. lie mostly lets the
other man do the rushing and the swinging, and has
found these tactics highly profitable. It was freckle-
fisted Fitz who did all the fighting in their match, until
he got one on the “ point. Sharkey operated in like
fashion till successive heavy counters doubled him up.
Against Oorbett the huge boilermaker had to act differ-
ently, because of C.’s extreme mobility ; besides, Oor-
bett is a typical defensive pug. who has consistently
fenced slugging and mix-ups, and is eternally jigging
out of harm’s way. In the art of blocking and dodging
punches the Beau Brummel of the Yankee ring has
no equal. ’Tis positively asserted that all through his
career nobody has brought blood from his nose. Sharkey,
Fitzsimmons, Maher and 00. are not so careful of that
organ, for they charge-in on a knocking-out mission
utterly regardless of wiiat might come to them. Ruhlin
jf a . c * oss between the offensive and defensive fighter.
“Kid”,M‘Coy, if his prospects seem good, always takes
chances, but, opposed to a dangerous man, he invariably
adopts a “ ware-hawk ” attitude. The most aggressive
pug in the ring to-day is ’Murkan Terry M'Govern, who
isn’t stopped by a punch or two when he starts his
cyclone act. Black George Dixon, when in his prime,
alwa3's set the pace. Most of those at the game to-day
hate to have their faces marked, but there are still some
who look for “ cauliflower” ears, broken noses, and facial
scars generally.
• • m *
One-time possible Australian middleweight
premier pug., Jem Ryan, got a parlous time from Yankee
bruiser Hart at Louisville (Ky.), Feb. 28. Ryan, in
desperate straits, fouled glaringly and often, but ulti-
mately went out in the eighth round, consequent upon a
right hook on the “point.” Jem was a good man when
the best at the game Australia ever saw were here, but
couldn’t be relied upon. He and George Dawson used to
put up the prettiest “ spars ” seen at the famous old York-
street Club of a dozen years ago. Big Ned Ryan, who
fought Joe Goddard, Steve O’Donnell, and others, and
died at Auburn (Sydney) two or three years ago, was a
brother of Jem’s.
• * * *
A quiet little fight with mittens went wrong in
Melbourne recently, and there is a disagreement as to the
disposal of the stakes. One expert amateur undertook to
knockout another amateur, who is not particularly expert,
in the third round for a £lO note or some similar trophy.
Elaborate arrangements were made, and the parties met.
The inexpert amateur was knocked clean out in Act 1,
Scene 1, and now lays vehement claim to the spoil, de-
claring that the document stated quite specifically that
he was to be “knocked out in the third round.” Judg-
ment deferred pending legal advice.
* * * •
Sydney amateur bikists Kershaw and Turnbull
failed to score at M.L. League of Wheelmen’s Champion-
ship meeting. Kershaw didn’t ride on opening day,
owing to a cropper when training. Turnbull jumped
into second place with a vigorous final kick in mile cham-
pionship, won by W. Reynolds (Auckland), but made no
showing at the finish in the three-mile dart. On second
day, he won his heat in the half-mile, but was passed-out
for interference, and the race was given to mate Kershaw,
who came to ground in the final, won by Reynolds.
Latter, in great form, also notched the five-mile go, the
Sydney pair being outed.

Two miles wheel premiership of M.L., with
attendant £lOO Enfield Challenge Cup, was secured by
Geo. Sutherland t’other day.
* * * *
G. Sutherland (who left subsequently for Melb.)
didn’t have things all his own way in the cash champion-
ships at M.L. wheel gathering. The five and one-mile
events fell to his little lot, but he went under in the half-
mile and three miles. The surprise of the meeting was
Ralston’s victory in last-named event. While others were
almost stationary at the start, waiting for each other to
go ahead, he shot off, got a 30.vds. lead, yelled to the
pacer to put on steam, went ahead, and won by nearly
a quarter of a mile. The outwitted “push ” tumbled to
the game when too late, and the crowd keenly relished
Ralston’s generalship.

form to prosper in Australia.

“ Inquirer ” (Ohura) : Write to S. Kirby,

♦ * * •
Canterbury (M.L.) Rugby Union has adopted
an insurance scheme for the benefit of members of
affiliated clubs who get flattened out. Union provides
doctor, and pays the victim £1 a week up to 10 weeks.
Schoolboys receive same amount as players in receipt of
big salaries. Senior clubs participate on payment of £3,
the unions for £2, and half-holiday clubs for £l.
* * * *
“Eller”: Re that recent cricket decision.
There is nothing in the rules of cricket to meet the case.
In inter-State matches such a difficulty is guarded against
by changing the ball every 200 runs (or before if showing
signs of wear). I consider the ball should have been
taken as “ dead.” ’Twas not a ball that was caught, but
a piece ; and, as the batsman usually gets the benefit of
the doubt, ’twould favor him in this instance.
* * » *
Top-notcher schoolboy swimmer Claude Cor-
bett finished up the season Saturday at N.S.W. Public
Schools A.A. A. Championships with a big flourish. He
secured 100yds. all schools’ supremacy (paddling 80yds.
of the way) ; and, with nearest opposing lad 4sec. ahead
of him, took up the going in last lap (50yds.), and won
200yds. all schools’ “ Flying Squadron ” Team premier-
ship for his school—Cleveland-st. Young Corbett (still
under 16) has put up a record this season, winning over
£5O worth of prizes, besides two Commonwealth gold
medals in Jan. last for the 100yds. and Half-mile School-
boy Championships of Australasia. He only needs to
winter well, and he’ll be with Read, Cavill, Lane and
Co. next year. As it is he could surely win the absolute
championships of Q., Vic., S.A., or M.L.

Interest in coming inter-State 8-oar race on the
Parramatta (May 4) increases. Vic. reps., whose stan-
dard is very high, have every confidence in their ability
to win. The N.S.W. men have unusual power and
physique. They average 11.8, and five of them stand
6ft. high.
• • • *
Hunter River (N.S.W.) is the only Australian
locale where prof, sculling flourishes. Matches are
frequently contested, talked-of, or pending. Latest is
between Chapman and Campbell, for June 1. Stake, £5O
a-side, and Raymond Terrace publicans have agreed to
subscribe something towards training exs., &c. Chap-
man concedes ssec., and a hot tussle threatens. Camp-
bell is 38, and among the oldest at the game on the river.
Twenty years back he gave promise of getting atop of the
heap, but, unfortunately, never aimed at anything
beyond regattas.
« * * *
Late ’Ome news is that Hunter River sculler
Geo. Towns is doing all possible to lure Canadian Gaudaur
into a match, and offers to row on Toronto Bay, Canada
(Jake’s own back-yard), but the wily Canuck is silent.
Failing connection this time the Australian says he’ll
close on the world’s supremacy himself. Others would
have done so long ago.
* * * *
Individual bowling supremacy of N.S.W. went
to “Jim” Hogue, M.P (City), on Balmain green (Sydney)
Friday last week, when he accounted for Capt. Kondio
(Waverley) by 21 to 16. Hogue has scored several suc-
cesses this season, but neither he nor Kondio evidenced
championship form in the final of the big thing. Blue
funk or something happened both. Kondio’s last failed
to reach within 6ft. of the “jack,” and left Hogue lying
the four necessary to win. It was much the softest
championship writer ever saw won. Kondio was runner
up last year, too, but shaped much better then.
m * * *
Betting at N.S.W. Gun Club shoots during
Easter week was reminiscent of its palmy days years ago,
and Mo. benefited accordingly. Odds totalling close to
hundreds were often laid in one hand. One punter is said
to have had £3OO to £l2O in one shot —and he landed the
bet. Sixties to twenties were common. Heigho 1 The
writer could point out two men, still surviving (and that’s
about all) who dropped the best part of their lost fortunes,
aggregating, perhaps, £150,000, in Sydney Gun Club
grounds.
In 1860, King Edward, as Prince of Wales,
had a day’s shooting between Chicago and St.
Louis. The Duke of Newcastle was with the
party. They arrived at a farmhouse pretty tired,
and the farmer came out, an unmistakeable
English yeoman. Says he, “ The Prince is wel-
come ; but as for you, Newcastle, I used to be one
of your tenants, and have sworn you shall never
enter my house.” The shooters passed on.
“ A Professional Criminal ” to The Bul-
letin :
You once said you’d buy copy from a lunatic, a
murderer, or Satan, if it were worth it. Buy some from
me now, and help me to turn an “ honest ” penny. Your
category of commentators is cosmopolitan. Why shouldn’t
one of us have a “ say”? I write truth.
There is a strong fascination in crime which few writers
understand, though Charles Reade did. We follow crime
as you follow your vocation. Ours is another world than
yours. That world is gaol—its environments and associa-
tions —its blood and its tears. We play a losing game
and know it. We gain to-day and lose for years, and we
walk into gaol as unconcernedly and mechanically as we
walk out. We are fulfilling our destinies. Most of us are
fatalists and grim ones. Our arguments: Excite-
ment and romance in preference to some dull
round of monotonous toil. Life is a chess-board.
Why restrict one’s-self to one set of squares—black or
white. Nothing like variety ! Excitement, romance,
danger, and a decent living, all going begging together
for the sake of a little pluck and the discarding of a little
sentiment. We can’t all be moralists ; we can’t all go
Boer-hunting.
The term “ criminal ” requires defining. Some of your
“ experts” opine that we are not born but made. Right
and wrong. Many of us are born and many made. The
born-criminal has the criminal head. There is no mis-
taking it. Take half-a-hundred of our regular gaol-birds
and put them in a row, or walk into a big prison’s
chapel, and there are the heads—to say nought of the
faces —before jmu. Born criminals these.
Each of us has his own particular line in crime, and
invariably clings to it—a fact which greatly assists the
detectives. We have the forger (the “checker”), the
burglar (the “ buster ”), the confidence man (the “ mags-
man ”), the pickpocket (the “ hook ”), the general spieler
and the sneak-thief. The checker despises the burglar,
the burglar the pickpocket, the pickpocket the sneak-
thief, and the magsman despises the lot of them. He
scorns a man who has to use his hands instead of his
brains and his tongue to get money.
Criminals are mostly lacking in anything like manli-
ness, and 9-10ths of them are ill-informed brutes, though
some, in time, become <?<sM>?-edueated.
Speaking professionally, there are few genuine success-
ful criminals ( artists in their work) in Australia. We
can’t in this respect approach the States, or France, which
two countries produce the world’s leading criminals. Of
educated “ crooks ”we haven’t sin 1000. The successful
“artist” has to be of good address, honest and intelli-
gent-looking, and well-informed. In fact, the majority of
criminals are not criminals—only “ scrouchers,” apologies,
blights, and eyesores.
Honor among thieves? Yes; but it’s limited. Our
slang is crude and uninteresting. A sample: “ Dear
Dago,—The sheeney has chatted me to smoke to the old
plotch with the splosh. I jerry to the bluff and duck.
My mouthpiece pulled the box for Paroo, and he got a
tray bit. Thought he was dead for a lin. Jo-Jo rapped,
and biff it must be when the talent rocker.—Yours,
Rajah.” Translated: “Dear Dago,—The Jew advises me
to go to the old place with the money. I understand his
dodge, and act accordingly. My lawyer talked the
Judge over for Paroo, and got him three years. Thought
he was sure to get five. Jo-Jo went hard against us, and
as soon as the boys know it he will get a hiding.”

In another column appears an advt. calling
attention to a book entitled “The Secret History
of the Coningham Case.” If the “History” is
half as sensational as the advt. it will be a startler.
The subdivisions of the book include such headings
as “ The Waddy Fiasco,” “A Game of Euchre,”
“The Romeo of Glenmore Road,” (the most un-
romatic ’bus-route in the eastern suburbs), “A
Belated Wedding,” “Coningham’s Body-guard” ;
“Dill Macky’s Revolver”; the “Story of the
Necklet”; and the “Relief of Burrilda” “The
trail of Zero” “and Zero No 2”, are mysterious
and quakeful.*

the Toorakites at the races. The usual black

‘ ‘ Cecilie Lorraine, fair, fat, and five-and-twenty,
looked on at the emotional Russell cuddling oalvi
in ‘II Trovatore ’ with deep interest. Someone
during the interval asked her what she thought
of the active Ella. ‘ Great,’ replied Cecilie.^
‘ There is much to learn from her performance,
she added, and went on to enumerate some of the
most telling points of her impassioned Leonore.
No professional jealousy here.
“Louis Arens’ love-making, which was the
deadliest and woodenest thing on record, annoyed
all the women with whom he acted. In .Lonen-
grin 5 one particular star used to have to remind,
him of his tin-soldier clasps, and crossly demand
of him to kiss her hand —‘or else he'd have left it
out,’ she dramatically adds.
“It is awkward when the call-boy appears and
demands the presence of the star before she is
quite dressed. Last week, in ‘Faust, Agnes
Jansen, as Siebel, came on to the stage in her
boy’s dress without her cloak, and without her
tunic being fastened properly up the back. While
she did her bit, Gilbert King, as Wagner fastened
her clothes up the back, and at the first chance
she slipped into the wings and reappeared pro-
perly cloaked. But something always goes wrong
m ‘ Faust ’. It is a tradition of the French stage
that, when the Devil is played on the boards, the
real old Nick watches the show critically from the
big house rolled up to sample Ibsen’s ‘Lady
Inger, of Ostrat,’ on the first night (April 20), but
never again, I guess. When last-trainers left at
11.30, the thing was still going strong—m fact,
seemed to have taken out a new lease of life. Not
a bad play, I thought—the plot rather Shake-
spearian, the dialogue terribly twaddlish and in
want of pruning. Besides, Shakespeare woulun t
have reformed his villain at the last minute
it’s unnatural. Kingston livened up things
whenever he came on. Nance couldn t
get into her part, but one can t blame
her. Lady Inger, after raising great expectations
as an enigmatical heroine, turns out to be merely
a person with a Past. Lady Macbeth is coupled
by some people with Lady Inger, but thank good-
ness her author didn’t drag in a squalid, secret
baby as the motive of her ambition.
“ At that Lottie Collins matinee (funds handed
to current shipwreck funds), Mdme. Slap, m
blue blouse, black skirt and gold ear-studs, gave a
hand. Salvi, with pink roses in his coat, followed
suit, and then came Charles _ Tilbury with his
spectacles and queer arm-actions. In operatic
get-up one doesn’t notice these flourishes quite so
much —they only look as if the gymnast never
ceased trying to clasp his arms tightly round a
huge, slippery ball. But it seems such a pathetic
waste of squeeze when a singer, in ordinary street-
wear, and nothing in his hand but a programme,
constantly stimulates empty embraces.
“Lottie herself got lots of flowers, and she
deserved them, because of her generosity (shared
by Harry Rickards) to the shipwrecked. But,
nowadays, the Collins looks and sounds over-
mature for her girlish song-sentiments and kick-
ups.
“The death of Lieut. Creswell Walter, of the
Tas. Imperial Contingent, who was ‘ sniped ’ by
a Boer, has caused sincere regret in Melbourne.
The deceased was a great practical joker. ( Being
possessed of a particularly nice skin, he ‘ made-
up ’ very well as a girl, and on one occasion walked
the Block, hatted, veiled, and decently skirted.
He was introduced, by a conspiring sister, to a—
then —flirty young man about town, who straight-
way escorted the, as he thought, pretty girl to a
tea-room, and whispered sweet nothings in her
responsive ear.
“ One can’t buy a yard of tape these days with-
out its being recommended m the stereotyped
phrase, ‘ Just what the Duchess of York wears.’
As for heavier articles in the linen or mantle
department, one grows desperate at the oft re-
iterated, ‘ What the Duchess wears.’ How im-
Solitic to publish such lengthy details of the
(uchess’s dresses and mantles ! Every suburban
needle-dodger has the list pasted up on her wall with
pins and patterns for reference, and is intent on
turning every dress out in the exact manner of the
Duchess’s. W hat an awful spectacle awaits us in
a thousand editions (some very cheap and nasty)
of the said dresses in May !
“ A Melbourne family living in a lonely suburb
advertised for a housemaid. The choice was
made from the applicants, and her duties, &c.,
were laid down for her by the master of the house,
who in this family was really master. On asking
to be shown her quarters she was conducted past
the outhouses, to what proved to be an old omni-
bus ingeniously made into a bedroom. The house-
holder explained to the astonished girl that his
two sons were a bit wild, and he objected to them
staggering in at all hours of the night. Hence the
’bus for their accommodation. “But,” he went
on, “ I’ve told them that they must behave them-
selves properly from this out, and be home before
I lock up. The ’bus-room is to be kept strictly
for you ! ” And to the injured man’s indignation,
the housemaid asked when the next tram left for
town.
“Quiet stick-at-home women (wives of rural
councillors, etc.) invited to meet Royalty, resolve
for this fateful once to make a splash, and dazzle
less lucky neighbors now and for evermore by
flaunting the privileges of hubfly 8 position, 'th
lion in the path of these unsophisti-
cated marms is ‘something to wear, and it s a
treat to hear them nervously worrying poor little
dressmakers on the subject of gala rdbes. «u Dur-
ban modistes, firm on the point that all-black is
the only correct garb, are multiplying tucked,
black-silk bodices, constructed so as to close
by daylight and open in a Vee of even-
ings. With these are to go skirts, made
at home by the wearers themselves, of ,useful
alpaca or sich, and of ‘comfortable length, Ihe
description, ‘tucked all over,’ seemingly affords
wondrous satisfaction to women who, as a rule,
don’t ‘dress,’ finding it as much as they can do to
‘clothe.’ Of course, there are tucks and tucks.
All tucks are not ruses to veil poor ‘ cut and bad
‘ fit ’ Still, for the credit of Australian beauty,
let us be thankful that contracted bodice-chests
and half-mast skirts will give away their victims
less in black than in colors.
“ Women are warned to conduct their conver-
sations in the fitting-rooms of this much-timbered
city in a minor key —the secrets that have been
lately given away are as numerous and awtul as
the wives of Herod. In these fitting-rooms par-
titions half-way up only divide the compartments,
and as the conversations between customers and
the fitter, whose mouth is always filled with pins,
are mainly one-sided, familiar and abundant, the
women waiting in all the adjoining boxes have an
ear-wanning time.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.
“ Bokeen,” from Far North :
Dear Bulletin, —Rumoured up here (Port Darwin) that
the SA and “Territory” Federal elections will be held
bn 30th March. So far, however (15/3/’01), no official no-
tification has been made here ; and, as it requires at least
a month to enable the news to reach some parts of the
w e NT. electorate, many voters will know nothing
about the election till it is all over, and many more won t
even know the names of the candidates till they
get into the polling-booth. There are in _ the N. 1.
about 800 voters; many of them doing their
best against heavy odds to make and keep a
“White Australia.” Cut off from the luxuries of a
civilised life, we feel that our future, and in fact our foot-
hold here, lies in the hands of our Federal Allies. And
now we find that owing to the carelessness (or worse) of
the Govt, we are practically disenfranchised at this most
important occasion. The Minister who (mis)controls the
N T. is also Premier of South Australia, and it is probably
he who has made that characteristic blunder; but the
fact that the voters here are mostly far apart or else in
Govt, employment prevents any open show of disapproval.
Australia, however, ought to know about the little tin
god who dares to interfere with the voting power of a
whole electorate, and to see that no further chance ol
doing injury is afforded him.

A lady correspondent of S. M. Herald —
after reverently describing her “ mournful plea-
sure ” in being permitted to handle one of the late
Queen’s cast-off gowns—proceeds to strip all the
poor little twopenny glamour off the incident by
giving the size of the waist as 4Gin., and the
length—or, rather, shortness—of the skirt as
36in.
MISS NELLIE STEWART, who gives a benefit for the poor children
of Sydney at the Theatre Royal on Saturday afternoon.
Photo, by Talma, Sydney and Melbourne.
Proposed by the Irish Nationalist M’sP.
that one of the party temporarily resign his seat
to allow John Xavier Merriman to be returned by
the electorate, so that he can address the Commons
and place on record the views of the Africander
Bond, in spite of Balfour, who has refused to
allow the delegates to be heard at the Bar. If
that stubborn old Englishman, J. X. M., is re-
metallic falsetto, and when howling audiences in
Cape Town refuse to hear him John simply keeps
them at it till their larynxes give out, whereupon
his weird screech sails on above all minor inter-
ruptions for a couple of hours at a stretch. He
has a good flow of language, and a rasp-
ing bitterness, and his speech on the
Jameson Raid in the Cape House is remem-
bersome—he had the Rhodesian jingoes foaming
at the mouth before it was finished. He spoke as
an Englishman who had to live in the country and
go about with the shame of the miserable, villain-
ous enterprise in question always hanging about
him. No doubt if he is elected the House will
listen to him in contemptuous silence, for it is too
much to hope that his word will have any effect
on the oourse of British S’African policy. Ihe
man who has done his utmost to heal the breach
between Briton and Africander is regarded by his
countrymen as a renegade.
A simple-minded back-blocker visited a
Ballarat (Vic.) registrar t’other day and got
united to the girl of his heart, cordially embrac-
ing the official after the ceremony for his kindness
in so speedily securing his happiness. A few
weeks later the officer got this letter
Dear Sir, —I am sorry to inform you the woman I
married turned out no use to me. I found out she was
not married, only living with a Chinaman before. She is
also a drunkard. In fact, I left her a few weeks ago. If
you will be so kind as to strike the marriage off the regis-
ter, as I will be clearing from her. Yours, . Pb-
She said she wants it struck off, so send me an answer as
soon as you can, as I want to be free from her sort.
“ Living with a Chinaman.” How The Bulletin
does smile !
Mrs. Charlotte La Mont, who lately died
near Melbourne, was probably one of the first
children born in Victoria. Her mother, wife of
Mr. John Manton, was the second woman who
rode across from Sydney to Poit Phillip district.

At Poverty Point.
Showman exhibiting cinematographic screen. Man in
the audience : “ Yah I why that war occurred before the
cinematograph was invented ! ” Showman : “ True, my
man, but nevertheless the pictures are perfectly accurate.
The representations I now submit are based upon slides
obtained per medium of the Rontgen rays operating upon
the medulla oblongata of a soldier who went all through
the most stirring fights of the campaign, and was de-
corated with the Victoria Cross by the fair hands of his
gracious Sovereign.”
The late W. S. Lyster and Armes Beaumont, when in
London in ’7B looking for musical compositions to pro-
duce in Australia, saw the first performance of “ H.M.S.
Pinafore.” .Both agreed, without hesitation, that it was
not worth touching!
The manager of the Waxworks Show sees a good deal of
human nature at times. The boss of a show now on
view in Sydney narrates how, on taking the wax present-
ment of a certain executed murderess to her native
town, he received letters from shoals of excited relatives
and friends asking : et Would he be so kind as to take the
figure of off his show during his stay and spare the
feelings, &c.” The manager did so, after which he
received about 30 secret visits from said relatives
and friends, each beseeching him as a special favor
to let him or her have a private view of poor at re-
duced rates ! On another occasion the widow of a gentle-
man who had decreased the population till the law in-
terrupted him was rather proud of seeing her deceased
spouse in the show, but insisted on dressing him in a
clean shirt and collar every week that he might look his
best. After a while, however, the inevitable occurred.
The widow annexed a flesh-and-blood husband and the
toilette of the wax predecessor was thenceforward neg-
lected.
“ H.” : Cannot help contrasting the cold, classic style
of the tributes to Queen Victoria in the English Parlia-
ment with the fashion in which the Italian Chambers,
both Senate and Assembly, received the news of the
passing of Verdi. The speeches were numerous, lengthy,
vivid, and passionate, interrupted by frequent bravos.
However, this was hot turkey instead of cold ham, and
Verdi’s not Victoria, nor was Victoria Verdi.
The late Edwin Kelly bore some resemblance to the
Duke of Wellington. The genial old gentleman was fond
of relating that, when walking as a four-year-old in Hyde
Park, the Iron Duke once stopped him, and, patting him
on the head, said to his mother, “ What a fine little
fellow 1 ”
“ What Happened to Jones” having been a great suc-
cess, an American writer promptly follows it up with
“ What Happened to Johnson.” Probably the series will
fo on till the public is weary, or till someone brings the
usiness to a natural end by writing “ What Happened
to Everybody."
The recently-dead G.O.M. of Music, Verdi, was, as a
youth, refused admission to Milan Conservatoriuin, on
the ground that “ he did not display a vestige of musical
talent! ” Verdi, besides being great as a composer, was
an excellent conductor and stage-manager, but he was
not suavitor in modo. At the final rehearsals of “ Aida,”
prior to its first production in Italy (the opera was first
presented in Cairo) the maestro “ performed ” so
vigorously that the orchestra sent him a “ round-robin,”
threatening to strike if he did not moderate
his expressions. The principals fared no better.
Verdi advised Waldmann, the contralto, to go
and take in washing, and told Fancelli
that he could carve a better tenor out of a log of wood.
But the “ first night ” was a great success, and then Verdi
wanted to hug everybody. Verdi’s only fiasco was “Un
Regno di Giorno ” (The King of a Day), an attempt at
comic opera—not repeated till, in his 80th year, he scored
an enormous success with “Falstaff.” Verdi began bybeing
parsimonious, but developed into great generosity. His
name used as a patriotic cry during the war of
Italian independence—had an esoteric meaning—“ Viva
V.E.R.D.1.,” standing for “ Viva Vittorio Emmanuele, Re
d’ltalia 1 ”

The recent Australian production of “ Lohengrin ’’ re,
calls a Parisian story about the opera. A patient-
recovered from typhoid, found himself stone-deaf ; but
his doctor said he’d soon cure him of that, and next
evening he took his patient to hear “ Lohengrin.” They
sat the performance out in silence, but, getting outside
the theatre, the patient, overcome with joy, exclaimed
to the doctor that he had regained his hearing. But the
doctor took no notice, and the patient again cried out
that he was cured. But the doctor heard him not—for
he was now stone-deaf 1
Consider the mystic silliness of the “ Lohengrin ” plot.
A nice girl named Elsa is made miserable by the mys-
terious disappearance of her little brother, said relative
having been converted into a swan by a wicked aunt.
Elsa is in a fair way to be hanged or poisoned for murder-
iug the lost brother, when she is saved by the interven-
tion of the spook-like Lohengrin, who comes along in a
skiff drawn by a swan. Elsa loves Lohengrin and he
loves Elsa. All the same he uses her metamorphosed
brother as a skiff-puller. Furthermore, ere marrying the
girl, he exacts her solemn promise that she will
never want to know who he is, or where he came
from, which is about the cruellest condition that
any fleshly spook could impose on his bride-elect.
And because Elsa breaks her promise by asking Lohen-
grin to explain himself, the gallant knight breaks her
heart by returning to his bachelor guardianship of the
Holy Grail. But before making himself scarce he changes
his blessed swan back into the person of Elsa’s brother, a
simple act of justice that would have made the
happy if he had done it at the outset without introducing
love and marriage questions into the opera. Under the
circumstances, Elsa is sorry to see her brother again.
She would have preferred to keep her nice new husband,
and let the swan swim.

“ Aloes ” to The Bulletin :—
A couple of recent eases where persons have shown
signs of life after being pronounced dead hy medical men
have evoked much comment upon the professional know-
ledge of the medicoes in question. “ Can’t be much of a
«?u 0 V f he doesn’t know when a man’s dead,” is the gist
of the lay argument, amplified more or less according to
the personal itj' of the speaker. As a matter of fact, this
very question of a simple and rapid method for diagnosing
the cessation of life was for many years a vexed question
in medico-legal circles, and was only settled a few vears
back by the awarding of a Paris bequest for the most con-
vincing essay on the subject. The method adjudged best
consisted in ligaturing a finger, when, if any life
remained, swelling would occur from the checking of the
capillary flow. If dead, the finger does not swell. Another
test is the application of heat or irritants to the skin
which will produce redness or blistering if life is not ex-
tinct. The absolute cessation of respiration or heart-
action is hard to positively ascertain, and the alteration
1 ™ a ’I b6 i de J? yed - The popular idea that
death can he instantly diagnosed by the “ feel ” is alto-
gether unreliable, and the stage method of dropping the
corpse s hand while telling the limelight man that The
‘"'f u P er . fc Dfmbad has gone to meet its Maker” is
not at all advisable in private practice. Writer knows of
several cases of faulty diagnosis of the kind, in one of
which the supposed corpse is now a well-known
business-man in S Africa. Several stories are extant of
0 - 8howed . si «' n< < of life on the post-mortem
thii i there ,-£ k P artlcuIarl -V ghastly French varn with
this as motif), but present scribe has never heard of a
The chances are altogether against the
coffin-strugg es of fiction, as the closeness of the coffin
would rapidly induce asphyxia, and the after-supper
stories of dreadful shrieks heard at midnight from newly-
!^ci!w aV h S m0 *\ t . Iy ,iterar .V' “ gu.vver.” To prevent
accidents, however, those at all nermus on the subject
often leave directions that their bodies shall be kept
whfo q h U,V ° Ca 6viden f es of death a PPear. A curious
fact, which may be new to some, is the “death-smell ” in
those about to die It may appear several days before
the end, and is not evident to all, but once recognised
its meaning and significance are very clear. It is pro-
” ab . - v due to commencing putrefaction in the more
easily decomposed tissues, and is especially marked in
those suffering from rapidly-advancing diseases of the
abdominal cavity, or from septicaemia.

After all, the proper policy for the Com
monwealth is simple enough. The country cries out
for a population of clean-living white men of ele-
vated thought and ideals, who are themselves not
too often elevated. Sobriety is to a great degree
determined by the brand upon the bottle. The
D. O.Lj. brand is a guarantee of pure, good whisky,
a liquor conducive to clean living rather than
antagonistic to it. Men don’t “go wrong” on
D.O.L. It is the Whisky of the Wise and no
respectable man scowls at it.*
Bunny has a poor time of it now—quoted
m the London market as flat at7Jd., he is bundled
out of existence in Victoria wit if the assistance of
M Lean Bros, and Bigg, Ltd., the big iron-
mongers in Melbourne. That firm supplies guns,
ammunition and traps, and, in cases of dire need,
can turn on a full blast of destruction with Dixon
Goulds Vacuum Destroyer. This machine is the
■Jim Dandy of its kind, is beautifully made,
certain in operation as a Waterbury watch, and
counts its victims by thousands. Poor bunny !*
Mr. Oscar Gr. Roberts, of Fairfax and
Roberts, Sydney, delivered recently, at the Bal-
mam Town Hall, a lecture which was “all my
eye in a very different sense from that usually
associated with the slang use of that expression.
Ihe discourse was, in fact, a practical exposition
by a man who understands his subject, of the
nature, causes, and proper treatment of sight-
failure.

The Secret History
* . . OF THE . ,
QONINCHAM
—— CASE.
DO’HMIII'S IMCITIIII
AND HOW IT WAS BROUGHT ABOUT.
The Cause Celebre of the
New Century.
The Church Militant and
the Church Triumphant.
IT may be safely said that the people of Austra-
lia—Catholic and Protestant, Jew and Gentile
—have seldom, if ever, before taken so much
interest in any trial as they did in the Coning-
ham Case. And it may be added that a verdict
never gave greater satisfaction to all decent citi-
zens, irrespective of creed, throughout the Com-
monwealth than that which, at the end of the
Second Trial, sent Dr. O’Haran out of the Courts
of Law with a stainless reputation, and a soul
made stronger by the suspense and suffering of
many weary months. The true story of this
example of modern martyrdom should be read by
not only every Catholic but by every lover of
Justice in the land. It will be found to be full of
surprising incidents, of plots and counterplots,
curious episodes and cunning intrigues.
The Secret History of the Coningham
Case
Will contain all these, and will be illustrated with
PHOTOGRAPHIC FACSIMILES of the Docu-
ments quoted in the Case, and many others that
did not appear in Court, and have never before
been published. The sole right to publish these
rests with the Pbopbietob of this Wobk.

Alice Barnett, just dead in London, is affectionately re-
membered by lovers of Gilbert and Sullivan in Australia.
Gilbert circulated much of his peculiar humor about the
substantial Alice, and her parts lose much piquancy as
played now by actresses who are only fair to medium in the
material sense. The massive lady in Patience and Bear
h lt , tl ®.“ utt , ercu P» these were parts made for Alice Barnett,
but Gilbert overlooked the fact that Alice Barnett cannot
be made for the parts, and so points are lost. Writer first
saw Ahce as Buttercup, 24 or 25 years ago, when a local
critic praised her performance as “ a clever bit of female
impersonation. Also, once saw the big lady in Dorothy
SrlT 1 ' spiriting yarn by playfully gathering
little Billy Elton up in her apron like a pet kitten. 8
Kingston is to play Macbeth when Nance O’Neil puts
the play up in Melbourne, so Melbourne will get the anti-
thesis of the Sydney Macbeth—extremes in meat.
The exceeding heaviness of all the big singers who have
been introduced to Australia of late suggests curious re-
flections on the fatty degeneration of the art.

grove, who does not get anything like the credit she de-

duct She appears as the shepherd in the second scene of
and Sln f» B a lovely little lay in a sweet, rich voice
that has more natural charm than can be found in one or
two other rather important outcries that probably cost
20 times as much. J
Donald Macdonald touched Colombo on March 27th
and started (D.V.) a series of three lectures at the Public
Hall on April Ist. Mac’s advent was heralded bv lone-
newspaper notices of his success as a showman. Also the
local press reprinted on the morning of his arrival a
eulogistic criticism of his Ladysmith book by London
Spectator. Then Ceylon Observer gave the “ famous
Australian an interview, in which he stated that he had
boiled his original three lectures down to two, and would
supplement these with a new yarn—“ Australians : in the
Bush and at War’’—which would be tried on a Colombo
audience before he decided to let it go in London This
as a matter of fact, is likely to be Mac’s “ trade-mark ’’’
lecture.
Agent Geach wrote from Colombo “I am beginning-
to think that Macdonald was born under the proverbially
lucky star; for in addition to the impending arrival of
‘ the Dook,’ which is fairly booming the town we have
■truck the Gloster Regiment here. The Glosters fell-in
at Nicholson s Nek, and were sent to Pretoria, so it goes
without saying that I have them warmly interested in
Mac. and Ladysmith .. Success is assured. It is very nice
to take a 5000-mile trip to a comparatively strange land
and, on the morning of arrival, find your ‘ star ’ big-typed
in the papers as you buy them on the steamer. That’s
business.”
Recently-deceased journalist R. P. Whitworth who
possessed a-great fund of stage anecdote, had himself
been on.the “boards” with G. V. Brooke. Whitworth
pleased Chas. Kean so much with his notices, in Sydney
Empire of ’63, of K.’s performances, that the tragedian
presented him with a diamond ring. “ Bob ” had in his
time done some steeplechase riding, and never entirely re-
covered the effects of a fractured skull thus sustained.
As a dramatic^ author he made a fairly good hit in Mel-
bourne with a piece called “ Oatchinga Conspirator ” in
which Barry Sullivan was splendidly caricatured by
Harwood. The composition was a cento of extracts from
those of Shakespeare’s plays in which the tragedian had
appeared. Whitworth also wrote for Madame Celeste a
successful drama, founded on the execution of Max-
imilian, in Mexico, and the subsequent madness of his
wife. As a writer of short stories, Whitworth had con-
siderable success, and, in conjunction with J. J
Shillinglaw, he supplied the solid part of Marcus Clarke’s
school “History of Australia,” a work which the late Angus
Mackay, then Vic. Minister of Education, entrusted to
Clarke on the ground that “ it ought to be done by a
man who was good at fiction.”
Wizard Oil Weston is back in Melb., younger than ever,
his silk hat and patent-leathers even more shiny than of
yore.
If the Federal Parliament would pass a law that every-
body who enters a theatre after the curtain is up should
be fined a penny at the door, said copper to go towards
building the transcontinental railway, one of two things
would happen. Either this bright young country woufd
have a chance to know what the first act is about, or else
it would soon have its transcontinental railway.
“ Melb.” : Williamson’s Biograph having shown the
“Tell Me, Pretty Maiden,” from our “ Florodora,” the
rival Biograph (Wyld and Freedman’s) shows same scene
from London “Florodora,” at the Lyric. The ladies are
lymphatic, not so fruity as ours. Nor is the business as
good, and it does not look anything like so lively. The
ladies play too much at the audience with their nods.
Maybe the film was at a crude stage of the piece. In
Australia we get the benefit of London’s 300 nights.
Bland Holt’s revival of “The World” reminds that the
late Aug. Harris played the hero in the first Drury
Lane production of that massive melodrama. Many
people who remember the portraits of Gus Harris,
the portly, bald-headed alderman and operatic im-
presario, have forgotten—if they ever knew the fact—
that he one time posed as an actor-manager, as well as
part-author of his Drury Lane productions. ’Gustus
wasn’t a finished actor, but he had a good-enough appear-
ance and style in his young, slim days, and he played his
own heroes rather than pay £25 a week, or more, to
another fellow whose superior qualifications would have
made no difference to the takings at the door.
Olga Nethersole, the clingsome kisser, was, at latest
dates, preparing to undergo a “ dangerous operation ” of
some sort or other. Olga, nowadays, is the constant
subject of interesting puff-pars, and The Bulletin reckons
fw hoverb , etween life and death for at least a
“^ thafc m ® ans four alarming reports in the Yankee
weeklies-ere she recovers from the operation in question.

the only Svengali in Europe who could really play the
withJuHusKnfght^ 8 Ga3fc ° n Mer ValC ’ Wh ° WaS ° Ut here
r,n N fh^v n fi er f S A Cie i ty ,. mumme rs are often inaudible
T °" their first Australian appearance, writes a Bul-
S P Tfv, po^ dent , from London. At London
west End theatres almost everybody books, every-
.2 Ulet -’ and well-behaved. The floors
are ell-carpeted, there is no music to take the curtain
upy and the players’ low, drawing-room tones are per-
fectly audible There is very little applause—an occa-
sional laugh at an epigram breaks the respectful silenee
ot a church, and the actor has every chance to hypnotise
himself into his part.
Here is why theatricals were stopped at Windsor
Lsstle. Jimmy Rogers appeared there, before his cele-
? lt y at the Strand. It was a professional grievance that
the Queen only gave the performers the exact amount of
their regular nightly pay. Next morning Jimmy ap-
peared in the Bow-st. Court, and said, “ Your Worship,
a donation for the poor box. I have much pleasure in
handing over the sum of thirteen shillings and fourpence,
the joint contribution of Her Gracious Majesty Queen
victoria and James Rogers, comedian.”
King Ned has been eminently sensible in patronising
Theatres. He went 67 times in the year 1900. Although
the National Anthemis played for all the Australian acting-
Lrovernors, and the audience rises, the Prince of Wales
wouldn t have any of this humbug. He always paid for
his seats, at the regular tariff. He would not allow the
raising of the curtain to be delayed for him. His practice
was to remain till the end of the play, unless there
was no private exit, and then he would leave a few
minutes before the finish.
The only chance of De Rougemont, the unblushing,
who rouges at naught, was to follow the lead of the
Davenport Brothers. They began as spiritualists, but when
young Henry Irving, the actor, and Fred. Maccabe ex-
posed their manifestations, they admitted the trick, and
only claimed to be smart deceivers.
One of the New York journals, says a Sydney paper,
compares Hilda Spong with Ada Cavendish, “ whom, in
■ point of dash, vivacity, and executive energy Miss Spong
resembles.” Any old Bulletin readers who may remem-
ber Cavendish firstly as a burlesque actress, and later, as
the original Mercy Merrick in “ The New Magdalene,”
will wonder at the comparison. Dash, vivacitv, and
energy in two actresses are not points of resemblance.
The old saying that the low comedian thinks he ought
l* P a -Y* n ? Hamlet, while the tragedian feels convinced
that he is cut out for farce, receives support from the
careers of Geo. Ooppin and Mark Last King, two leading
actors who were for years members of Vic. Parliament.
In the legislative hall, Coppin the comedian was always
intensely serious, while King the tragedian (his favorite
characters were Hamlet, Shylock, and Richard III) in-
variably imparted a strong element of low comedy to his
proceedings.


AFTER TYPHOID
• -V
WEAK, WEARY AND WORN.
A Complete Resuscitation.
The cnee of
MRS. E. S. GLENN.
(Reported for The Journal.)
foi consequence of hearing a few remarks
fcnent the indisposition of a lady residing at
78 Cleveland-street, Sydney, our repre-
sentative called at that address and ex-
pressed a desire to her husband, who
answered the door, to be granted the favor
of an interview with Mrs. Glenn on the
subject. “By all means,” replied Mr. Glenn.
“Just step inside, and I’m sure ray wife
will be glad to tell you all about it.” The
invitation was accepted, and the erstwhile
patient related her story in the following
manner•
£ “ Some time ago—-T«j«wwit two ana three
years, it must be—l became conscious of the
fact that an illness of some kind was
creeping on me, almost imperceptibly. It
Was quite clear to me that I was becoming
Unwell, because of a most unpleasant sense of
goneral lassitude, a feeling of lack of interest
and absolute indifference to everything that
previously absorbed ray attention, a feeling
of unusual languor and depression. I was
always ill at ease, being so restless and dull.
A sensation of drowsiness was continually
upon me, but yet my days and nights were
nearly always sleepless. What little sleep
i did manage to obtain.was not at all refresh-
ing as I would awake just as weary as
oefora The work of the day, which hitherto
had been a pleasure to me, gradually became
an aversion. Everything. X tried to do
speedily developed itself into a monotony,
and had the effect of dragging out what
little strength remained in my body. I was
altogether exhausted, and the listlessness
and apathy that had seized hold of me was
made mere unbearable by the incessant pre-
sence of dull frontal headaches. I became
veaker and weaker through a total loss of
vnpetite, aad lost flesh rapidly. At times
had a strange ringing sensation in the
irs, and would feel chilly at regular later-
als. My state of prostration was now so
complete that the mere action of raising my
head from the pillow produced a feeling of
dizziness and sickness at the stomach. At
other times I lay in a state of utter stupe-
faction, not even being able to engage
myself in thought, and would remain for
long periods gazing into vacancy. I would
arouse myself from this predicament after a
while, but only to realise more clearly the
weak condition I was in.”
You had a doctor’s attention, surely?”
enjoined the reporter.
“Oh, of course ; and my case was pro-
nounced to be one of typhoid fever. Ido
not know whether such a thing is customary
in typhoid fever cases, but I had swellings
of various sizes all over my body. They
caused me no end of anxiety and pain, and I
was afraid of other complications setting in,
although my medical attendant at length
persuaded me there was no reason to be
unnecessarily alarmed. To add to the long
list of other ailments, I must not forget to
tell you that on numerous occasions I was
suddenly made aware of duU, heavy pains in
the chest, as though it wese indigestion ;
but considering that I ceutd not eat I do not
think it could have been that. Well, this
state of affairs went on for ten weeks, and
during that time I was just about as bad as
you can imagine a patient could be reduoed
to. The doctor’s medicines I drank during
my term of sickness would surprise you ;
but they evidently did not suit my ease.
They made me no stronger, and aid nob
remove any of my distressing symptoms.
At length I got tired of taking physic, and
decided to act upon the advice of a friend
who was continually advocating the ex-
cellence of Clements Tonic. This gentleman
acquaintance had himself suffered from the
same ’oomplaint as my own, and he was so
enthusiastic regarding the amount of benefit
he had derived from Clemente Tonic, and
the way it restored his lost vitality, that he
convinced me there must De something In its
preparation which would do me good.
Without any more delay I procured a bottle,
and discarded the doctors medicine alto-i
gether, being quite satisfied that the stuhj
he had ordered was not doing me an atom of
good, although his instructions were care-
fully carried out. I had been almost desti-
tute of hope for a thorough recovery from
my long and serious illness, even after the
fever had left me, until the necessary
assistance arrived in the form of Clements
Tonic; and my feelings of gratification can
be more easily imagined than described
when I found the great amount of benefit
which had accrued from the consumption of
the first bottle.”
“ What effect did it have upon you ? ” d
“The most noticeable result of my first
bottle of Clements Tonic was a marked
decrease in the swellings on my body, which
clung to me in spite of my convalescence ;
and the agonising pains in my head, which
had been the cause of so much torture and
anguish to me, soon left me altogether, and
I was then in such a happy frame of mind
that my prospects of regaining strength
soon became quite bright. I could also par-
take of the nourishing food which im
enfeebled condition so much required, ano
which for weeks had been returned un-
tasted. And after eating there was ar
absence of that dull, heavy feeling on the
stomach which I had previously experienced.
and I knew that my meals were beint
‘ " LI ‘ “
properly digested. I continued Clements
Tonic, and I seemed to reap advantage from
each dose. My cheeks began to display a
healthy colour, and each day added more
strength and vigor. By the time I had
arrived at the bottom of the second bottle
the swellings had gone down entirely, and 1
was able to go about the house with an
appreciable degree of comfort and energy.
With my daily increasing appetite came an
increase in flesh, and I could now take an
interest in my household arrangements
having sufficient vitality to do my work
with feelings of mingled interest and
pleasure. When night arrived I could look
forward to have plenty of rest, for I no
longer turned from one side to another in
the vain hope of gaining repose. No ; I could
always feel sure of a good sleep, and would
arise in the morning feeling very much
better for it.”
“You appear to have made a complete
recovery ? ”
“ Yes; lam thankful to say that
Clements Tonic has restored me to the best
of health, although when I sometimes catch
a chill I get an attack of neuralgia ; but I
always keep to the one remedy, and after
taking two or three doses of Clements Tonic
the neuralgia leaves me,”
“May I make your case known?”
“ Indeed you can, and I trust it may be the
means of alleviating the suffering of other
people.”

“ If I were asked what struck me as the

Australian loyalty is going to smash over
a matter of 3s. 4d. per day. Sydney D.T. lately
had a column-and-quarter yell from one Mark
Eaucett Story N 0.266,.266, “C.” Squadron, Patriotic
Bushmen, to the effect that the Rhodesian authori-
ties had tried to diddle the Patriotic Bushmen
out of somewhere about that amount. When the
corps left Capetown for Beira to march through a
country where there were hardly any Boers at
all, they were notified that they were to
receive the same wages as the Rhodesian Fietd
Force, to wit, 4s. 6d. per day. The Bush-
men considered that the amount was in addition
to their screw of 3s. 4d. paid out of the Patriotic
Bushmen’s Fund, subscribed by the war-crazy
Cornstalks. When the Fund ran out, the Bush-
men agitated for the Imperial Govt, or someone
else to take the matter up and keep the extra
3s. 4d. going, and N.S.W. Govt, was fool enough
to do so. But when the Rhodesian authorities
found out that the Patriotic N.S. W\ Bushman was
getting 3s. 4d. per day from his own mis-
led State they docked it out of the 4s. 6d.
they had been paying him, and continued
to pay only Is. 2d. To all protests the
Khodesian tyrants brutally reply, “You are getting
exactly the same screw the rest of the Rhodesian
toeld Force is getting, and if it’s enough for them
it s enough for you—you’re no better than any
a I Fom'-sticker.” And trooper Storey wants
w • to declare war on Rhodesia at once, unless
that 3s. 4d. per man per day is promptlv squared
U P- ' -The historian of the Decline and Fall of
the British Empire will probably conclude his
work with the sentence : “So perished one of the
most remarkable Empires the world has ever seen,
?y® r of 3s. 4d. per day in the screw of
N.S.W. Patriotic bushmen.” i

The Conting enter’s Return.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wayback is the centre of a big agricultural dis-
trict • the Farmers’ Home Hotel is the centre o
Wayback ; and William Harrison became the cen-
tre of the Farmers’ Home. He was a butcher by
Lade exceedingly voluble, with a large fund of
more or less untrustworthy stories and uncertain
information. Yet he »» revered
as being the most generous “shouter in Wayback:.
Harrison had been out of town for several days
and on his return he made straight for the
Farmers’ Home, conjuring-up as he went pleasant
thoughts of the warm welcome which awaited him.
He could picture to himself the desolate bar, the
gloomy countenances and dry throats, the furtive
g ances cast in the direction of the door every
toe there rv>s a footfall outside. What a subjsmt
fnr a picture ! A smile on every face as he entered,
| S stretched out to welcome him, the
silence broken by a babel of aomee and hmown
voice saying majestically. Well, J ,
‘VncoSusly hl’quickened hie footsteps, and
re ihp lnnse silver in his pocket an extra jingle.
But he stopped almost paralysed within 20 yards
of the hotel, for there arose such a shout of
laughter from the bar that the baker s horse,
tethered outside, jerked up its head in alarm. A
few seconds, and the spasm had died away, but,
in«t as his hand was on the door, there arose
another shout— mightier, if possible, than the first.
As he went in the laughter died away, as if
extinguished by sudden dread. The bar was full
SS to overflowing, but a space was reserved
fora man who stood in front of the counter a
man in kharki, slouch-hat and putties. And Har-
rison groaned in spirit as he recognised lorn
Baggs, the returned contingenter.
There was an awkward silence. Those present
felt that they had been detected m an act of trea-
son of base injustice, for had they not made merry
in the absence of their benefactor; their fuend,
mentor and beer-provider? Harrison s surprise was
plainly visible, and only the boldest of those pre-
sent could muster up sufficient courage to stam-
mer out a few words of greeting. All this,
however, was lost on the returned soldier, who
had been a blacksmith’s assistant before leaving
for South Africa, and as such had had no stand-
ing whatever. But his turn was now come, and
he was determined to make the most oi it.
He nodded carelessly to Harrison and said:
“What will you have, old chap? And without
waiting for an answer he turned to the nymph
behind the bar : “A glass of ale, ducky, for Mr.
Harrison.” And the astonished butcher saw that
Miss Smith, whose bearing towards himself had
always been one of severe respect, was literally
aglow with pleasure and excitement. . .
Baggs waved his hand in the air as an mdica-
tion that he was about to resume the thread of his
yQu that I’d got the blanky Boer
a pris’ner. ‘ Hurry up, old man ! saysl, for the
beggar gammoned he was lame. But he wouldn t
hurry up, so I gives ’iin a prod m the back with
my gun. Gorstruth, you should have heard 1m
y< The kharki hero expected another burst of
laughter at this point; but the response was halt-
hearted, and he looked round in
with his glass half-way to his lips. IP r «“ n
with my gun,” he repeated defiantly , hum y,
it uas a joke ! ”

By this time Harrison had collected a por-

that there was to be a duel f °r supremacy
but he had money, and Baggs, he felt
had not a sovereign to his name. So he s tePpea
to the front of the bar with an important air.
“ Drinks all round, Miss Smith ! Long-sleevers,
except Tom : a glass of whisky for him. .
He would be magnanimous and condescending ,
it was the proper game to play the on y >
fact There was a sigh of relief from twenty
throats, which the crisis of the last few minutes
had rendered parched and dry. „
“I am pleased to see you back again, lorn,
said Harrison, graciously
said, after a short pause, I saw a fuim X t^ g
while I was in Sydney. I was going down George-

How the sentence was to have been finished
will never be known. As the butcher, started
speaking Tom Baggs gave a look of
cleared his throat ominously. Then he commenced
in a voice which was at least two notes higher
than Harrison’s: .. ~ ■ -r, »
“ I was telling you about this blanky Boer, tie

the latter’s words froze on his lips.
“I didn’t tell you about the scrap we had at
Bloomingfontein. The Boers was pottm at us
all the raornin’, and we couldn t see blessed o
one of ’em ; the cowards were afraid to show them

• • • *
Baggs stopped here very impressively, fixing

“ Now you ’old yer jaw !” But a diversion was
caused’ by Jim Wilks, the baker, with whom the
war had ways been an absorbing theme but

“ Veteran ” : Somebody writes to S.M.
Herald on behalf of N.S.W. Lancers that they
“have just taken part in what is admitted to be the
record march of the war—lBo miles m 8 days.
■What i—After all we’ve heard about horses being
ridden' to death by these men, and about the
40 000 horses French’s cavalry killed in the march
to Bloemfontein —the fastest.time that as been
made is 22i miles a day for 8 days ! Why, m Lidia,
S 1804 three British cavalry regiments of Lakes
command rode 73 miles in 24 hours! Also, for
straight-out, steady marching : m May, 5/
(height of the Indian Summer) the Guide Corps
cavalry and infantry—marching from Naoshera
upon Delhi, covered 621 miles m 23 days—average
27 miles a day-and 3 hours after arrival went into
notion Also in ’5B, Brigadier Park, with a mixed
column marched 240 miles in 9 days, having on
the last day to work through a thick jungle and
fight a battle (27 miles a day).
The danger to English Royalty is now
financial. Perhaps much over 100 millions for the
Boer war; expenditure that cannot be estimated
for China; the bloated cost of the fleet going up
two millions a year; the Army to be vastly in-
creased—how are the taxpayers to meet it unless a
knife be thrust into the rich ? Combine all this
with the dangers to trade, and the candle will
burn at both ends.
“ Ex-R.E.” : Wilson, in his recent
National Review paper, entirely misses the real
point when he says that the various bodies of
British who have surrendered m 8 Africa had,
“on an average, only lost 12 J P® r , cei ? '
casualties before giving in.” It is ,
men that causes soldiers to surrender in bodies
but want of sustenance or having their retreat
cut off. The Austrians at Ulm had lost none, an
the French at Sedan very few before they sur-
rendered. There is no recorded instance of any
bodv of soldiers surrendering through loss of men.
What is the matter in S’Africa is (1) gross l -
competence of the staff (2), subdivision of the
army into numerous small, widely-scattered
bodies and (3) want of supervision and organisa-
tion. It is absolutely impossible to keep all these
little roving detachments supplied with food.
The country has been deliberately stripped of
everything that will support life, so that when
one of these small commands encounters an enemy
it has simply either to surrender or starve. In fact^
the time may not be far distant when the British
army, as a whole, will have either to abandon th
desert it has made or leave its bones there .

an old-time Melbourne journalist
There are some inaccuracies in that paragraph ( ~ / / )
ahont Alfred Telo. I was personally acquainted with
Telo, having frequently met him in company
Clarke and other well-known pressmen of his peno
Whatever may have been the faults of Telo, h
kindly-hearted fellow, and posssessed that charity
which covers a multitude of.sins” T as was evidenced in
thp case of Marcus s accident in the hunting neia,
when his skull was fractured. While Marcus was con-
fined to bed Telo sat up with him night after
night, nursing him with all the care and tende
ness of a woman—which incident recalls at the
same time the constant and kindly attentions of George
Walstab who, in order that Marcus s serial story Long
Odds ” should not be delayed or fall into arrear, wrot
nuite a couple of chapters for him, which chapters, when
the book came to be reviewed, obtained particular notice
and the highest praise—everyone believed them irom
the pen of Marcus himself. Alfred Telo wa ®^° n ‘
angel, in Russia. His father was a wealthy m er c « a "<;
there and afterwards removed to Liverpool. In early
life Telo was an with one of the British Embassies
on the Continent: later on, he became a lieutenant in a
Prussian regiment of Guards, from which he retired owing
to having had one of his thighs badly broken inafal.
He was dead-lame all his life after, and h g
was quite a marked feature in his personality. . Of
Hebrew extraction possibly; but cveri at .
neither Spanish by birth nor descent-according to the
story of your correspondent it would seem as though
in a spirit of the purest fun, put his head out of the
window “ to oblige” Mr. Windsor and take the risk of a
“ loyalist ” missile or two which Mr. Windsor declined.
Telo was a highly-educated man and an excellent lin-
guist, and tokU story well, despite a certa,n n h ® s 1 1 ( tat ‘°.?
in his speech—altogether, a man who made a pleasant
first impression. I fancy I can see him now coming up
the street, with his jerky gait, his hatthrown
that always pleasant and quizzical smie- winch
were full and large and handsomely fringed, were nne
enough for a Jew’s, but they were grey The Preposterous
beard never existed in my time. It was just a, %rly
laro-e neatly-kept black beard, that went well with his
salfow complexion. The enormous nose is pure imagina-
tion.

ommon purpose in going down the coast to secure
the additional comforts procurable in comparative
civilisation during a trying time, and upon a cer-
tain interesting occasion.”
“Yes my dear,” said Mrs. Uriah Lennox, who
was making the voyage for the second time, to
Mrs. Michael Thorn, for whom the trip and its
purpose was new, “I’m sure it’s quite lucky for us
to ave a doctor on board, and he looks such a nice
one too, don’t you think.”
Mrs. Henry Higgins, none of whose family of

lar concern m Mrs. Higgins’ family affairs, she’d
always managed very well without a meddling
doctor. Personally, she would have just as soon
stopped at riie mine, only her old man was better

But Mr. Mack, knowing nothing of the
character attributed to him, only regretted that
there wasn’t a girl on board with whom he could
pass the time pleasantly ; and thought he would
have a drink. So he descended to the saloon and
secured a drmk, and the steward availed himself
of the opportunity to collect Dr. Dixon’s ticket.
Mr. Mack was momentarily puzzled, but remem-
bered in time that his ticket bore the name of Dr.
Dixon, and accordingly handed it over. The
steward deliberately read it, re-folded it and
smiled.
Ibis ain t exactly an invalid ship, doctor,” he
remarked, the smile spreading itself, “ but maybe
you 11 come in useful this voyage with three of ’em
on board. ’
Then it suddenly dawned upon Mr. Mack that
he would have to be careful. He climbed up on
to the hurricane deck, three strides long and one
wide, and whistled.
“Well, I’ll go !” he slowly muttered. “What
k-TT ~T~, 1 W !V, lt Ido ? It would be
a bit of a lark ! ”
He saw the possibilities before him with three
days at sea. For the present one thing only
seemed desirable—he would have another drink,
broing down, he met the captain, a short, red-brick
sort of man who affably shook hands with the
doctor, and the doctor lamely suggested whisky.
Phe skipper agreed; but Mr. Mack, who had
thought of explaining things, couldn’t remember
what the penalty was for travelling on another
man s ticket, and weakly kept silence. When
the whisky had gone its allotted path the skipper
casually remarked: “The wimmen are dashed
glad to have a doctor on board. If we’d only
thought of it we might have charged ’em more for
medical attendance. ’ He laughed, and Mr. Mack
laughed too, but not quite the same kind of laugh.
